LIFE ON THE MISSISSIPPI

By Mark Twain

TABLE OF CONTENTS

CHAPTER I. The Mississippi’s worth your time. It’s something else. Doesn’t widen at the mouth, gets narrower. Dumps four hundred and six million tons of mud. First seen in 1542. Older than some European history. De Soto’s got the pull. Older than the Atlantic Coast. Few half-breeds join in. La Salle figures he’ll join the game.

CHAPTER II. La Salle shows up again, so does a catfish. Buffaloes too. Indian paintings on rocks. “Father of Waters” doesn’t hit the Pacific. More history and Indians. Some odd performances—not Early English. Natchez, or close enough, comes into view.

CHAPTER III. Bit of history. Early trade. Coal fleets and timber rafts. We start a voyage. I dig for info. Some music. Trouble kicks off. Big talk. The Child of Calamity. Ground and lofty tumbling. The wash-up. Business and stats. Mysterious band. Thunder and lightning. Captain speaks. Allbright cries. Mystery solved. Chaff. I’m spotted. Art proposal. I talk about myself. Freed.

CHAPTER IV. Boys’ dreams. Village scenes. Steamboat pictures. Big swell. Runaway.

CHAPTER V. A traveler. Chatty fellow. Wild-cat victim.

CHAPTER VI. Pilot siege. Taken along. Nap ruined. Fishing for a plantation. “Points” on the river. Fancy pilot-house.

CHAPTER VII. River inspectors. Cottonwoods, Plum Point. Hat-Island crossing. Touch and go. It’s a go. Lightning pilot.

CHAPTER VIII. Big gun, heavy load. Sharp sights in the dark. Left to fate. Scraping banks. Learn him or kill him.

CHAPTER IX. Shake the reef. Reason off the rails. Face of the water. Bewitching scene. Romance and beauty.

CHAPTER X. Getting cocky. Bit of a takedown. Learn it straight. River rising.

CHAPTER XI. Tract business. Rise effects. Plantations vanish. Endless sea. Sleepwalking pilot. Supernatural piloting. No one there. All saved.

CHAPTER XII. Low water. Yawl sounding. Buoys and lanterns. Cubs and soundings. Boat sunk. Looking for the wreck.

CHAPTER XIII. Pilot’s memory. Wages up. Universal grip. Skill and nerve. Testing a “cub.” “Back her for life.” Good lesson.

CHAPTER XIV. Pilots and captains. High-priced pilots. Demand's high. A whistler. Cheap trade. $250 speed.

CHAPTER XV. New pilots shake up the Pilots’ Association. Crutches and wages. Getting cocky. Captains give in. Association laughs. Secret sign. Admirable system. Tough on outsiders. Tight monopoly. No loophole. Railroads and war.

CHAPTER XVI. All aboard. Glorious start. Loaded to win. Bands and bugles. Boats galore. Racers and racing.

CHAPTER XVII. Cut-offs. Ditching and shooting. Mississippi changes. Wild night. Swearing and guessing. Stephen’s debt. He muddles creditors. New deal. Pays alphabetically.

CHAPTER XVIII. Tough schooling. Shadows. I get inspected. Where’d you get those shoes? Pull her down. I want to kill Brown. I try to run her. I get a compliment.

CHAPTER XIX. Veracity on the line. Bit of tension. I meet the captain. Mr. Brown steps down.

CHAPTER XX. Become a passenger. News hits. Thunderous crash. Standing firm. Blazing sun. Grim scene. His time's up.

CHAPTER XXI. Get my license. War kicks in. Jack-of-all-trades now.

CHAPTER XXII. Try the alias game. Land of goatees. Boots show up. River man missing. Young guy’s down. Sample water. Fine smoke. Big mistake. Check the town. Desolation way-traffic. Wood-yard.

CHAPTER XXIII. Old French settlements. Head to Memphis. Young ladies and Russia-leather bags.

CHAPTER XXIV. Receive info. Alligator boats. Alligator talk. She was a rattler. I’m found out.

CHAPTER XXV. Devil’s oven and table. Bombshell drops. No whitewash. Thirty years on the river. Mississippi uniforms. Accidents and casualties. Two hundred wrecks. Literature loss. Sunday-schools and brick masons.

CHAPTER XXVI. War talk. Tilt over backwards. Fifteen shot-holes. Straight story. Wars and feuds. Darnell vs. Watson. Gang and a woodpile. Western grammar. River changes. New Madrid. Floods and falls.

CHAPTER XXVII. Tourists and notebooks. Captain Hall. Mrs. Trollope’s feelings. Hon. Charles Augustus Murray’s sentiment. Captain Marryat’s sensations. Alexander Mackay’s feelings. Mr. Parkman reports.

CHAPTER XXVIII. Swinging down the river. Named after me. Plum Point again. Lights and snag boats. Endless changes. A lawless river. Changes and jetties. Uncle Mumford testifies. Pegging the river. What the government does. The commission. Men and theories. “Had them bad.” Jews and prices.

CHAPTER XXIX. Murel’s gang. Consummate villain. Ditching witnesses. Stewart’s a traitor. Start a rebellion. Get a new suit. Cover our tracks. Pluck and capacity. Good Samaritan city. The old and the new.

CHAPTER XXX. Melancholy picture. On the move. River gossip. She went by sparklin’. Life’s amenities. Misinformation world. Silence’s eloquence. Hit a snag. Photographically exact. Plank sidewalks.

THE 'BODY OF THE NATION'

The Mississippi's something else. It's not your run-of-the-mill river. If you count the Missouri as its main branch, it stretches out the longest in the world—over 4,300 miles. And it's not just long, it's twisty too. There's a part where it takes 1,300 miles to cover what a crow'd fly in just 675. It dumps out way more water than the St. Lawrence, 25 times more than the Rhine, and a whopping 338 times more than the Thames. Its drainage basin's massive, pulling water from 28 states and territories, stretching from Delaware all the way to Idaho. That's 45 degrees of longitude right there. It feeds into the Gulf from 54 steamboat-navigable rivers and countless others fit for flats and keels. The basin's as big as England, Wales, Scotland, Ireland, France, Spain, Portugal, Germany, Austria, Italy, and Turkey combined. Fertile too, especially the Mississippi Valley itself.

What’s odd is how it narrows toward the mouth instead of widening. From where the Ohio joins, all the way to halfway down to the sea, it's about a mile wide when the water’s high. But then it starts shrinking until it's just over half a mile at the 'Passes,' near the mouth. At the Ohio junction, it’s 87 feet deep, and just keeps getting deeper, hitting 129 feet near the mouth.

The rise and fall's another story. Up top, it's steady down to Natchez, rising about 50 feet. But past that, at Bayou La Fourche, it only rises 24 feet; New Orleans sees 15; and just above the mouth, it’s down to 2.5 feet.

so the 'Times-Democrat' ran a piece about the Mississippi, saying it dumps four hundred and six million tons of mud into the Gulf every year. reminds me of Captain Marryat calling it the 'Great Sewer.' all that mud could stack a mile square and two hundred forty-one feet high.

slowly but surely, the river's building land, but real slow — like a third of a mile over two hundred years. scientists say the river's mouth was once at Baton Rouge, and it's built the land down to the Gulf over a hundred and twenty thousand years. still, it's the youngest dirt around.

Mississippi's got a knack for wild leaps, cutting through land and straightening itself. sometimes it'll chop thirty miles off in one go. these cut-offs can leave towns stranded, like Delta moving from three miles below Vicksburg to two miles above because of a new cut. boundary lines get messy — one day you're in Mississippi, next day Louisiana.

this river's also shifting sideways. Hard Times, La., used to be in Louisiana, now it's in Mississippi 'cause the river moved. that old Mississippi river La Salle traveled? mostly dry land now. river's left and right of the original route.

at the mouth, mud builds slow against the Gulf's waves, but upstream, it's quicker. take Prophet's Island — fifteen hundred acres thirty years ago. now, it's got seven hundred more.

that's enough about the river's quirks. let's pivot to its history, covering its sleepy start, its lively middle, and its calm now in the chapters ahead.

we throw the word "new" around so much with america, it's like we forget there's some old bones here too. sure, we know a few old dates in history class, but they just float by without much weight. saying de soto saw the mississippi in 1542 doesn't really tell us anything. it's like measuring a sunset and missing the colors.

1542 on its own? not much. but drop it next to other events, and it starts to feel like something. when de soto saw that river, the world was buzzing. francis I had just lost at pavia, raphael and bayard were gone, knights got kicked out from rhodes, and the reformation had kicked off. ignatius loyola was just a name, the jesuits were newborns, and michelangelo wasn't done with the sistine chapel. mary queen of scots hadn't shown up yet. elizabeth was a kid. calvin, benvenuto cellini, and charles V were doing their thing. margaret of navarre was writing, and wit kept her alive longer than her religious books did.

religion was on fire, literally. council of trent was in motion, inquisition was having its heyday, and henry VIII was busy reshaping england in his own messy way. when de soto hit the mississippi, luther was still around, servetus hadn't been burned, st. bartholomew's massacre was a ways off. rabelais and cervantes hadn't written their stuff, shakespeare wasn't even born, and cromwell was just future noise.

finding the mississippi sticks a bit of age on us, makes america seem less shiny new and a bit more seasoned.

De Soto caught a glimpse of the river, then kicked the bucket, and got buried in it by his team. You'd think they'd hype up the river's size by ten just to get more adventurers on board, but nope. When they got back, their stories didn't spark much interest. The Mississippi stayed off the radar for a crazy long time, especially by today's standards. To put it in perspective: after De Soto saw the river, about 25 years passed, Shakespeare was born, lived over 50 years, died, and it was more than half a century after that when the second white guy saw the Mississippi. Nowadays, we wouldn't let a marvel like that sit unvisited for 130 years. If a creek was found near the North Pole, Europe and America would scramble — one team to explore and a bunch more to find each other.

Over 150 years, white settlements popped up on the Atlantic coasts. They had all kinds of dealings with the Indians — Spaniards were doing their robbing and converting thing, the English traded beads and blankets (with a side of civilization and whiskey), and the French were playing school and missionary with them, bringing them to Quebec and Montreal for furs. So, they must've heard about the big river out west, but in such a vague way that no one could even guess where or what it really was. You'd think the mystery would pique interest, but it didn't. Nobody seemed to want or need the Mississippi for a century and a half. It stayed untouched. De Soto wasn’t looking for a river, didn't need one, so he didn't care much about it.

Eventually, La Salle, a Frenchman, decided to hunt down and explore the river. Funny how once someone latches onto a good idea, lots of others start thinking the same way.

So why the sudden interest in the river now when no one cared before? They thought it emptied into the Gulf of California, offering a shortcut from Canada to China. Before, folks assumed it flowed into the Atlantic.

CHAPTER 2

The River and Its Explorers

La Salle went to Louis XIV, looking for big privileges, and got them. He got the right to explore, build forts, claim land for the king, and pay for it all himself. In return, he got a few perks, like a monopoly on buffalo hides. He spent years and most of his cash on risky trips between Montreal and his fort in Illinois before finally getting ready to aim for the Mississippi.

other folks lucked out back then. joliet, the merchant, and marquette, the priest, in 1673 made it to the Mississippi. they took the Great Lakes route, canoed from Green Bay, through Fox River, and then the Wisconsin. marquette had this deal with the Virgin Mary on the Immaculate Conception feast—if he found the big river, he'd call it Conception for her. he stuck to it. explorers then always had priests along. de soto had 24 with him. la salle too had a bunch. they might've run low on food or clothes but always packed what was needed for mass. always ready, as they said, to 'explain hell to the natives.'

on june 17, 1673, joliet, marquette, and their crew hit where the Wisconsin meets the Mississippi. parkman noted the wide, fast river cutting through high, forested banks. they turned south, paddling through a silent stretch with no sign of man.

a big catfish hit marquette's canoe. scared him good. indians had warned him about the river's demon who'd swallow them whole. i've seen Mississippi catfish over six feet long, 250 pounds. if marquette's was like that, no wonder he thought the demon had come.

buffalo showed up soon, grazing on prairie near the river. marquette talked about the bulls’ fierce, dumb stares through their tangled manes.

they were careful. landed at night, cooked, then put out the fire, paddled more, and anchored in the river, always on watch till morning.

did this for two weeks without seeing anyone. river was a lonely place then, and mostly still is.

but then, they spotted human footprints on the western bank—a real Robinson Crusoe moment. they’d heard the river indians were as deadly as the demon, attacking without reason. still, joliet and marquette headed into the country to find the track makers. they did, got a warm welcome. if you call an indian chief stripping down to impress hospitable, and having food shoved in your face by ungloved hands, then yeah, they were treated well. next morning, the chief and 600 tribesmen saw them off with a friendly goodbye.

on the rocks above alton, they found some old indian paintings. just below, the missouri dumped its muddy water into the mississippi, dragging along logs and branches. they passed the ohio next, floated through cane-brakes, fought off mosquitoes, and drifted under makeshift awnings in the heat. met some indians, had a feast instead of a fight. they figured the mississippi emptied into the gulf of mexico and took that news back to canada. but they didn’t prove it.

la salle did. he finally got moving at the end of 1681. with henri de tonty and some others, they dragged canoes over frozen rivers till they found open water at peoria lake. paddled down past the missouri and ohio. stopped near the third chickasaw bluffs to build fort prudhomme. the further they went, the more spring showed up—hazy sun, warm air, new leaves.

at the arkansas, greeted by war drums like marquette was, but peace pipes fixed it this time. la salle put up a cross with france's arms, claimed the land for the king, and a priest sang a hymn. he explained their faith to the natives with signs—swapped possible heaven for their actual land. then they got the locals to pledge loyalty to louis the putrid across the sea. no one laughed at the irony.

those performances happened right where Napoleon, Arkansas was gonna be. first confiscation-cross went up there too, on the riverbank. Marquette and Joliet wrapped their voyage there; same spot. De Soto got his first look at the river from there too. pretty wild how three big events in river exploration landed on that same patch of ground. France snatched the land there, and later Napoleon himself gave it back, not to the original folks, but to white American heirs.

the explorers moved on, hit some future historic spots like Vicksburg and Grand Gulf. they met an Indian leader in the Teche area, had a city of sun-baked brick and straw. better built than some places today. chief's house had a big room, sixty elders in white cloaks there when Tonty visited. there was a temple too, decorated with enemy skulls.

then they met the Natchez Indians, near today's Natchez. found a society with religious and political power, a class claiming descent from the sun, a temple, and a sacred fire. kinda like home, but minus Louis XIV.

few days later, La Salle was back at his cross, the waters from all over meeting there. his job done, big achievement. Parkman wraps it up saying France got a massive land grab on paper—Texas, the Mississippi basin, from the cold north to the Gulf’s edge, between Alleghanies and Rockies. all thanks to a voice you couldnt hear a half mile away.

CHAPTER 3

Frescoes from the Past

looked like the river was open for business. but nope, settling along its banks took as long as exploring it. seventy years after the explorers, there werent many white settlers. commerce took another fifty. between La Salle opening the river and actual trade starting, seven English kings came and went, America broke away, French kings Louis XIV and XV died off, French monarchy crumbled in revolution, and Napoleon's name started making the rounds. slow times back then.

early river commerce was all about big barges like keelboats and broadhorns. they’d drift from the upper rivers to new orleans, swap cargo, then the long haul back upstream by hand — that took nine months sometimes. this grew into work for loads of tough guys. rough around the edges, drank hard, fought harder, lived it up in places like natchez-under-the-hill. spent all their money, but mostly honest and reliable.

then steamboats showed up. for about 15-20 years, keelboats still went downstream, but steamers ruled upstream. keelboatmen sold their boats in new orleans, bought a deck spot on the steamers back home.

eventually, steamboats took over everything. keelboating died off. these guys became deckhands, mates, pilots on steamers, or worked on coal-flats or pine-rafts up the mississippi.

in the steamboat boom, the river was full of coal-fleets and timber rafts, all run by hand, all with rough crews. i remember the big rafts passing by hannibal when i was a kid — big enough for a couple dozen men, some wigwams for shelter, and the wild talk of these ex-keelboatmen.

to show you keelboat life, i've got a chapter from a story im working on. it's about a village boy, huck finn, son of the town drunk. he skips out from his dad and a widow trying to clean him up. he's joined by the widow’s slave escaping too. they find part of a lumber raft and drift at night, hide by day, aiming for cairo. but they miss it in the fog. huck swims to a big raft at night to eavesdrop and get their bearings.

a young guy gets impatient when he's dying to know something. we talked about it, and jim figured it was so dark out now, might as well swim to the big raft and sneak on. they'd probably talk about Cairo, planning to stop there, maybe send boats for whiskey or meat. jim had a good head on him, always had a plan when you needed one.

so i shook off my rags, jumped in the river, and swam for the raft's light. got close, slowed down, went careful. all clear, nobody at the sweeps. swam along till i was by the campfire, crawled on and hid among some shingles. thirteen guys there, rough bunch. they had a jug and tin cups, kept it going. one guy was singing, real loud, and it wasnt a song for polite company. stretched the last word of every line. they all whooped like indians after, and another song started:

'there was a woman in our town, loved her husband but another man too.'

fourteen verses of that. kinda lame. before the next verse, someone said the tune was old as dirt, and another wanted a break, told him to walk. they laughed till he got mad, jumped up, cussing, said he could take any thief there.

they were ready to jump him, but the biggest guy stood up:

'stay put, gents. he's mine.'

he jumped, cracked his heels, flung off a buckskin coat with fringe, tossed his hat with ribbons. 'stay there till the beatin's done,' he said.

jumped again, cracked heels, yelled:

'whoo-oop! i'm the iron-jawed, brass-mounted, copper-bellied corpse-maker from arkansas! look at me! sudden death and general desolation! born from a hurricane, damned by an earthquake! i eat alligators and whiskey for breakfast, rattlesnakes and a corpse when sick! i split rocks with a glance, thunder stops when i speak! whoo-oop! give me room! blood's my drink, death's music to my ears! look and hold your breath, im bout to unleash!'

while he was getting all this off, he was shaking his head, looking fierce, kind of puffing up, making a little circle, rolling up his sleeves, and now and then beating his chest with his fist, shouting, 'look at me, gentlemen!' when he finished, he jumped up, clicked his heels three times, and let out a big 'whoo-oop! i'm the bloodiest son of a wildcat alive!'

then the guy who started the mess tilted his old hat down over one eye, bent forward, sticking his rear out, fists pumping in and out, circling around a few times, puffing himself up. he straightened, jumped up, clicked his heels three times, landed to cheers, and started shouting—

'whoo-oop! bow your neck and spread, kingdom of sorrow's coming! hold me down, my powers are working! whoo-oop! i'm a child of sin, don't let me get going! smoked glass for all! don't look at me without it! when i play, i use meridians for a net, drag the atlantic for whales! scratch my head with lightning, purr to sleep with thunder! when cold, boil the gulf and bathe; when hot, fan with a storm; thirsty, suck a cloud dry! when hungry, famine follows! whoo-oop! bow and spread! hand on the sun, make night; bite the moon, hurry seasons; shake and crumble mountains! see me through leather—not bare eyes! petrified heart, iron guts! massacre's my pastime, destruction my business! vast desert's my yard, bury my dead there!' he jumped, clicked heels, landed to more cheers, shouted: 'whoo-oop! bow and spread, pet child of calamity's coming!'

then the other started puffing and blowing again—first one, called bob; then child of calamity jumped back in, bigger than ever; both at it, circling each other, fists close to faces, whooping and jawing like injuns; bob called child names, child called back; bob got rougher, child hit back with worse; bob knocked child’s hat off, child picked it up, kicked bob's hat six feet; bob got it, said this wasn't over, he'd remember and never forgive, child better watch out, time's coming, he'd answer with his best blood. child said he'd wait for that, warned bob not to cross him again, couldn't rest till he waded in his blood, sparing him for now, on account of family, if he had one.

both of them started backing off, growling and shaking their heads, talking big about what they’d do next. but this little black-whiskered guy jumped in and said—

'come back here, you couple of chicken-livered cowards, and i'll thrash you both!'

and he did. he grabbed them, jerked them around, kicked them, knocked them down quicker than they could get up. in no time, they were begging like dogs. everyone else was laughing and cheering, shouting things like 'go get 'em, corpse-maker!' and 'hit him again, child of calamity!' it was chaos for a bit. bob and the child ended up with red noses and black eyes. little davy made them admit they were cowards, not fit to eat with a dog. then bob and the child shook hands, all serious, saying they always respected each other and decided to let it go. they washed up in the river, and then got called to help with the crossing. some went forward, others went aft.

i stayed put for fifteen minutes, lit up a smoke from a pipe one of them left. after the crossing, they came back, had a drink, and started up with talking and singing again. someone pulled out an old fiddle, one played, another patted juba, and the rest joined in for a good old-fashioned keel-boat breakdown. they couldn’t keep it up long, got winded, so they circled back around the jug.

they sang 'jolly, jolly raftman's the life for me,' with a loud chorus, then started talking about all sorts: hogs and their habits, women and their ways, putting out house fires, what to do about the indians, what kings do and how much they make, making cats fight, handling fits, and differences between clear-water and muddy-water rivers. ed claimed the muddy mississippi was better to drink than the ohio. said if you let it settle, you’d get a bunch of mud at the bottom, and it wasn't better than ohio water then. he said you had to keep it stirred up and even add mud when the river was low.

the child of calamity agreed, said the mud was nutritious. said a man drinking mississippi water could grow corn in his stomach if he wanted. he added—

'look at the graveyards; that’s proof. trees barely grow in a cincinnati graveyard, but in st. louis, they reach up to eight hundred feet. it’s all because of the water folks drank before they got buried. a cincinnati corpse doesn’t enrich the soil any.'

they got talking about how ohio water doesn't mix well with mississippi water. ed mentioned if you catch the mississippi on a rise when the ohio's low, you'll see a wide band of clear water down the east side of the mississippi for a hundred miles or more. the minute you get out a quarter mile from shore and cross the line, it's all thick and yellow. then they switched to keeping tobacco from getting moldy, and finally went on about ghosts folks have seen. ed cuts in—

"why don't you talk about what you've seen yourselves? let me tell you this. five years back, i was on a raft this big. it was a bright moonlit night, i was on watch, boss of the starboard oar forward. one of my pals, dick allbright, came by, yawning and stretching, washed his face in the river, and sat down next to me, lighting up his pipe. he'd just filled it when he looks up and says—

'“isn't that buck miller's place, over in the bend?”

'“yeah,” i say, “it is—why?” he puts his pipe down, leans his head on his hand, and says—

'“i thought we'd be further down.” i replied—

'“i thought so too, when i went off watch—six hours on, six off—but the boys told me the raft didn't seem to move much for the last hour, though she's slipping along fine now.” he groans and says—

'“seen a raft act like this before. current's almost gone above this bend the last two years.”

he stood up a few times, looking around on the water. that got me doing it too—people just follow others, even if it doesn’t make sense. soon, i spot something black floating off starboard, behind us. he was looking at it too. i ask—

'“what's that?” he says, sort of annoyed,—

'“nothing but an old empty barrel.”

'“an empty barrel!” i say, “a spyglass can't match your eyes. how can you tell it's empty?” he says—

'“don't know; reckon it ain't a barrel, but thought it might be,” he says.

'“yeah,” i say, “might be, might be anything else too; can't tell from this distance.”

with nothing else to do, we kept watching it. then i say—

'“look, dick allbright, that thing's gaining on us, i think.”

he said nothing. the thing kept gaining, and i figured it was a dog, tired out. as we swung into the crossing, it floated across the moonlit water, and sure enough, it was a barrel. i say—

'“dick allbright, what made you think it was a barrel from half a mile away?” he says—

'“don't know.” i say—

'“tell me, dick allbright.” he says—

'“well, i knew it was a barrel; seen it before; lot of folks have seen it; they say it's a haunted barrel.”

called the rest of the watch, they came over, and i told them what dick said. the thing floated alongside, not gaining. about twenty feet off. some wanted it aboard, but others said no. dick allbright said messing with it brought bad luck. watch captain didn't buy it. figured the barrel was in a better current than us. said it'd drift off eventually.

we switched topics, had a song, and a breakdown, then the captain wanted another song. started clouding up, though, and the barrel stayed put. song didn't take off, fizzled out, no cheers. awkward silence. everyone tried talking at once, one guy cracked a joke but nothing. no laughs. even he didn't laugh, which is weird. we all got quiet, watching the barrel, feeling uneasy. then it got dark, wind moaned, lightning started, thunder rolled. storm hit hard. in the chaos, a guy running aft fell, sprained his ankle, had to lay up. boys shook their heads. lightning showed the barrel with blue lights blinking. kept an eye on it. by dawn, gone. daylight came, no sign of it, and we weren't sad to see it go.

but next night, around half-past nine, while songs and fun were going, it showed up again, same spot on the starboard side. killed the mood. everyone solemn, no talking, just sitting around staring at the barrel. clouds rolled in again. watch changed, off watch stayed up instead of sleeping. storm raged all night, another guy tripped, sprained his ankle, had to stop working. barrel vanished by daybreak, no one saw it leave.

everyone was down all day. not sober like laying off booze, more like quiet. but they drank more, not together, each slipping off for a private drink.

after dark, nobody went to bed. no singing, no talking. they all huddled together up front, staring in the same direction, just sighing now and then. then, here comes the barrel again. parked itself right where it was before. stayed there all night. storm hit after midnight—really dark, pouring rain, hail, thunder roaring, wind like a hurricane. lightning lit everything up, showing the whole raft like it was daytime. river looked white and wild, and there's that barrel, same as always. captain said to man the sweeps for a crossing, but nobody moved—no more sprained ankles for them, they said. then, bam, lightning took out two men, hurt two more. how? sprained their ankles.

the barrel disappeared before dawn. nobody touched breakfast. rest of the day, the men just hung around in small groups, talking low. but they shunned Dick Allbright. whenever he came near, they split up. wouldn't work the sweeps with him. captain kept the skiffs close and wouldn't let the dead be buried, thinking no one would come back if they stepped ashore, and he was right.

night came, and everyone knew there'd be trouble if the barrel showed again. talk turned ugly. some wanted to kill Dick because he'd seen the barrel before. others said put him ashore. or maybe all go ashore if the barrel came back.

whispers were still going when, sure enough, here it comes again. quiet as a pin drop. captain shows up and says, "stop acting like kids. we don't want this barrel all the way to Orleans. best way to deal with it? burn it. i'm bringing it aboard." before anyone could argue, he dove in.

he swam to it, pushed it to the raft. men stepped aside. old man busted it open, and there was a baby inside. Dick Allbright's baby. he admitted it.

“yep,” he leans over it, “that’s my lamented darling, poor lost Charles William Allbright deceased,” he says. man could wrap his tongue around any word he liked, lay them out smooth as you please. he said he lived up the bend, one night choked his crying kid, not meaning to kill it—probably lying—and got scared, buried it in a barrel before his wife got home, and ran off to the north, took up rafting. third year now, that barrel’s chasing him. bad luck starts light, then four men die, and the barrel stops. said if the men could stand one more night—going on like that—but they’d had it. tried to boat him ashore and lynch him, but he grabs the kid, jumps overboard, hugging it, crying, and we never saw him or Charles William again.

'who was crying?' says Bob; 'Allbright or the baby?'

'allbright, of course; baby’s been dead three years—how could it cry?'

'never mind that—how’d it keep all that time?' says Davy.

'i don’t know how, just did,' says Ed.

'what’d they do with the barrel?' says the Child of Calamity.

'tossed it overboard, sunk like lead.'

'edward, did the child look choked?' says one.

'did it have its hair parted?' says another.

'what brand was on that barrel, Eddy?' says a guy named Bill.

'have papers for those stats, Edmund?' says Jimmy.

'say, Edwin, were you one of the guys killed by lightning?' says Davy.

'him? oh no, he was both of 'em,' says Bob. and they all laugh.

'edward, take a pill? you look bad—don’t you feel pale?' says the Child of Calamity.

'come on, Eddy,' says Jimmy, 'show up; you must’ve kept part of that barrel. show us the bunghole—do it, and we’ll believe you.'

'say, boys,' says Bill, 'let’s divide it. thirteen of us. i can swallow a thirteenth of the yarn, if you can down the rest.'

Ed got mad, told them off, cussed to himself walking off, them yelling, jeering, laughing so loud you'd hear it a mile away.

'boys, let’s split a watermelon on that,' says the Child of Calamity; rummaging in the dark where i was, put his hand on me. warm, soft, naked; he says 'ouch!' jumps back.

'fetch a lantern or fire here, boys—there’s a snake big as a cow!'

they run with a lantern, crowd up, look at me.

'come out, you beggar!' says one.

'who are you?' says another.

'what’re you after? speak up, or overboard you go.'

'snake him out, boys. snatch him by the heels.'

i start begging, crawl out trembling. they look me over, wondering, and the Child of Calamity says—

'a cussed thief! lend a hand, let’s toss him overboard!'

'no,' says Big Bob, 'let’s get the paint-pot, paint him sky blue head to heel, then toss him!'

'good, that’s it. go for the paint, Jimmy.'

when the paint showed up, and Bob grabbed the brush ready to start, everyone else was laughing, rubbing their hands, and i started crying. that hit Davy, and he goes—

"Stop that! He’s just a kid. I'll deal with anyone who touches him!"

i looked at them, and some grumbled, but Bob put the paint down, and no one else picked it up.

"Come by the fire, let’s see what you’ve got going on," Davy says. "Sit down and explain yourself. How long have you been here?"

"Not even a minute, sir," i said.

"How’d you dry off so fast?"

"Don’t know, sir. Just happens like that."

"Oh, does it? What’s your name?"

i didn't wanna give my real name, so i said—

"Charles William Allbright, sir."

they all roared with laughter; i was glad i said it, hoping the humor would lighten the mood.

when they finished, Davy goes—

"That won’t do, Charles William. You couldn’t have gotten this big in five years, and you were just a baby when you came out of the barrel, and dead at that. Come on, tell it straight. No one’ll hurt you if you’re not causing trouble. What _is_ your name?"

"Aleck Hopkins, sir. Aleck James Hopkins."

"Alright, Aleck, where’d you come from?"

"From a trading scow up the bend. Born on it. Pap’s been trading here forever. He told me to swim over because when you passed, he wanted you to tell Mr. Jonas Turner in Cairo—"

"Oh, come on!"

"Yes, sir, it’s true; Pap says—"

"Oh, your grandmother!"

they laughed again, cutting me off.

"Listen," says Davy, "you’re scared, so you’re talking wild. Honestly, do you live in a scow, or is it a lie?"

"Yes, sir, in a trading scow. Up at the bend. But it’s our first trip."

"Now you're making sense! What are you doing here? Stealing?"

"No, sir, just wanted a ride on the raft. All boys do."

"Yeah, I know. But why hide?"

"Some folks chase the boys off."

"Right. They might be stealing. Look, if we let you go this time, will you stay out of trouble?"

"Promise I will, boss. Try me."

"Alright. You’re not far from shore. Overboard now, and don’t be dumb like this again. Some raftsmen would’ve beat you black and blue!"

i didn’t stick around for goodbyes, just jumped overboard and swam to shore. when Jim came by later, the big raft was out of sight around the point. i swam out, got on board, and was real glad to be home again.

the boy didn’t get what he was after, but his little adventure gave a glimpse into the life of old-time raftsmen and keelboatmen, which i wanted to share here.

now, let’s dive into a part of Mississippi River life during steamboat’s glory days—pilot science. i reckon there’s nothing like it anywhere else.

when i was a kid, everyone around me in hannibal, missouri, wanted to be a steamboatman. we had other dreams pop up now and then, like being clowns when the circus rolled through or trying our hand at minstrel shows. sometimes we even hoped to be pirates if we were good and lucky. but those dreams faded out pretty quick. becoming a steamboatman, though, that stuck.

every day, a flashy packet would come from st. louis or keokuk. the whole village waited for it. before it showed up, the day was full of promise. after, it was just empty. i can still see it in my mind: the town all sleepy in the summer sun, streets mostly empty, a couple clerks dozing in front of the stores with shavings around them, pigs snuffling through watermelon rinds, freight piles scattered on the levee, and the town drunk asleep in the shade. the mississippi, wide and shining, with the thick forest on the other side. then, a wisp of smoke on the horizon, and the drayman’s shout, 'steamboat a-comin'!' and everything changed. the town woke up, people spilled out of every building, and suddenly, the place was buzzing.

drays, carts, folks, kids all rushin from all sides to the wharf. everyone’s eyes locked on the incoming boat like it’s their first time seeing it. and yeah, it’s pretty impressive. long, sharp, neat; two tall chimneys with some fancy gilded thing between ’em; a decorative pilot-house perched on the texas deck behind. paddle-boxes flash a picture or gilded rays above the boat’s name; decks fenced with spotless white railings; flag waving proud from the jack-staff; furnace doors wide open, fires blazing strong; upper decks packed with passengers; captain by the big bell, calm and in charge, everyone’s envy; thick black smoke rolls from the chimneys—just a touch of pitch pine for show before hitting town; crew gathered on the forecastle, broad stage extended over the bow, a deckhand stands with a rope, looking sharp; steam hissing through gauge-cocks, captain raises his hand, bell rings, wheels halt; then reverse, churning foam, and the steamer rests. chaos follows—boarding, disembarking, loading, unloading freight all at once; mates yelling and cursing to make it all faster! ten minutes later, steamer’s off, flag down, smoke gone. ten more, town’s dead again, and the town drunk’s back to sleep by the skids.

my dad was a justice of the peace. i thought he could hang anyone who ticked him off. that was cool enough for me most days. but i couldn't shake wanting to be a steamboatman. started dreaming of being a cabin-boy, wearing a white apron, shaking a tablecloth where my buddies could see. then thought maybe a deckhand with a rope would be more my style—right in the spotlight. those were just dreams, though—too good to be true.

then one of our guys left town, and when he came back, he was a striker on a steamboat. flipped everything i believed from sunday school. that guy was worldly, and i was the opposite, yet there he was, living the dream, while i was stuck here. he wasn’t humble about it either. always managed to find a rusty bolt to scrub in full view so we'd all envy him. when his boat was docked, he'd strut around town in his greasy clothes, talking in steamboat lingo like we were supposed to understand.

he'd drop words like 'labboard' about horses and talk about 'St. Looy' like he'd lived there forever. mentioning times he was on Fourth Street or handling fires on 'the old Big Missouri'. he told big tales about towns burning down there, making our trips to St. Louis seem lame. he had money, hair oil, a cheap silver watch, and a flashy chain. no suspenders, just a leather belt. every girl fell for him. he beat every guy in town.

when his boat blew up, we finally felt at peace. but he came back, a hero all bandaged up, and everyone ogled him at church. seemed like fate really had it in for us, favoring this guy who didn’t deserve it.

that whole river dream, it was like a fever. one kid after another got on those boats. minister's kid ended up an engineer. doctor's and post-master's boys became 'mud clerks.' liquor dealer's son? barkeeper. top merchant's sons, couple of judge's sons—they got to be pilots. pilots were the big shots. even with the small pay, they pulled in $150 to $250 a month, no board costs. two months of that could cover a preacher's salary for a year. but some of us? we just watched. our folks wouldn't let us near it.

so i decided, screw it, id run off. said i wouldn't come back till i was a pilot. but man, that was harder than it sounded. hit up a few boats over at st. louis, all lined up like sardines, and tried asking for pilots. mostly got the cold shoulder from mates and clerks. took it on the chin, but i dreamed of one day being the big pilot, loaded with cash, and showing those guys what's what.

months went by, and i finally had to let that dream die. didn't have the guts to go home, though. ended up in cincinnati, trying to figure out my next move. been reading about some government expedition down the amazon. they hadn't covered the headwaters, four thousand miles upriver. from cincinnati to new orleans was just fifteen hundred miles. figured id hop a ship there. had thirty bucks left. decided id finish what they couldn't. details? not my strong suit. packed my bag, boarded this old tub, 'paul jones,' for new orleans. sixteen bucks got me a run-down saloon mostly to myself. nobody else wanted to touch that boat.

once we hit the ohio river, i felt reborn. traveler—that word tasted sweet. never felt such a rush since. felt so high above it all, i even pitied folks who hadn't traveled, without much contempt. but when we stopped, id lean on the railings, watching the country kids on the banks. if they didn't notice me, id sneeze or shift so they had to see me. as soon as they did, id act all bored with the whole traveling thing.

kept my hat off the whole time, letting the wind and sun do their thing. wanted that bronzed, weathered look of a seasoned traveler. by day two, i was grinning like an idiot. skin started to blister and peel. felt like a badge of honor. wished the folks back home could see me.

we hit louisville—well, near it. got stuck on the rocks in the river for four days. started to feel like part of the boat crew, like the captain's kid or something. was all proud and attached. didn’t know the real boatmen laughed at landlubbers like me. really wanted the big, gruff mate to notice me. waited for a chance to help him out. finally, during a spar setup, he shouted for a capstan bar. jumped up and said, "where's it at? i'll grab it!"

mate looked at me like i’d grown a second head. stopped cussing for a second, just stared. finally said, "well, if this don't beat hell!" and went back to his work, like i was some unsolvable puzzle.

spent the rest of the day laying low. skipped dinner, avoided supper till it was over. didn’t feel like part of the crew anymore. but as we moved on downriver, my mood picked up bit by bit. hated the mate for making me feel small, but couldn’t help admiring him. he was a giant, all muscle and beard, tattoos of red and blue women on his arms, and man, his swearing was something to hear. when he unloaded cargo, i was always nearby, soaking it in. he knew his power and made sure everyone else did too. his orders were like thunder, full of energy and cussing. couldn’t help but compare it to how a regular guy would ask for something. a landsman would probably say, "hey, could you move that plank?" but the mate? he’d bellow, "move that gang-plank! hurry up! what’re you doing? SNATCH it! aft again! don’t you hear me? where you taking that barrel? forward with it before i make you eat it!" 

wished i could talk like that.

when the soreness from my trip with the mate finally started to fade, i thought about getting to know someone on the boat. the night watchman was the lowest rank i could find. he brushed me off at first, but i offered him a new chalk pipe, and that cracked the ice. he let me sit by him near the big bell up on the hurricane deck, and after a bit, he started talking. couldn't help it really, i listened like every word was gold, and he noticed. he started naming off capes and islands we passed under the stars and then got into talking about himself. he seemed pretty sentimental for a guy making six bucks a week. maybe an older person would've found it strange, but i soaked it all in. didn't matter that he was a bit rough around the edges, with his gin breath and bad grammar. he was a wronged man, had seen his share of trouble. that was enough for me. as he told his story, tears hit the lantern in his lap, and i found myself tearing up too.

he claimed he was the son of an english nobleman—couldn't remember if it was an earl or an alderman, maybe both. said his dad loved him, but his mom didn't, so he got shipped off to some old college. after his dad died, his mom took the estate and 'shook' him, as he put it. nobility friends got him a spot as a 'loblolly-boy in a ship' after that. from there, his story turned wild, full of bloodshed and narrow escapes. i sat there, speechless, soaking up every crazy detail.

later, it was a letdown to find out he was just a low-brow, half-witted storyteller from illinois. he'd read too many dime novels, mixed up their plots, and spun them into his own yarn until he even believed it.

chapter 6

a cub-pilot's experience

what with being stuck on the rocks four days at louisville and other delays, the 'paul jones' took about two weeks to get from cincinnati to new orleans. this gave me time to get to know one of the pilots. he taught me how to steer the boat, making river life even more fascinating.

also met a young guy who took deck passage—more's the pity. he borrowed six bucks, said he'd pay it back the day after we docked. never saw him again. probably died or forgot, since he claimed his folks were loaded and said deck passage was cooler.

found out quick. no ships heading to the Amazon for a decade, maybe. plus, the cash i had wouldn’t cover even a basic exploration. needed a new plan. the 'Paul Jones' was set for St. Louis. decided to negotiate with my pilot, and after three days, he caved. he'd teach me the Mississippi from New Orleans to St. Louis for five hundred bucks, payable once i started earning. so, i jumped into the challenge of learning over a thousand miles of river, confident and clueless. thought piloting was just about keeping the boat in the river—didn’t see the trick in that, considering its width.

we pulled out of New Orleans at four. 'our watch' till eight. Bixby, the chief, got us going, and then tossed me the wheel, saying, 'shave those steamships close.' heart raced; felt like we'd graze every ship. tried to steer us clear, had thoughts about my pilot’s judgment but stayed quiet. got us a nice gap, then got shoved aside. Bixby took over, weaving us close again, calling me a coward. stung, but had to respect how he handled that wheel. once he cooled, explained the easy water was near shore, current outside. hug the bank going up, stay out going down. i figured i'd stick to downstream piloting.

Bixby kept pointing things out—'Six-Mile Point,' 'Nine-Mile Point,' 'Twelve-Mile Point.' all looked the same to me. hoped he'd change topics, but no luck. he’d hug the shore, then say, 'slack water ends here, cross over.' he'd take the wheel back when i messed up, which was often. almost took out sugar plantations or drifted too far.

finally, watch ended. ate, crashed. midnight, lantern in my face, night watchman saying, 'come on, turn out!'

and then he left. no clue why, so I just stopped thinking about it and dozed off. then the watchman came back, all gruff. annoyed me. told him, "why you bothering me? now i wont sleep."

watchman goes, "if this an't good, i'm blest."

the off-watch guys were turning in, laughing at me like, "hey, watchman! new cub's not out yet? delicate, huh? give him sugar and sing him a lullaby."

then mr. bixby shows up. a minute later, im heading up to the pilot-house, half-dressed, the rest in my arms. bixby's behind me, talking. never really thought about this part of piloting, getting up in the dead of night. boats run all night, but someone’s gotta be awake to steer. starting to see piloting's less romantic, more work.

night’s kinda dingy, few stars out. big mate’s steering by a star, keeping us up the river middle. riverbanks about half a mile off, but they feel far, vague. mate says, "gotta land at jones’s plantation, sir."

inside, im thinking, good luck, bixby. hope you find it, but doubt it. 

bixby asks, "upper or lower end?"

mate says, "upper."

bixby goes, "cant do it. stumps out of water. lower’s not far, you'll make do."

mate says, "all right. jones can lump it."

mate leaves. my exultation cools, wonder rises. bixby plans to find this plantation, any end you want, in this dark. wanted to ask if he’s crazy, but held back. kept quiet. 

bixby heads for shore, scraping it like it’s daylight, singing. felt like my life’s with a reckless guy. then he asks me, "what’s the first point above new orleans?"

happy to answer, i say, "dont know."

"don't know?" he says, jolting me. back to square one. i repeat, "dont know."

bixby says, "smart one, aren’t you? what’s the next point?"

again, i dont know.

man, this is something else. name one point or place i told you.

i thought hard and realized i couldnt.

what do you start out from, above Twelve-Mile Point, to cross over?

i--i--dont know.

you--you--dont know? what do you know?

i--i--nothing, for certain.

by the great caesars ghost, i believe you! youre the stupidest dunderhead i ever saw. the idea of you being a pilot—you! you dont know enough to pilot a cow down a lane.

he was fired up. pacing around like the floor was hot. he'd stew, then lash out at me again.

look here! why do you think i told you the names of those points?

i thought a moment, then temptation got me: well—to—to—be entertaining, i thought.

that set him off. raging, storming, he ran over the steering-oar of a trading-scow. traders cursed up a storm and mr. bixby was grateful for the backtalk. he threw open a window, cursed louder and longer, till he was all out of words.

then, in the gentlest way—my boy, get a little notebook, write everything down. only way to be a pilot is to know this river by heart.

that hit hard; my memory was a sieve. but i figured old bixby was exaggerating a bit. later he rang the bell. night was dark as ink. wheels churned along the bank; i wasnt sure i saw the shore. the watchman called up—

whats this, sir?

joness plantation.

i thought, id bet its not. but i kept quiet. bixby handled the engine bells, boat hit land, torch glowed, man jumped ashore, voice said, gimme de kyarp-bag, mars jones, and we pulled away. i thought, finding that plantation was pure luck; it couldnt happen again in a hundred years. and i believed it was an accident, too.

by the time we hit seven or eight hundred miles up that river, i got decent at steering upstream in daylight. before we got to st. louis, i'd made a little headway on night work, but just a bit. my notebook was packed with town names, points, bars, islands, bends, reaches — you name it. problem was, all that info was stuck in the book, not my head. bugged me that half the river was missing in there. we did four hours on, four hours off, day and night, so every sleep left a four-hour gap. 

my chief got a gig on a big new orleans boat, and i tagged along. she was something else. standing in the pilot-house felt like being on a mountain; decks stretched out forever, made the little 'paul jones' seem tiny. pilot-house there was a grand glass palace, room to dance, with fancy red and gold curtains, an impressive sofa, leather cushions, even a proper backrest for visiting pilots to spin yarns and check the river. fancy spit cups instead of a sawdust box; new oil-cloth floor; big stove for winter; wheel as tall as me, all inlaid; wire tiller-rope; shiny brass knobs for bells; and a white-aproned texas-tender bringing tarts, ices, and coffee during mid-watch. this was 'something like,' and i figured maybe piloting was romantic after all. soon as we got moving, i prowled around that steamer, just soaking it all in. clean as a drawing-room; looking down her long saloon was like peering through a golden tunnel. oil paintings on every stateroom door, loads of chandeliers, elegant clerk's office, amazing bar, and the barkeeper was polished to perfection. the boiler deck was like a church, and the forecastle the same, packed with a battalion of deckhands, firemen, roustabouts. fires blazing from a long line of furnaces, eight massive boilers! unutterable pomp. mighty engines — but yeah, enough. never felt so fine. and when the regiment of neat servants started 'sir'-ing me, i was over the moon.

when i got back to the pilot-house, st. louis was gone and i was lost. this stretch of river was all in my book, but i couldn't make sense of it. it was flipped around. i'd seen it coming upstream, but never stopped to picture it from the other side. heartache again, because turns out i had to learn this tricky river both ways.

pilot-house was jammed with pilots, all there to "look at the river." upper river's tricky — between St. Louis and Cairo, where Ohio joins in. river changes channels all the time, so these guys had to head down to Cairo if they were docked a week; especially when water's low. some were there hoping for a gig — staying sharp, ready to snag a trip if a regular pilot got sick or something. others just rode along for the free grub; cheaper than staying onshore. they'd gotten picky, only riding boats with decent food. anyway, these visiting pilots helped out with buoying the channel, whatever was needed. they could talk river all day, and did. nothing else mattered to them.

we had a handful of these guys with us this trip. maybe eight or ten, plenty of room for them in the pilot-house. some were decked out: silk hats, fancy shirts, diamonds, kid gloves, shiny boots. they talked like they had money and reputation to burn. rest were dressed casual, like they stepped out of an old painting with those tall felt hats.

me? i was just a shadow there, felt like i barely existed. didn’t even get to touch the wheel; closest guest did when things got tight, which was often with the winding channel and low water. i parked in a corner, listening — and man, that talk drained any hope i had. one guy asked another how he ran Plum Point.

'jim, how'd you take Plum Point, coming up?'

'ran it at night, like a guy on the “Diana” told me. started fifty yards above the wood pile on the false point, kept to the cabin under Plum Point till i hit the reef—quarter less twain—then aimed for the middle bar till i was even with that old cottonwood in the bend, then lined up the stern on the cottonwood, head on the low spot above the point, and plowed through—nine and a half.'

'solid crossing, huh?'

'yeah, but the upper bar's shifting down fast.'

another pilot chimed in—

'i had deeper water, ran it lower down; started at the false point—mark twain—hit the second reef by the big snag in the bend, had quarter less twain.'

one of the fancy ones piped up—

'not to nitpick your leadsmen, but that's a lot of water for Plum Point, if you ask me.'

everyone nodded when the bragger got shut down. but what stuck with me was realizing, 'man, if my ears aren't lying, i gotta memorize all the towns, islands, bends, every snag and one-limbed tree for twelve hundred miles. and know it in the dark, too. unless these guys can see through pitch black. wish piloting was in jericho, and i'd never thought of it.'

as dusk hit, mr. bixby rang the big bell three times to land, and the captain showed up. mr. bixby goes, 'we'll lay up here all night, captain.' and the captain's just, 'very well, sir.' the boat docked for the night. pretty wild that the pilot calls the shots, not the captain. had my dinner, hit the sack, worn out by the day's mess. my notes were a jumble of names, all twisted up. hoped sleep would help, but nah, my brain kept replaying it till sunrise.

next morning, feeling rough. we pushed on, taking risks to get out of the river to cairo before night. but the other pilot grounded us. lost time, and night was catching up. bad news for visiting pilots, their boats stuck waiting. chatter in the pilot-house got serious. upstream, pilots didn't fear low water or dark—just fog. downstream, though, with a strong current, it's risky to run at night in low water.

one hope: clear the tricky hat island crossing before dark. then smoother sailing. trying it at night? madness. watches checked, speeds calculated, hat island on everyone's mind. hope rose and fell with each delay. tension built up, even i felt it, wishing for a breather on shore. we weren’t doing regular shifts. each pilot took the parts of the river they knew best, running constantly.

an hour before sunset, mr. bixby took the wheel. everyone clutched their watches, silent, restless. finally, someone sighed, heavy as doom...

well, there's Hat Island up ahead—no way we're hitting it. all the watches snapped shut, everyone mumbling about how it was "too bad, too bad—just half an hour sooner!" place was thick with disappointment. some folks started to leave, but hung around, no bell to land. sun dipped past the horizon, boat kept going. we exchanged looks; one guy had his hand on the door, but let it go. kept heading down the bend. more looks, nods, but no words. guys gathered behind Mr. Bixby as the sky darkened and stars peeked out. silence was heavy. Bixby pulled the cord, two deep bell notes drifted into the night. then another. watchman shouted from the deck—

'labboard lead, there! stabboard lead!'

leadsmen's cries started up, echoed on the deck.

'm-a-r-k three!... m-a-r-k three!... quarter-less three!... half twain!... quarter twain!... m-a-r-k twain!... quarter-less—'

Bixby pulled two more ropes, faint jingles from below, speed dropped. steam whistled. cries kept on—eerie sound in the dark. pilots watching, whispering, only Bixby calm. he held the wheel, stood on a spoke, steamer swung into invisible marks—felt like a vast, dark sea—he steadied her. among the murmurs, caught phrases—

'there; she's over the first reef all right!'

after a pause, another voice—

'her stern's coming down just exactly right, by george!'

'now she's in the marks; over she goes!'

someone muttered—

'oh, it was done beautiful—_beautiful_!'

engines stopped, drifted with the current. stars were out, couldn't see us drift. felt the boat's stillness. noticed a darker shadow—head of the island. closing in. deeper shadow, peril felt suffocating, urge to act strong. but Bixby stood by his wheel, silent, focused, and pilots gathered at his back.

'she'll not make it!' someone whispered.

water got shallower and shallower, leadsman's cries down to—

'eight-and-a-half!.... e-i-g-h-t feet!.... e-i-g-h-t feet!.... seven-and—'

Bixby warned the engineer through the tube—

'stand by, now!'

'aye-aye, sir!'

'seven-and-a-half! seven feet! six-and—'

we hit bottom! Bixby rang the bells, shouted, 'NOW, let her have it—every ounce you've got!' to his partner, 'put her hard down! snatch her! snatch her!' boat scraped through sand, leaned on the edge of disaster, then over she went! the cheer behind Bixby was like nothing before!

no more trouble after that. Bixby was the hero that night; took a while before river men stopped talking about it.

really to get how precise you gotta be steering a big steamer in that murky mess, you gotta know it's not just dodging snags and hidden reefs. you gotta skirt so close to the island you brush the trees with your stern, and there's a spot where you're almost touching a sunken wreck. hit that, and it’d tear the hull apart, wreck a quarter mil in boat and cargo in minutes, and maybe take a hundred and fifty souls with it.

last thing i heard that night was one of our guests muttering, "by the shadow of death, but he's a lightning pilot!"

chapter 8

perplexing lessons

felt like ages, but i finally crammed my head with islands, towns, bars, points, and bends. felt like a dead weight of lumber in there. still, i could shut my eyes and rattle off a bunch of names, only skipping like ten miles of river every fifty or so. started thinking maybe i could take a boat to new orleans if it could jump those gaps. but any smugness got stomped fast by mr. bixby. one day he asked, "what's the shape of walnut bend?"

might as well asked me about my grandma’s take on protoplasm. i thought for a second, said i didn’t know it had a shape. he blew up, let off steam till he ran out of words.

i'd learned he had a set number of words, and once spent, he'd calm down. called him "old" but he was only thirty-four. i waited. then he said, "my boy, you gotta know the river’s shape perfectly. that’s all there is to steer by on a pitch-dark night. everything else is gone. but remember, it don’t look the same at night as in the day."

"how am i ever gonna learn it?"

"how do you walk your hall at home in the dark? cause you know its shape. you can’t see it."

"you're saying i gotta know every tiny twist in this river like i know my own hallway?"

"better than any man knows his own hallway."

"i wish i was dead!"

"not to discourage you, but—"

"just give it to me straight. better now than later."

you gotta learn this, no dodging it. starlight nights throw shadows that mess with you. if you dont know the shore's shape, you'll freak at every shadow, thinking it's land. you'd stay 50 yards out when you should be 50 feet away. can't see snags in those shadows, but the river's shape tells you where they are. pitch-dark nights? different game. everything looks like straight lines, but you gotta trust your memory of the curves. then there's gray mist, messes up any shore's shape. even the oldest river guy gets tangled. moonlight changes things again. 

'do i really have to learn all these variations? my head can't handle that.'

'no, just learn the river's shape so well you steer by memory, not sight.'

'ok, but does the shape stay the same or will it change?'

before bixby could answer, mr. w came in for the watch. 

'bixby, watch out for president's island and up past old hen and chickens. banks are caving, shores are shifting. point above 40's unrecognizable. you can go inside the old sycamore-snag now.'

so, question answered. shores change constantly. felt like i'll never know enough to be a pilot. gotta learn it new every day.

that night, we watched till midnight. river pilots have this ritual when switching watches. one puts on gloves, lights a cigar, the other reports:

'upper bar's shifting at hale's point. quarter twain on the lower lead, mark twain on the other.'

'yeah, saw it last trip. any boats?'

'met one at head of 21, hugging the bar. think it was “sunny south”–no skylights before the chimneys.'

so the other pilot, Mr. W, shows up twelve minutes late, and that's a big no-no among pilots. Mr. Bixby just hands over the wheel and storms out, doesn't say a word. i'm thinking, this is crazy, we're in the dark part of the river, no landmarks, and he's leaving W to figure it out himself. i decide to stick around, wait for W to ask where we are. but he just keeps going, doesn't ask a thing. i think, this guy's got some pride. 

i must've dozed off because next i know, it's morning, W is gone, and Bixby's back. i feel like i've been run over. Bixby asks why i stayed up. i say to help W out. Bixby laughs, calls me all types of fool, asks why i thought W needed help.

i say i was trying to be helpful. he tells me, "on the river, you gotta know it at night like your own front hall." i argue, sure, if i know it's my hall. but if you just drop me in a random hall in the dark, how would i know?

Bixby says, "you just gotta." glad i didn't say anything to W. Bixby says he'd have thrown me out the window and wrecked a hundred bucks worth of stuff. dodged a bullet there. would've been bad news with the owners. they hate careless types.

went out to learn the river's shape today. trickiest thing ever. you focus on a sharp point ahead, try to burn its shape in your mind, and just when you're getting it, poof—it's gone, folds back into the bank. dead tree on the point? disappears into the forest by the time you're next to it. hills don't stick to a shape, they're like butter in the tropics. what looks the same going upstream just morphs on the way back down. told Mr. Bixby about it.

he goes, "that's the point. if shapes didn't change, they'd be useless. take that hill. as long as it's one hill, i'm fine. but when it splits, i know to steer starboard quick. when the V shifts, i swing larboard or risk a snag."

so, had to learn the river's shape in every way possible—upside down, inside out, you name it. gray nights, though, no shape at all. started to get the hang of it, feeling good about myself, until Bixby hits me with—

"how much water in the middle crossing at Hole-in-the-Wall, last trip?"

felt like an ambush. "leadsmen sing through that mess for ages. how'm i supposed to remember?"

"you gotta remember, son. every shoal spot between St. Louis and New Orleans. keep 'em straight."

came to, thinking, "if i can do that, i can raise the dead. i'll retire, grab a slush-bucket. piloting's too much—brains and strength both."

drop it. when i say i'll teach a man the river, i mean it. i'll teach him or kill him trying.

CHAPTER 9

Continued Confusion

no point arguing with someone like that. i pushed my memory hard, trying to remember the shoal waters and all the crossing-marks. but every time i'd learn one tricky thing, another would pop up. i'd see pilots staring at the water like it was a book, but it never told me anything. finally, mr. bixby thought i was ready for a lesson on reading the water.

"see that long slanting line on the water? that's a reef. a bluff reef. there's a solid sand-bar under it, straight like a wall. plenty of water next to it, but not on top. hit it, and it's game over for the boat. see how the line fades at the top? that's a low spot; the head of the reef. cross there, no damage. now follow along close, easy water, not much current."

i followed the reef, nearing the fringed end. then bixby said—

"get ready. wait for my word. she won't want to climb the reef; boats hate shoal water. stand by—wait—WAIT—keep her steady. NOW crank her down! snatch her! snatch her!"

he grabbed the wheel too, spinning it hard down. the boat resisted, then surged to starboard, climbed the reef, sending foam flying from her bows.

"watch her; watch her like a cat, or she'll get away. when she fights and the tiller slips, ease up a bit; that's her way of saying it's too shallow. edge her up slowly to the point. you're on the bar now; there's a bar under every point due to the eddy. see those lines like fan ribs? those are little reefs; miss the ends, but run them close. now watch out—don't crowd that slick spot; not nine feet there; she won't make it. she's sensing it; look sharp! oh hell, there you go! stop the starboard wheel! quick! back her up!"

engine bells rang, steam shot up, but too late. she 'smelt' the bar, foamy ridges gone, a swell rolled forward, she lurched left, tearing away to the other shore, scared out of her wits. a mile off course by the time we got control again.

next day, afternoon watch, bixby asks if i know the next stretch. i said—

go inside the first snag above the point, outside the next one, start out from the lower end of higgins's wood-yard, make a square crossing and—

that's all right. i'll be back before you close up on the next point.

but he wasn't. still below when i rounded it and got into a part of the river i wasn't sure about. didn't know he was hiding behind a chimney watching me. kept going, feeling proud, since he'd never left me in charge this long. even started 'setting' her and letting the wheel go, turned my back, checked stem marks, hummed a tune. trying to act like bixby and other top pilots. looked back a bit too long, and when i turned around, heart jumped up to my throat. if i hadn't clamped my teeth, would've lost it. a bluff reef stretched right across our path. head went blank; didn't know which way was up. spun the wheel so fast it could've woven a web; boat turned, but the reef stuck with her. fled, but it stayed right ahead. crash was coming—where was that guy? if i rang the bell, i might get tossed overboard. better that than wreck the boat. in a panic, rang those bells like crazy, engines started backing and filling, mind lost it—we were heading for the woods on the other side. then mr. bixby shows up on the hurricane deck. relief flooded in. would've felt safe even at niagara with him there. calm as ever, took the toothpick out like it was a cigar—just as we were about to hit a tree, passengers scurrying—and said so gently:

'stop the starboard. stop the larboard. set her back on both.'

the boat hesitated, nosed into the branches, then backed away.

'stop the larboard. come ahead on it. stop the starboard. come ahead on it. point her for the bar.'

sailed away smooth as a summer morning. mr. bixby came in, said with a teasing tone—

'when you have a hail, my boy, tap the big bell three times before landing, so engineers can prep.'

i blushed, said no hail.

'ah! wood, then. officer of the watch will tell you when to wood up.'

i stammered, said i wasn't after wood.

'indeed? what could you want here in the bend, then? ever seen a boat follow a bend upstream at this river stage?'

'no sir, wasn't trying. getting away from a bluff reef.'

'no bluff reef there; none within three miles.'

'but i saw it. bluff as that one yonder.'

'just about. run over it!'

'do you give it as an order?'

'yes. run over it.'

'if i don't, i wish i may die.'

alright, im taking the responsibility. wanted to kill the boat as much as save her before. burned my orders into memory for the inquest and aimed for the reef. held my breath as we went over it, but we slid like oil.

"now see the difference? just a wind reef. wind does that."

"i see. but looks like a bluff reef. how do i tell them apart?"

"can't explain it. it's instinct. one day you'll just know, but never explain how."

turned out true. water's face became a book, unreadable to passengers but clear to me. every mile had something new, never boring, never a page you'd skip. never a book like it by man; so absorbing, always fresh with every look. passenger saw dimples, but to me, they screamed danger—wreck or rock hidden there that'd gut any vessel. simplest marks, yet they shout at a pilot. passengers saw pretty pictures, but for us, it was serious reading.

once i got the hang of the river's language, knew every little feature like the back of my hand, i thought i made a big gain. but i lost something too. all the beauty, the poetry, it disappeared. there's this sunset i remember from when i was new to steamboating. river turned blood red, then gold, with a lone log floating by. water sparkled with slanting marks, and the surface broke with opal-tinted rings. shadows from the woods, a silver trail among them, and a dead tree glowing in the fading sun. everything was bathed in these shifting lights, colors changing every second.

i was spellbound, speechless. never seen anything like it back home. but as time passed, those sights faded for me. if that sunset happened again, i'd just see signs — sun hinting at tomorrow's wind, log showing the river rising, slanting mark warning of a dangerous reef. the boils, the slick water, all signs of shifting channels and shoaling spots. snag in the forest's shadow waiting for a steamboat. that dead tree, once a landmark, about to fall. the river's romance and beauty vanished. now it's just about getting a steamboat through safely.

since then, i get doctors. a beauty's cheek flush, to them, is a sign of something wrong. they see signs of decay in her charms. do they even see beauty, or just a patient? do they wonder if they've gained or lost learning their trade?

if you've been keeping up with what i've written so far, you might be wondering why im going deep into piloting as a science. that's what those chapters were about, and im still not done. i want to show how amazing it is. piloting clear-water rivers is straightforward, but the Mississippi and Missouri are different beasts. their banks shift, snags move, sandbars don't sit still, and you got no lighthouses or buoys to help you out. it's a real challenge. nobody has written about it from a pilot's perspective, so i feel like it's worth taking the space to explain it all.

when i learned every feature of the river, could map it in my mind from St. Louis to New Orleans, and read the water like a newspaper, i thought i was set. i even started acting the part, cap tilted, toothpick in mouth at the wheel. Mr. Bixby noticed. one day he asked—

'what's the height of that bank at Burgess's?'

'can't say, sir. it's three-quarters of a mile off.'

'bad eye—take the glass.'

i used it, and said—'i guess about a foot and a half.'

'foot and a half? that's a six-foot bank. how high was it last trip?'

'don't know; never checked.'

'you must from now on.'

'why?'

'the bank shows the river's stage. more or less water than last trip.'

'leads tell me that.' thought i had him there.

'sure, but if the leads lie? the bank would show it, and you'd know to push those leadsmen. there was a ten-foot bank last trip, now it's six. what's that mean?'

'river's four feet higher than last trip.'

'good. river rising or falling?'

'rising.'

'nope.'

'i think so, sir. there's driftwood floating down.'

a rise gets the driftwood moving, but it floats for a bit even after the river stops rising. the bank will clue you in on this. wait till you hit a spot where the bank shelves a bit. see that narrow band of fine sediment? that's from when the water was higher. driftwood starts beaching too. the bank's got more to tell. see that stump on the false point?

'ay, ay, sir.'

'water's just at its roots. jot that down.'

'why?'

'it means seven feet in chute 103.'

'but 103's a ways up the river.'

'that's where the bank helps. water's in 103 now, might not be when we get there. bank keeps us updated. don't run close chutes on a falling river upstream, and few are allowed downstream. U.S. law says so. river might rise by the time we hit 103, then we run it. how much we drawing?'

'six feet aft, six and a half forward.'

'well, you seem to know a bit.'

'but do i have to keep measuring the banks, 1200 miles, month after month?'

'of course!'

my emotions ran deep. eventually said--

'what about these chutes. are there many?'

'plenty. i doubt we'll run this river as you've seen before. if it rises, we'll go behind bars you've seen dry, cut across low spots unnoticed, through big bars, sneak through cracks that looked like solid land, dart through woods leaving miles off to one side, see the backside of every island between new orleans and cairo.'

'so i need to learn as much river as i already know.'

'about twice as much.'

'well, live and learn. think i was a fool getting into this.'

'yeah, you were. still are. but you won't be once you learn it.'

'ah, never can learn it.'

'i'll make sure you do.'

later, i asked--

'do i need to learn this like the rest of the river, shapes and all, to run it at night?'

'yes. need good marks from one end to the other, help the bank tell you when there's enough water, like that stump. first rise, you can run half a dozen deep ones; next foot, another dozen; another foot, more. so you gotta know banks and marks dead certain, no mix-ups. once you start a crack, no backing out, unlike big river; gotta go through or stay six months if caught on a falling river. around fifty cracks, can't run unless river's brim full.'

'this new lesson sure is a cheerful prospect.'

cheerful enough, sure. but listen up, when you get into those spots, you gotta keep going. too narrow to turn, too crooked to back out, and the shallow water's always at the head. heads fill up slow, so marks you use this season might not work next.

learn new ones every year?

exactly. tighten her up to the bar! why you in the middle of the river?

next few months got wild. same day we had that chat, a big rise hit us. river was packed with dead logs, broken branches, whole trees. steering through that was tricky, even in daylight. nighttime made it worse. big logs would pop up at the last second, no avoiding them. had to stop engines, let one wheel roll over it, shaking up the boat and passengers. sometimes hit one dead center with full steam, felt like hitting a continent. if a log stuck, had to back up and try again. white logs were tough at night, couldn’t see em till too late. black ones easier to spot.

on the rise, timber-rafts, coal barges, trading scows, and broad-horns came down from everywhere. pilots hated those rafts, and the feeling was mutual. law said they needed a light, but that didn’t always happen. suddenly, on a dark night, a light would pop up under our bows, and you’d hear some backwoods voice yellin’—

'whar'n the ---- you goin' to! cain't you see nothin', you dash-dashed aig-suckin', sheep-stealin', one-eyed son of a stuffed monkey!'

as we flew by, furnace light would flash on the scow and the yelling guy, like lightning. our crew would trade insults and junk with em, and one wheel would smash a steering-oar. then darkness again. that flatboatman would head to new orleans, sue us, swearin' he had a light all along, when really, his crew was below, drinking and gambling, no watch topside.

night run went sideways once. slipped into a crevice behind an island, dark like they say, 'inside of a cow'. almost swallowed a whole Posey County family, house and all. lucky for them, they were fiddling below. heard it just in time to veer off, no real harm done, but close enough for a scare. they popped up with a lantern, both sexes, all ages, cussing us till the air turned blue. another time, a coalboatman shot at our pilot-house. borrowed a steering oar in a tight spot, he wasn't thrilled.

CHAPTER 11

The River Rises

during the big rise, small boats were a real pain. running chute after chute, all new to me. tight spots, always a broad-horn waiting. if not in the chute, then hogging the head in shallow water. lots of colorful language exchanged there.

foggy nights, feeling our way, suddenly yells and tin pans clanging. log raft appearing through the fog. no time for niceties. yank the engine bells, max steam, get outta dodge. rocks and rafts aren't friends for a steamboat.

hard to believe, but steamboat clerks lugged religious tracts. twenty times a day, cramping around bars, small boats drifting our way. skiff would break out, fighting the water, shouting for a 'pa-a-per!' clerk tossed New Orleans journals. no complaints? more skiffs swarmed. clerk chucked tracts tied to shingles. the swearing that followed? impressive. twelve hot and tired crews, pulling skiffs two miles for tracts. curses flew like mosquitoes.

river went over its banks, changed everything. old paths gone, now climbing bars that used to stand tall. hugged stumpy shores like at madrid bend, usually avoided those. shot through chutes like 82, wall of trees until we were right there. some chutes were untouched, like no one had been there before. grape-vines, grassy nooks, red blossoms on dead trunks — all overgrown and wild. steering through was a dream, deep except at the head, gentle current, water dead under points. banks were steep, could bury the boat in willow thickets, made you feel like you were flying.

behind other islands, rundown farms, worse cabins. rail fences barely above water, men perched on top chewing tobacco, spitting through gaps in their teeth. families and animals crammed in a flat-boat, waiting for water to drop so they could return to their homes and their chills. river rises twice a year, december and june, forces them out. but when a steamboat passes, they see life again, mouths and eyes wide open.
