once, found a chute blocked by a fallen tree, shows how narrow they can be. passengers got a break in the wilderness while crew chopped it away. no turning back, had to keep going.

from cairo to baton rouge, when the mississippi floods, it's like being in a fenced lane; thick forest walls on both sides, no easy escape. but from baton rouge to new orleans, it's another story. river stretches a mile wide, deep too, up to two hundred feet in places. both banks stripped of timber, just sugar plantations for miles. when the frost threatens, planters rush to harvest, burn bagasse piles, smoking up the place like hell's kitchen.

levees, ten to fifteen feet high, line the banks, set back a bit from the shore. fill the air with smoke from burning bagasse, and steer a steamboat at midnight—good luck. you can't see the levees, just a sea of murky distance. plantations vanish in the smoke, looking like ocean. you're guessing, hoping you're still in the river. but you're probably closer to the bank than you think, maybe six feet when you'd swear it's half a mile. if you hit the embankment, lose your chimneys, at least you saw it coming. once, a vicksburg packet ended up in a plantation and stayed put for a week. nothing new, it's happened before.

thought this chapter was done, but gotta add something that's been bugging me. it's about piloting. there was this top-notch pilot, Mr. X, used to sleepwalk. if he got stressed about tricky river spots, he'd get up and do weird stuff in his sleep. he co-piloted with George Ealer on a big New Orleans passenger boat. first trip, George was jumpy, but X stayed in bed. one night, the boat's nearing Helena, Arkansas. water's low, crossing's a mess. George's thinking about waking X for help when X walks in. now, on pitch-black nights, light messes you up. if you're in a lit room, you can't see squat outside, right? so, pilots don't smoke, no fire in the stove, cover the furnaces, black out the sky-lights. boat's dark. this shadowy figure with X's voice says—

'let me take her, George; been through here more recent, i can handle it.'

'kind of you, im beat. been spinning the wheel like crazy, can't tell which way she's turning.'

Ealer collapses on the bench, worn out. X takes the wheel, steadies the boat with a few turns, smooth like it's midday. Ealer watches, amazed.

'thought i could steer a steamboat, but i was wrong.'

X is quiet, just works. rings for leads, slows steam, guides her carefully into marks. checks positions, stops engines as water shallows, then hits steam to clear the shoal. works through the next set of marks, no touching bottom, into the last tricky bit. boat eases through, finds her marks, drifts till the shallowest part, then full steam over the reef into deep water.

Ealer finally exhales, relieved—

that's some piloting magic right there on the Mississippi. didn't think it could be done till i saw it.

no answer, so he goes—

'hold her steady for five, partner. gonna grab some coffee.'

minutes later, Ealer's munching pie and sipping coffee in the 'texas'. night watchman strolls in, spots Ealer—

'who's at the wheel, sir?'

'X.'

'run to the pilot-house, now!'

they bolt up the steps, three at a time. nobody at the wheel! boat's cruising downriver on her own. watchman bolts out again; Ealer grabs the wheel, reverses the engine hard, holds his breath as the boat barely avoids a 'towhead' that would've sent us to the Gulf of Mexico.

watchman comes back—

'didn't that fool say he was asleep when he got here?'

'nope.'

'well, he was. found him walking the rails like it was a sidewalk; put him to bed. but there he was again, doing his tightrope act.'

'i'll stick around next time. but hope he has more of these fits. you should've seen him handle the boat at Helena crossing. never saw such fancy work. if he can do that asleep, imagine what he'd do dead!'

CHAPTER 12

Sounding

when the river's low, and the boat's drawing more water than there is, you gotta be careful. we had to 'sound' tricky spots almost every trip when water levels dropped.

here's how sounding works. boat ties up above a shallow crossing; off-duty pilot takes his 'cub' or steersman and a crew, heads out in the yawl—unless the boat's got a fancy 'sounding-boat'—and searches for the deepest water. pilot on duty watches with a spyglass, maybe signals with the whistle—'try higher' or 'try lower'; distance gives a better view of the water's surface, like an oil painting. whistle signals are rare, though, unless wind messes up the water's ripples. once at the shoal, they slow down, pilot uses a ten to twelve-foot pole to sound the depth, steersman follows orders—'hold her starboard', 'let her fall to larboard', or 'steady as you go.'

when the measurements show the yawl's near the shallowest part of the reef, it's 'ease all!' time. men stop rowing, yawl drifts with the current. order comes next, 'stand by with the buoy!' the second they hit the shallowest point, pilot yells, 'let go the buoy!' — over it goes. if the pilot ain't satisfied, he checks again; finds better water up or down, moves the buoy there. once satisfied, he calls it, and the men raise their oars straight up. a blast from the boat's whistle shows the signal's seen. then it's 'give way' on the oars, yawl sidles up to the buoy. steamer comes down slow, aimed right at the buoy, saving its power. then, at that moment, cranks all its steam, grinds over the buoy and sand, hitting the deeper water. or not — maybe it 'strikes and swings.' then it's waiting, hours or days, sparring itself off.

sometimes, they skip the buoy, yawl scouts for the best water, steamer follows in its wake. sounding can be a lot of fun and thrill, especially on a sunny day or wild night. winter's cold and danger strip the fun.

a buoy's just a board, four or five feet long, one end turned up; like a flipped school bench. anchored on the shallowest reef spot with a rope and heavy stone. without the turned-up end, the current would drag it under. at night, a paper lantern with a candle sits on top, a tiny glimmer in the darkness, seen a mile or more.

nothing excites a cub like going out sounding. it's adventure; sometimes danger; feels man-of-war-like steering a fast yawl; the boat's spring when seasoned sailors row with all they've got; white foam trailing from the bow; water's rush is music; summer's breezy river is exhilarating. grandeur for a cub is giving orders; pilot might say, 'let her go about!' and the cub commands, 'ease starboard! strong on the larboard! starboard give way! with a will, men!' cub loves sounding, knowing passengers watch every yawl move by day, and at night, eyes follow the yawl's lantern fading into the distance.

one trip, this girl, maybe sixteen, spent all her time in our pilot-house with her uncle and aunt. i fell for her. tom g----, thornburg's cub, did too. we were tight till then, but this cooled things. i told her my river tales, made myself a hero. tom tried too, but he embroidered a lot. i was a bit ahead because virtue pays off. pilots decided to sound the crossing at the head of 21—it'd be thornburg's watch. my chief would do the sounding. we had this perfect sounding-boat, sleek, fast. twelve oarsmen, a mate for orders—lot of 'style' on our steamer.

we tied up above 21, got set. foul night, wide river, couldn't see the other shore in that gloom. passengers were engaged, everything set. rushing through the engine-room, dressed for a storm, i ran into tom. couldn't resist a jab: "aint you glad you dont have to go out sounding?"

tom spun around, snapped, "now just for that, you can go get the sounding-pole yourself. i was going, but i'd see you in halifax first."

"who wants you to get it? i dont. it's in the sounding-boat."

"it ain't. it's been new-painted, drying on the ladies' cabin guards."

i dashed back. got there just as they yelled, "give way, men!" looked over—tom was at the tiller with my chief, and the pole, i'd been sent on a wild goose chase for. that girl said, "awful to go out in that little boat tonight! is it dangerous?"

felt like a stab. went to help in the pilot-house. boat's lantern vanished, then a spark a mile off. thornburg whistled, backed us out, headed for it. sped up, then eased toward the spark. suddenly, thornburg said, "hello, the buoy-lantern's out!" stopped the engines. then, "there it is again!" came ahead on engines, rang for leads. water shoaled, then deepened. thornburg muttered, "buoy drifted off the reef, seems left. safest to run over it."

creeping down on the light, just before our bows hit, thornburg rang the bell, "my soul, it's the sounding-boat!" wild alarms below, a pause, then grinding and crashing. thornburg exclaimed—

there! the paddle-wheel turned that sounding-boat into matchsticks! run! check who got hurt!

i was on the main deck in a flash. my chief, the third mate, and most of the crew were safe. they saw the danger late, but knew what to do. at my chief's order, they jumped, grabbed the guard, and got hauled onboard. next thing, the yawl got smashed to bits. two men and cub tom were missing, and word got around fast. passengers rushed forward, worried and whispering about the disaster. "poor fellows! poor boy!" kept echoing around.

by then, the boat's yawl was out searching for them. then a faint call from the left. the yawl went the other way. half the folks yelled to the swimmer; the rest shouted for the yawl to turn. swimmer's getting closer, but maybe getting weak. everyone leaned over the rail, peering into the dark. every cry pulled out more "poor fellow, poor fellow!"

but the cries kept coming, getting closer, then: "i can make it! rope ready!"

they cheered him on! the chief mate stood ready, rope in hand, torch lit. swimmer's face hit the light, and they pulled him aboard, soaked and limp, cheers everywhere. it was tom.

the yawl found nothing of the other two. likely missed the guard, hit the wheel, gone. tom didn't even go for the guard, just dove under the wheel. no big deal; i could've done it easy. said so, but they still made a fuss over that fool. that girl wouldn't leave him alone the rest of the trip. whatever. couldn't stand her anyway.

we messed up, thinking the sounding-boat's lantern was the buoy light. chief said he set the buoy, watched it, then moved a bit downstream. got talking, looked up, buoy gone, thought the steamer hit it. kept talking, steamer got close, figured it was normal. then realized they mistook his lantern for the buoy light. yelled "jump for the guard!" just in time.

let's dig into what piloting really demands. it's about memory—a pilot has to perfect it. not just "i think" but "i know." imagine knowing every detail of 1200 miles of river, like knowing every house and sign on a long street in New York, even in pitch black. and then, half those signs move around monthly, but you still have to nail their new spots without a hitch. that's a pilot's life on the Mississippi.

now, think about it: a pilot's memory is something else. knowing every twist and turn, like someone reciting the Bible backward without a single slip. sounds wild, but for pilots, that's just another day on the job.

crazy how a pilot's memory works, huh? it's like automatic storage. take this: the leadsman keeps calling 'half twain, half twain' over and over, just like a clock ticking. the pilot's chatting away, not even paying attention. then suddenly, 'quarter twain!' slips in there, no emphasis, just part of the background noise. weeks later, ask that pilot where they were when they heard it, and bam, they can paint you a picture. they remember the exact spot on the river, all the landmarks. it's like they snapped a photo without even thinking about it. but try the same with a walk and some random sounds, and nothing sticks unless your brain's been trained for it.

give someone a decent memory and stick them in piloting, and it grows like crazy. but only for what they're drilled on daily. they'll remember every landmark and depth, but ask them what they ate for breakfast? probably not a clue. just shows what focused training can do.

back when river wages spiked, my boss, mr. bixby, learned over a thousand miles of the missouri river like it was nothing. saw each part once in the day, once at night, then got his 'daylight' license. few more trips, and he was licensed full-time, piloting day and night, top of his game.

he had me with a pilot, mr. brown, whose memory was something else. seemed like he was born with it. someone mentions a name, and he's off: 'oh, i knew him. sallow face, red hair, little scar on his throat. southern trade for six months, thirteen years ago. took a trip with him. five feet in the upper river, “henry blake” grounded at tower island, “george elliott” busted her rudder on the “sunflower” wreck.' and on he'd go, like a walking history book.

knew when she went under. three years before, december 2nd. asa hardy was captain, his brother john first clerk, first trip for him too. tom jones filled me in a week later in new orleans. he was first mate on the “sunflower.” captain hardy got a nail in his foot july 6th the next year, died of lockjaw on the 15th. his brother passed two years later, march 3rd, erysipelas. never met the hardys—alleghany river guys—but folks who did told me. said captain hardy wore yarn socks year-round, first wife was jane shook from new england, second wife died in a lunatic asylum. ran in the family. she was horton from lexington, kentucky.

this guy couldn’t forget a thing. trivial details, memorable events, all stuck in his head the same. not just a pilot's memory, universal grasp. he'd recall a letter from seven years ago, verbatim. then detour into the writer’s life story and their family history. lucky if he didn’t dive into their ancestors too.

memory like that’s a curse. everything’s the same size in his mind. can't tell interesting from dull. his stories bogged down by useless details. can't stay on track. every memory a distraction. starts with a funny dog story, can't get to the punchline. laughs himself silly, but then it's the dog's breed, owner's family, their weddings and funerals, poems, the hard winter, folks freezing, pork prices, corn and fodder, cows and horses, circus, then the menagerie, elephants, africa, religion. three hours later, watch changes, and he’s quoting sermons. after all that, still don’t know a thing about the dog.

a pilot needs a good memory, but two things matter even more. quick judgment and cool courage that doesn't flinch. give a man a touch of bravery and by the time he's a pilot, no steamboat trouble will faze him. but judgment? that's brains. gotta start with a good supply or you'll never make it.

courage builds steady in the pilot-house, but it doesn't hit its stride until a young pilot's been on his own for a while, with all that weight on his shoulders. once an apprentice knows the river, he might think he's fearless, but as soon as the pilot steps out, he realizes it was borrowed courage. suddenly the river's full of surprises, and he's not ready. all his training flies out the window, and he’s white as a sheet, scared stiff. so pilots have tricks to teach these young guys to face danger calmly. they like to play a little con on them.

mr. bixby got me with one of those once. for years, i'd blush just thinking about it. i was a solid steersman, handling most of the work on our watch, day and night. mr. bixby hardly gave me directions; just took the wheel on rough nights or tricky crossings, landed the boat, and let me do the rest. the lower river was full, and i was sure i could handle any crossing from cairo to new orleans without a hitch. being afraid of any crossing in daylight seemed ridiculous. so there we were, on a perfect summer day, cruising down the bend above island 66. i was cocky, thinking i knew it all, when mr. bixby said—

'going below for a bit. you know the next crossing?'

felt like a slap. simplest crossing on the river. you couldn’t mess it up if you tried, and depth was never an issue. i knew it cold.

'know it? i could run it blindfolded.'

'how much water’s in it?'

'strange question. couldn't hit bottom there with a church steeple.'

'you think so, huh?'

the question hit me like a ton of bricks. mr. bixby was waiting for that. he just left, no more words. my mind started racing with all sorts of scenarios. turns out, he secretly sent someone down to the forecastle with some shady instructions for the leadsmen, and another messenger to stir up the officers. then he hid behind a smoke-stack, watching it all unfold. soon enough, the captain stepped on the hurricane deck, then the chief mate, then a clerk. one by one, more folks gathered, and before i knew it, there were about twenty people right under my nose. i was baffled. as i moved across, the captain looked up at me and said, pretending to be worried—

"where's mr. bixby?"

"gone below, sir."

that was it for me. my mind went wild, conjuring dangers out of thin air, multiplying faster than i could keep track. suddenly, i imagined shoal water ahead! the wave of panic nearly tore me apart. all my confidence in the crossing just vanished. i grabbed the bell-rope, let it go, grabbed it again, let it go, then finally pulled it so weakly, i barely heard it myself. captain and mate shouted out together—

"starboard lead there! and quick about it!"

another jolt. i was all over the wheel like a squirrel, trying to steer to port, but then i'd see new dangers and swing to starboard, only to find more trouble there. chaos. then the leadsman hollered—

"d-e-e-p four!"

deep four in a spot that’s supposed to be bottomless! it was terrifying.

"m-a-r-k three!... m-a-r-k three... quarter less three!... half twain!"

this was bad. i yanked the bell-ropes to stop the engines.

"quarter twain! quarter twain! _mark _twain!"

i was lost. shaking head to toe, eyes bugged out. 

"quarter _less _twain! nine and a _half_!"

we were _drawing _nine! my hands were useless. i couldn’t ring a bell to save my life. dashed to the speaking-tube, yelled at the engineer—

"oh, ben, if you love me, _back _her! quick, ben! oh, back the immortal _soul_ out of her!"

then i heard the door close gently. turned around, and there was mr. bixby, smiling. the crowd on deck erupted in laughter. i got it now, and felt like the lowest of the low. got back in rhythm, set the boat right, and said—

"fine trick to play on an orphan, huh? guess i'll never live down heaving the lead at the head of 66."

"well, no, you probably won't. and i hope you don’t. you should learn from that. didn't you _know _there was no bottom in that crossing?"

"yes, sir, i did."

"then don't let anyone shake your confidence in what you know. remember that. and when you're in danger, don't panic. that won’t help."

lesson learned, but hard. and for months, i had to hear that line i grew to hate: "oh, ben, if you love me, back her!"

i've tried to dig deep into piloting, showing what it's really about. it's a curious science, no doubt, and deserves attention. i loved it more than any job since, and the pride in it was huge. back then, a pilot was truly free, unlike kings tied down by politics or editors with hands tied by patrons. everyone had a master, but the mississippi pilot was his own boss.

once the boat hit the river, control was all the pilot's. he could run it how and where he wanted. no commands, no suggestions. u.s. law backed him, knowing he knew best how to handle that boat. it was genuine power, not just for show. seen kids barely 18 steering into danger like it was nothing, with captains just watching, unable to step in. to interfere would've set a bad precedent. pilots held serious sway in those steamboat days, respected by captains, officers, and even passengers. they didn't even flinch around foreign princes. when you're in charge like that, nothing fazes you.

pilots got used to giving orders instead of making requests. it still bugs me to make a request when i could just issue a command. back then, running a steamboat from St. Louis to New Orleans and back took about 25 days. 7 or 8 days were spent at the wharves, everyone working hard except the pilots, who were off enjoying town life but still getting paid their full wages. the moment the boat docked, they were gone till the last bell rang for the next trip.

a captain valued a top-notch pilot and would keep him even when the river was frozen, paying $400 a month, which was a fortune then. pilots were respected, especially those from the Missouri River who made $900 a trip. here's how a chat went back then: some guy from the Illinois River approaches two fancy Missouri pilots.

'got a decent trip for the upcountry, need you for a month. what's the price?'

'eighteen hundred each.'

'jeez! take my boat, pay me your wages, and we can split!'

steamboatmen were big shots, especially if they worked on boats like the 'Aleck Scott' or 'Grand Turk.' even the crew—firemen, deck hands, barbers—knew their status. once at a New Orleans ball, a deckhand on the 'Aleck Scott' got called out for showing off.

'who _is_ you, anyway?'

he puffed up, confident, 'i fire the middle door on the “Aleck Scott!”'

that settled it.

the barber on the 'Grand Turk' was this sharp young guy, a black dude with a real sense of his own importance. everyone around him kind of gravitated to him. now, if you know new orleans, you know the young folks there love to flirt around sunset, especially on those backstreet sidewalks. one evening, someone caught this scene: a middle-aged black woman popped her head through a busted window and shouted, making sure the neighbors heard, 'hey Mary Ann, get in the house right now! you're out there messing with that low crowd and here's the barber from the “Gran' Turk” wanting to chat with you!'

talking about pilots and their untouchable status reminds me of Stephen W----. he was a top-notch pilot, full of talk, wit, and humor. didn’t care a lick about rank or wealth, just super relaxed. always had a gig, never saved a dime, always borrowing money, owing every pilot and most captains on the river. he had this way of turning reckless piloting into something almost magical—though not everyone saw it that way. he did a trip with old Captain Y---- and got 'relieved' when they hit New Orleans. someone asked why, and Captain Y---- shuddered just thinking about Stephen. 

'man, i wouldn't have that wild guy on my boat for anything. he swears, sings, whistles, yells—you name it. never seen anyone yell like him, even in the dead of night. i'd barely fall asleep before he’d scare me awake with those crazy yells. strange guy, no respect for anything. called me “Johnny” sometimes. had a fiddle and a cat, played horribly, and the cat would howl along. nobody could sleep with him around. and reckless, yeah. believe it or not, he took my boat through the snags at Chicot full steam ahead with the wind howling. my officers saw it. and get this—while tearing through those snags, with me shaking and praying, he starts _whistling_ “Buffalo gals.” just as calm as if we were at a funeral. when i spoke up, he just smiled, told me to behave, and not mess with my superiors!’

once there was this grumpy captain who snagged stephen in new orleans when he was broke and jobless. captain hounded him till stephen agreed to work for half wages, 125 bucks a month. captain promised to keep it quiet, not to shame stephen. but not even a day out of new orleans, and captain's bragging to everyone. stephen took it in stride, didn’t say a word. 

afternoon, captain's on the hurricane deck, looking puzzled, eyes darting to stephen who's just whistling and handling business. captain's itching to say something, but river rules kept his mouth shut. he wandered around, puzzled, then ducked back inside. but he came out again, more confused. finally, he tried small talk with respect—

'pretty good river stage, huh?'

'yeah, bank-full sure is.'

'lotta current here.'

'lotta current? it's like a mill-race.'

'easier by the shore than here, right?'

'yeah, but safer here; no bottom to hit.'

captain left, looking glum. at this rate, his boat'd reach st. louis by his old age. next day, stephen's still in the river's middle, whistling, while a slower boat's gaining by the shore. captain had to speak—

'mr. w----, that chute cuts distance, right?'

'think so, not sure.'

'not sure? enough water to pass?'

'probably, can't say for certain.'

'well, those pilots are trying it. you don’t know as much as them?'

'them? they're two-fifty-dollar pilots! but don’t worry, i know what i can for a hundred twenty-five!'

captain gave up. five minutes later, stephen's cruising through the chute, showing the other boat a two-fifty-dollar pair of heels.

chapter 15

the pilots' monopoly

another time, on the 'aleck scott,' mr. bixby's threading a tight spot at cat island, leads out, all tense. captain, nervous guy, finally yells from the hurricane deck—

'give her steam, mr. bixby! she won't clear the reef!'

bixby didn't flinch. when the danger passed, he unleashed a furious tirade on the captain, best cursing session i've ever heard. no fight broke out, but only 'cause the captain knew he was wrong. usually, he'd not take that quietly.

so here's the deal on steamboat pilots back then. they had this crazy tight organization going, the Pilots' Benevolent Association. it was maybe the strongest commercial group ever. pilots used to get $250 a month, but then more boats showed up and business boomed, weirdly the pay started dropping. too many pilots getting made. every captain had a son or nephew they wanted to bring up, so suddenly everyone had a steersman doing the grunt work while they chilled. problem was, this flooded the market with pilots, and wages tanked.

realized too late they messed up. needed a tight group to turn it around. seemed impossible, and risky to even try. but a dozen bold pilots gave it a shot, got a special charter, and called it the Pilots' Benevolent Association. they set wages back to $250, but got fired for it. thing is, their by-laws had some sneaky benefits. idle members got $25 a month. better than nothing, right? initiation fee was $12, no dues if unemployed. so new pilots started joining up.

they also took care of widows and buried members at their expense. this brought out all the old pilots from the woodwork. they'd roll in any way they could, pay the $12, and start collecting. it was a smart move that pulled the whole community together.

So here’s how it went down. The association scooped up all the pilots nobody wanted, plus a few good ones, but most of the top pilots stayed out and laughed at them. People joked about the rule where members had to pay 10% of their wages to the association even though no one would hire them. The association basically helped by taking the bad pilots off the market, leaving the good ones, and driving wages up from $100 to $125, even $150. Folks loved pointing out that this was thanks to a group that got no benefit themselves. They even joked to the members about taking them on trips so they could see the river again. But the association didn't seem to mind. Sometimes they grabbed a pilot who was down on his luck, and these were usually skilled. As business picked up, wages went to $250, but still, no one in the association got hired. The jokes just kept coming.

Then winter hit, business boomed, and suddenly pilots were scarce. Time for a little payback. Captains had to swallow their pride and go after association pilots. Captain ----, who mocked them the most, went first. He found a top association pilot and said he was ready to hire him. But the pilot wouldn't budge because the other pilot on board wasn't part of the association.

‘What? You’re saying you won’t work with one of the best pilots because he’s not in your group?’

‘Exactly.’

‘This is a twist. I thought I was doing you a favor, but looks like I’m the one needing help. Is this some association rule?’

‘Yep.’

‘Show me.’

They headed to the association rooms, and the secretary backed up the rule. Captain asked, ‘What now? I’ve already got Mr. S---- for the whole season.’

secretary says, "i'll set you up with a pilot, he'll be on board by noon." captain's worried about firing S---- and the wages he'll owe. secretary shrugs, "your problem, not ours." captain's mad but has to let S---- go, paying him a thousand bucks, and takes an association pilot instead. tables turn, more captains bite the bullet, swap their guys for association men, grumbling about revenge once business slows.

boats with two non-association pilots hold out longest, but association's got rules. no sharing river info with outsiders. half the boats have association pilots, half don't. looks like a stalemate, but not quite. at towns along the river, wharf-boats replace piers. association locks info in special boxes, same lock as U.S. mail. association pilots have keys. if a stranger asks about the river, they flash the key. no key back? no answer.

each member gets fancy blanks from the secretary, like billheads. they fill them with river details as they go. for example, leaving St. Louis, they note crossings, depths, landmarks: "nine and a half feet, stern on court-house," and remarks like "avoid the new snag."

when they hit Cairo, the pilot drops off his filled blank, picks up fresh reports from incoming boats, learns the river between Cairo and Memphis. can't mess up unless they're trying.

imagine trying to navigate a river over a thousand miles long with a channel that shifts daily. pilots used to spot a shoal just once or twice a month, but now they've got a hundred eyes watching for them and a bunch of brains figuring out the best path. info was never more than a day old. if a pilot had doubts about a crossing, he'd blow a special steam-whistle. if another boat's pilots were in the association, they'd answer back, and the two steamers would line up to swap fresh details.

first thing a pilot did in new orleans or st. louis was drop his detailed report at the association's headquarters before heading home. there was always a crowd in those meeting spots, talking about the river's changes. when someone new came in, all chatter stopped until the latest updates were shared. pilots couldnt afford to talk about anything but their work. being perfect one day and lost the next wasn't an option. staying updated was mandatory.

outsiders had it rough. no set place to gather info, no reliable reports, just luck and old news. they might run 500 miles on week-old info. that worked when the river was high, but in low water, it was a disaster.

this led to predictable chaos. outsiders started grounding boats, sinking them, running into trouble, while association guys had smooth sailing. soon, even independent boat owners felt the pressure. they kept up appearances until the day came when they were told to ditch their outsiders for association pilots. the order didn't come from the association, but from the underwriters, who saw the clear safety advantage of the association's reporting system. this wasn't collusion, just smart business. every outsider found himself on the dock with his trunk, no question about it.

the outsiders were making a fuss, but there was only one way forward for them. they had to pay up, plain and simple. they came around with their twelve bucks, wanting in, but found out quick things had changed. initiation fee was now fifty bucks, plus ten percent of all wages since the association started. that meant a few hundred dollars for most. even then, a single "no" and you're out. every member had to vote in person. took forever since pilots were often away. still, they scraped by, joining one by one.

eventually, only ten holdouts remained. they swore they wouldn't join, but work dried up. the association announced wages were jumping to five hundred a month. red river was at seven hundred. that got the holdouts moving. they applied, but a new rule hit them — dues on what they would've earned if they hadn't sulked. electing them was tough, but it happened. one guy had to cough up six hundred and twenty-five dollars.

the association was strong now, cash in the bank. no outsiders left. new rule: no new apprentices for five years. after that, a few would be taken by the association under strict terms. they needed to be eighteen, from a good family, pass an exam, pay a thousand upfront, and stick around until most members backed their pilot's license.

existing apprentices were taken from their masters, assigned by the association. they moved around as needed. if a pilot was sick, a cub would help him out.

the widow and orphan list grew, but so did the funds. they handled funerals with ceremony, even sent members to find bodies lost in accidents, sometimes costing a grand.

they got a charter and jumped into insurance, covering members' lives and even taking on steamboat risks.

that monopoly felt like it could never be broken. tightest grip around. by US law, you needed two licensed pilots to sign off on any new pilot, and only the association had those signatures. so, no new pilots. every year a few would pass on or get too old, and no one new to step up. they could jack wages as high as they wanted, just as long as they didn’t poke the bear and get the government to change the rules, owners had no choice but to deal.

the only ones standing in the way of total control were the owners and captains. and guess what, they took themselves out of the game. when the association said wages would jump to 500 bucks a month by september 1861, captains and owners bumped up freight costs a bit and told the farmers it was because of the new wages. farmers bought it, didn’t see through the thin argument. the extra freight cost on a big load was way more than needed for the wages.

so owners and captains formed their own crew, wanting to hike captains' wages to 500 too. figured they’d push freight even higher. they also made rules: hire a non-association pilot, pay a $500 fine. a few fines got paid before they got their act together. tried to get pilots to agree not to work for non-association captains, but pilots saw they were covered either way and stayed out of it.

the pilots' association seemed unbeatable. but things changed. new railroads up through Mississippi, Tennessee, and Kentucky started pulling passengers off steamboats. then the war hit, nearly killed off steamboating for years, pilots left hanging with the cost of living climbing. then, the St. Louis association’s treasurer vanished with all the funds. and after the war? railroads were everywhere. steamboats had little left but freight. then someone brought in tugboats to tow loads, making steamboat piloting obsolete overnight. that once mighty association, and the art of piloting, just faded into history.

boats would leave new orleans between four and five in the afternoon. from three onward, they’d start burning rosin and pitch pine. you’d see this long line of black smoke columns stretching two or three miles, covering the city like a dark ceiling. flags flying on every boat, mates shouting orders louder than usual. barrels and boxes spinning across the levee, passengers dodging and weaving, trying to board in one piece. women juggling babies and luggage, losing it in the chaos. drays and baggage-vans clattering everywhere, getting stuck and cursing up a storm. windlasses whirring like crazy, dropping freight below, crews singing ‘de las’ sack!’ like it was the anthem of the chaos.

by now, the hurricane and boiler decks packed with passengers. last bells start clanging up and down the line, powwow doubles. then the gongs hit, ‘all dat ain’t goin’, please to git asho’!’—powwow quadruples. folks swarm ashore, bumping into those trying to get on. stage-planks haul in with folks clinging on like their life depends on it, last-minute jumpers springing for the shore.

boats slide back into the stream, gaps in the line. citizens crowd on boats not leaving to watch. one by one, steamers straighten, gather steam, swing by with flags flying, smoke rolling, crews singing their hearts out. cannons boom, spectators cheer, and the procession wings up the river.

back in the day, races between fast boats happened with crowds watching. night races were something else, torch-baskets glowing on the forecastle. folks thought racing was dangerous, but it wasn’t, not after they set steam limits. engineers were sharp during races, always checking gauges. danger was on slow boats, where engineers got lazy, let chips block water to the boilers.

Back in the heyday of steamboating, races between fast steamers were a big deal. Dates got set weeks ahead, and the whole Mississippi Valley buzzed with excitement. Nobody cared about politics or weather, just the upcoming race. As it got closer, the steamers stripped down. Anything adding weight or drag came off. Spars, derricks—gone. Boats even risked it without gear to get unstuck if they ran aground. When the 'Eclipse' and 'A. L. Shotwell' had their big race, folks said they scraped off the 'Eclipse's' chimney gilding, and the captain ditched his kid gloves and shaved his head. Always doubted that part.

Boats ran best when loaded just right. Five and a half feet forward, five feet aft. Not a pound extra, not even a homeopathic pill. Hardly any passengers either—too much weight and they never stay put. They crowd the sides to see stuff, while a real steamboat man sticks center, hair parted with a spirit level.

No stopping for small towns, no way-freights or way-passengers. Just 'touch and go' at big cities. Pre-arranged coal and wood flats ready to latch on quick. Double crews for speed.

Race day, everything's set. Steamers back into the stream, watching each other like hawks. Flags droop, steam shrieks, black smoke rolls. Crowds everywhere—on shores, rooftops, boats, ships. Twelve hundred miles of people from New Orleans to St. Louis ready to cheer them on.

Then steam blasts out, guns fire, red-shirted guys wave flags, and they're off. Bands blare Hail Columbia, and cheers thunder as they fly past.

They won't stop between New Orleans and St. Louis, only a quick pause at big towns or to hitch wood-boats. If you blink during one of those, you'll miss the action.

Two steamers with good matches can keep each other in sight day after day, maybe even side by side. But pilots aren't the same, and the sharpest ones win the race. If one boat's got a 'lightning' pilot and the other's just a shade slower, you'll know who's on watch by whether that boat's gaining or losing ground every four hours. A clever pilot can slow a boat down if he doesn't steer like a pro. Steering right is an art. Keep the rudder from dragging, and you'll move up the river fast.

Boats themselves differ a lot. I was on one so slow we forgot what year we left port. Rare, but it happened. Ferryboats lost trips because folks aged waiting for us to pass. I've got docs but misplaced them. The 'John J. Roe' was so slow, it sank at Madrid Bend, and owners didn't hear for five years. Confusing, but true. Still, we had fun racing islands and rafts. Once, we got to St. Louis in sixteen days. Even at that speed, we changed watches three times in a five-mile reach. A 'reach' is a straight river stretch where the current's lively.

We made it to Grand Gulf from New Orleans in four days (340 miles); 'Eclipse' and 'Shotwell' did it in one. Nine days for the chute of 63 (700 miles); they did it in two. A while back, 'J. M. White' went from New Orleans to Cairo in three days, six hours, forty-four minutes. In 1853, 'Eclipse' made it in three days, three hours, twenty minutes. Some say add an hour and 16 minutes to that. In 1870, 'R. E. Lee' did it in three days, one hour. Fastest trip on record, but I'll argue it wasn't. Here's why: when 'J. M. White' ran, the distance was about 1106 miles, averaging just over 14 mph. By 'Eclipse's' time, it was 1080 miles, averaging under 14 and 3/8 mph. In 'R. E. Lee's' day, it was about 1030 miles, averaging about 14 and 1/8 mph. So, 'Eclipse' was the real speed champ.

Chapter 17: Cut-offs and Stephen

These details matter for one thing. They let me introduce one of the Mississippi's quirks: it shortens itself over time. Toss a long apple peel over your shoulder, and it'll look like a bit of the Mississippi—900 to 1000 miles from Cairo, Illinois, to New Orleans. It's all winding, with a straight section here and there. The stretch from Cairo to St. Louis is less twisty since the rocky land keeps the river in check.

the mississippi's got a mind of its own, carving out these crazy horseshoe bends. so much so, if you land on one end and walk half a mile across the neck, you could sit there and wait a couple hours while your boat takes the long way around. folks used to take advantage of that. some guy with a less valuable plantation inland would cut a ditch at night. river finds that ditch and bam, his land’s now prime riverfront, and the other guy’s land is stuck on a useless island. they keep watch on those necks now. catch someone ditching and he might not get another chance.

look at some history on this. there was a neck near port hudson, louisiana, half a mile across. walk it in fifteen minutes, but raft around it? that’s thirty-five miles. river blasted through it in 1722, shaved off those miles. same at black hawk point in 1699, took off twenty-five miles. raccourci cut-off, forty or fifty years ago, dropped another twenty-eight miles. from the southernmost to the northernmost cut-off, it’s seventy miles now. back then, 158 miles. that's eighty-eight miles gone in just that stretch.

in my time, we've had cut-offs at hurricane island, island 100, napoleon, arkansas, walnut bend, council bend. together, those clipped sixty-seven miles. american bend cut-off, took off ten miles or more.

so, the mississippi from cairo to new orleans was 1,215 miles long 176 years ago. after 1722, it was 1,180. then, after american bend, 1,040. lost sixty-seven more since. now it’s down to 973 miles.

now, if i wanted to be all scientific and prove past events by recent changes, or predict the future from past trends, this would be the moment. geology's never seen data like this. glacial epochs? vague. vague. but look here:

Lower Mississippi’s been busy. Over 176 years, it’s cut itself down by 242 miles. That’s about a mile and a third each year. So, if you do the math, back in the Old Oolitic Silurian Period, just a million years ago, that river stretched over a million three hundred thousand miles, poking out into the Gulf like a fishing rod. Fast forward 742 years, and it'll be just a mile and three-quarters long. Cairo and New Orleans might as well share a mayor by then. Science is wild like that — small facts, big guesses.

Now, when water starts flowing through one of those ditches—time to move. It slices the banks clean. Once it hits twelve to fifteen feet wide, it’s game over. Can’t stop it. When it hits a hundred yards, banks just peel off in half-acre chunks. Current used to be five miles an hour, but that short distance cranks it up. I was on the first boat trying the American Bend cut-off. Didn’t make it. Wild night — thunder, lightning, rain. Current was fifteen to twenty miles an hour; our boat couldn’t manage more than twelve or thirteen. Maybe foolish to try, but Mr. Brown was ambitious. We’d speed up the shore, hit the current, and spin like a top. Water flooded the forecastle, boat leaned hard, and we were off downriver, dodging trees.

We tried four times. I watched from the forecastle companionway. Every time the current hit, we spun and shook like we’d hit a sandbank. Lightning lit up cabins and acres falling into the river — sounded like thunder. Once, we spun and missed a house by twenty feet; it went overboard seconds later. Couldn’t stand on the forecastle; water washed over it. After the fourth try, we ended up in the woods, two miles downriver. Everything flooded. Couple days later, cut-off was three-quarters of a mile wide. Boats got through and saved ten miles.

old Raccourci cut-off shaved twenty-eight miles off the river. Used to be a tale tied to it. Story goes a boat floated through one night, didn't know the cut-off existed, and stuck to the massive bend. Dark, messed-up night, shapes twisted in the mist. The old bend was filling up, and they kept dodging shadows, sometimes smacking into one. Pilots got lost and swore they'd never leave. And like magic, they didn't. To this day, folks say that ghost ship still roams that empty river, lights glowing faint in the fog, engine hissing, crew calling out into the night. More than one watchman swears on it.

no new stats, so let's close with a story about 'Stephen.'

Stephen owed most captains and pilots, notes for borrowed cash starting at $250. Never paid back, but always renewed each year.

Eventually, Stephen couldn't squeeze more from his old pals. So he hunted fresh faces like young Yates (fake name, but starts with Y). Yates got his pilot gig, earned $250, and bam, Stephen was there. Smooth talker, Stephen snagged Yates's cash fast. News hit the pilot circle, and the old creditors got a kick out of it. Yates didn't catch on that Stephen's promise was empty. He came for his money, got sweet-talked, and left empty-handed again. This kept up. Yates kept chasing Stephen, with no luck, until he quit. Then Stephen flipped the script. Wherever Yates went, Stephen popped up, full of charm and excuses. Eventually, Yates would run when he saw him coming, dragging anyone with him. But Stephen always caught up, breathless, arms wide, shaking Yates's hand loose, and...

what a chase. i saw you didn’t spot me, so i cranked it up to make sure i caught you. now look at you, just the same as ever. [to Yates's friend:] check him out! isn’t it something? some say he’s a picture; i say he’s a whole panorama. anyway, i wish i’d caught you an hour ago. i had that $250 ready for you, been looking everywhere. waited at the Planter’s all night, no sleep, no food. wife was asking where i’d been. told her it’s this debt, weighs on me. she told me to rest, but i wouldn’t till you got your money. first thing in the morning, i hear you shipped off to New Orleans. just broke me. leaned against a building, crying. owner came out, didn’t want me crying there. felt like the world was against me. met Jim Wilson, paid him instead. and now here you are, and i’m broke. but i’ll find that money and pay you by noon tomorrow, promise. now, let me take one last look at you.

and man, Yates couldn’t catch a break. couldn’t dodge this guy or his woes. avoided the street, fearing he’d run into Stephen again.

Bogart’s billiard saloon was the spot for pilots back then. they’d come for river news as much as the game. Yates was there one morning, and Stephen hid away. but soon enough, Stephen burst in like Yates was a lost brother.

oh man, glad to see you all. listen, i owe each of you money, about forty thousand total. i wanna pay it all back, every cent. you know it's been rough for me being in debt to such patient friends. but the hardest part is owing this young man here. came today just to say i've got a plan to pay it all off. and i wanted him to hear it first. found the way, my friend. gonna pay in alphabetical order!

then he left, and it took a couple of minutes for folks to get what stephen meant. yates just sighed, “well, the Y's are outta luck. he wont get past the C's in this life, and in the next, i'll still be 'that poor, ragged pilot from st. louis.'"

CHAPTER 18

Extra Lessons

during my two-ish years as an apprentice, worked with a lotta pilots, saw all kinds of steamboatmen and boats. sometimes mr. bixby couldn't have me with him, so he'd send me with someone else. i'm still learning from that time. got to know pretty much every type of person you'd find in stories or history. regular jobs take decades to teach that. don't mean i'm a judge of men—those are born, not made. but it's given me a real flavor for reading. when i run into a well-written character, feels like i know them from the river.

the guy i think of most is brown from the 'pennsylvania'—mentioned him before. middle-aged, long, slim, horse-faced, ignorant, stingy, always looking to find fault. dreaded every watch with him. no matter how good the break was or how high my spirits were, they'd sink when i got near the pilot-house.

first time i met that guy... boat was backing out from St. Louis, and i headed up to the pilot-house feeling all proud, like i was part of the crew on this fast and famous boat. Brown was at the wheel. i stopped, ready to introduce myself, but he didn’t turn around. maybe he glanced at me, but no other sign, so probably imagined it. he was busy navigating tricky waters, so i quietly took a seat.

we sat in silence for about ten minutes. then Brown gave me a long, hard look, top to bottom. felt like forever. finally, he asked, “are you Horace Bigsby’s cub?” i nodded. another pause, another look over.

“what’s your name?” i told him. he repeated it, but he never used it again. always just called me “Here!” followed by what he wanted me to do. 

“where were you born?” “in Florida, Missouri.”

“dern sight better staid there!” he shot back. then he fired off a bunch of questions about my family.

we hit the first crossing, leads going in, breaking up the interrogation. once done, he asked, “how long you been on the river?” i answered. then, “where’d you get them shoes?” i told him. “hold up your foot!” i did. he gave the shoe a long, disdainful look, scratching his head with his hat tilted. “well, i’ll be dod derned!” and back to the wheel he went.

why he was so surprised? still no clue. about fifteen minutes of silence dragged by. then he turned, face red as a fire, and yelled, “here! you going to set there all day?”

i jumped up, shocked. managed to say, “i have had no orders, sir.”

“no orders! fine bird we are, need orders! father was a gentleman, had slaves, went to school. yes, we are a gentleman, need orders! i’ll teach you about orders! get away from the wheel!” didn’t realize i’d moved that close.

stepped back, feeling dazed by the whole attack. “what you standing there for? take that ice-pitcher down to the texas-tender—move it, don’t be all day!”

when i got back, he snapped, “here! what took you so long?”

“couldn’t find the texas-tender; had to go to the pantry.”

“derned likely story! fill up the stove.”

i set to work. he watched me like a hawk. then he shouted, “put that shovel down! deadest numskull i ever saw—can’t even load a stove right.”

so here's how it was every watch. same routine for months. dread was the norm. even in the darkest night, i felt those yellow eyes waiting to pounce. he'd start with—

'here! take the wheel.'

two minutes in—

'where in the nation you going to? pull her down! pull her down!'

then—

'say! you gonna hold her all day? let her go—meet her! meet her!'

he'd jump up, snatch the wheel, and handle it himself, spewing wrath all the while.

george ritchie, the other pilot's cub, had it easy. his boss, george ealer, was as kind as brown wasn't. ritchie had dealt with brown before, so he knew how to make my life hell while having a laugh. when i took the wheel on ealer's watch, ritchie would sit back and mimic brown—

'snatch her! snatch her! derndest mud-cat i ever saw!' 'where you going now? gonna run over that snag?' 'pull her down! don't you hear me? pull her down!' 'there she goes! just as i expected! told you not to cramp that reef. g'way from the wheel!'

so it was rough all the time, no matter whose watch. sometimes ritchie's joking was almost as bad as brown's serious nagging.

wanted to kill brown often, but that wouldn't do. a cub had to take whatever the boss dished out. we all thought there was a law against striking a pilot on duty. so i'd just imagine killing brown instead. no law against that. every night, instead of running the river in my mind, i'd get creative and kill brown in all sorts of new ways. never the same, always fresh and gruesome.

brown was always finding fault or inventing it. scold for shaving a shore, then for not. hug a bar, then don't. pull down, don't pull down. fire up, wait for orders. everything you did, wrong. and always as an insult.

one day near new madrid, heavy load, brown steering. i was standing by. he'd glance at me, plotting some trap. finally, he stepped back and said in that snarly voice—

'here! see if you've got gumption enough to round her to.'

this was bound to crash and burn. he never let me round the boat before, so no matter what i tried, he could rip it apart. brown watched me like a hawk. i got rattled quick, tried bringing the boat around too early, spotted that smug look in his eyes, and corrected. tried again, still too high, fixed it again. i made mistake after mistake but kept it together, until i finally blew it by starting too late. brown's moment arrived.

he went red, lunged, tossed me aside like nothing, spun the wheel, and unloaded on me. full tirade until he was gasping. he called me every name he knew, almost swore—dod dern was the worst he dared, thanks to some fire and brimstone upbringing.

that hour sucked, with a crowd watching from the hurricane deck. that night in bed, i imagined killing brown seventeen different ways, all new.

CHAPTER 19

brown and i exchange compliments

couple trips later, more trouble. brown steering, me pulling down. my brother yelled from the deck to stop at some landing a mile down. brown ignored it—his style. wind howling, and brown's half-deaf, though he'd never admit it. i doubted he heard, but i kept my mouth shut. i've only got one head.

sure enough, we sailed past the landing. captain klinefelter showed up, asking why we didn’t stop.

brown said, 'no, sir!'

'i sent him up,' captain said.

'he came up, and that's all the good it did. the dod-derned fool never said anything.'

captain turned to me, 'didn't you hear him?'

i didn’t want in on this, but no dodging it, 'yes, sir.'

i knew what brown would say next: 'shut your mouth! you never heard anything.'

so i shut up. an hour later, henry came in, unaware. i felt bad—brown wouldn't spare him. brown jumped right in, 'why didn't you tell me to land?'

'i did tell you, mr. brown.'

'it's a lie!'

i said, 'you lie. he did tell you.'

brown looked shocked, speechless for a second, then snapped, 'i'll deal with you soon!' then to henry, 'out of the pilot-house!'

pilot rules were strict, no getting around them. kid was already outside when Brown lost it, grabbed a ten-pounder of coal, and charged. i was ready, though, with a heavy stool. laid Brown out flat.

i'd done the unthinkable—hit a pilot on duty. thought for sure i was headed to the pen. figured might as well settle the score while i could, so i kept at him. lost track of time, but it was a good while. felt longer cause i was enjoying it. Brown broke free eventually, ran to the wheel. understandable, the boat was going fifteen miles an hour with no one steering. luckily, Eagle Bend was wide and deep enough she was fine on her own. luck, though—could’ve been in the trees.

Brown saw the 'Pennsylvania' was safe, grabbed the spy-glass like a club, told me to get out. i wasn't scared anymore. instead, i stuck around, critiqued his grammar, fixed his rants into proper English. he wasn't geared for that kind of fight, so he gave up, took the wheel, grumbling. i backed off to the bench. noise brought everyone out, including the captain. my stomach dropped. he was usually fair, but he could be tough when it mattered.

wondered what he'd do to a cub pilot for this kind of mess. boat full of freight and passengers, and i'd picked a fight. our shift was ending. thought about hiding till i could slip ashore. snuck out of the pilot-house, made my way around, but the captain caught me. head down, he stood there, then said—

'Follow me.'

trailed him to his room. doors shut, just us. he sat, i stood. he looked at me, then said—

'So you’ve been fighting Mr. Brown?'

meek—

'Yes, sir.'

'That’s serious.'

'Yes, sir.'

'Boat was unmanned at the wheel for five minutes.'

'Yes, sir.'

'Did you start it?'

'Yes, sir.'

'With what?'

'A stool, sir.'

'Hard?'

'Middling, sir.'

'Knocked him down?'

'He fell, sir.'

'Did you continue?'

'Yes, sir.'

'What’d you do?'

'Pounded him, sir.'

'Pounded him?'

'Yes, sir.'

'Was it severe?'

'Could say that, sir.'

im real glad about it, but listen up, dont mention i said that. youve messed up big time, and dont ever do it again on this boat. but give him a good thrashing on shore, got it? ill cover the costs. now go, and not a word to anyone. get out—you're in deep trouble, kid!

i slipped out, feeling like i dodged a bullet. heard him laughing and slapping his thighs after i closed the door.

when Brown finished his shift, he went straight to the captain, who was chatting with passengers, and demanded i be put ashore in New Orleans, saying—

'i wont work on this boat again while that kid stays.'

the captain replied—

'he doesnt need to be around when youre on watch, Mr. Brown.'

'i wont even stay on the same boat with him. one of us has got to go ashore.'

'fine,' said the captain, 'then let it be you;' and went back to his conversation.

for the rest of the trip, i felt like a freed slave; cause i was one. while docked, i listened to George Ealer's flute, or his readings from Goldsmith and Shakespeare, or played chess with him—wouldve beat him sometimes, but he'd always redo his last move and change the outcome.

CHAPTER 20

A Catastrophe

we hung around New Orleans for three days, but the captain couldnt find another pilot. he suggested i take the daylight watch, leaving nights to George Ealer. but i was scared; never stood a watch solo, thought id mess up at some chute or ground us in a shortcut. Brown stayed, but refused to travel with me. so the captain gave me a ticket on the 'A. T. Lacey' to St. Louis, said id get back to piloting there. the 'Lacey' was leaving two days after the 'Pennsylvania.'

the night before the 'Pennsylvania' left, Henry and i sat on a freight pile talking till midnight. mostly about steamboat disasters. little did we know, one was on its way to us. water up the river was already heading our way to make the steam for it. we figured folks without authority werent much help in disasters, but maybe some; decided if disaster hit, we'd stick to the boat and help however we could. Henry remembered that later, when disaster struck, and acted on it.

the 'Lacey' set off two days after the 'Pennsylvania.' at Greenville, Mississippi, a couple of days out, someone shouted—

'the “Pennsylvania” blew up at Ship Island, and a hundred and fifty lives lost!'

at Napoleon, Arkansas, that evening, we got an extra Memphis paper with some details. mentioned my brother, said he wasnt hurt.

further up the river, we got another extra. brother mentioned again, this time hurt beyond help. full details didnt come till Memphis. and heres the sorrowful story—

six in the morning, summer heat bearing down. the 'Pennsylvania' was creeping along north of Ship Island, about 60 miles below Memphis, towing a wood-flat that was almost empty. George Ealer in the pilot house, probably alone. second engineer and a striker were on watch in the engine room. second mate had the deck. George Black, Mr. Wood, and my brother, all clerks, were asleep. Brown, the head engineer, carpenter, chief mate, and one striker too. Captain Klinefelter was in the barber's chair, getting ready for a shave. plenty of cabin passengers and a few hundred on deck, but most were still asleep. with the wood nearly gone, Ealer rang for full steam ahead. then, bam, four of the eight boilers blew. the whole front third of the boat shot skyward. when the debris fell back, it was a mountain of wreckage, and then the fire started.

people were thrown into the river, including Mr. Wood, my brother, and the carpenter. the carpenter hit the water still on his mattress, 75 feet from the boat. Brown, the pilot, and George Black, chief clerk, vanished after the explosion. Captain Klinefelter, still in the barber's chair, unharmed. the barber, also fine, just stood there with his toe over the edge, mindlessly stirring his lather.

Ealer saw the chimneys go up and knew what happened. he wrapped his face in his coat lapels, pressed his hands tight to keep steam out. he had time while he went up and came back down. he landed on the unexploded boilers 40 feet below where the pilot house was, with his wheel and other debris in a cloud of steam. many breathed that steam and died. Ealer didn’t. he got to clear air fast, then climbed back to find his chess pieces and flute.

fire started getting serious. screams and cries everywhere. many scalded, many hurt. an iron crowbar had gone through a man, maybe a priest. he didn’t die right away, suffered terribly. a young French naval cadet, just 15, scalded badly but stayed strong. both mates badly scalded too, but they stuck to work. they moved the wood-boat aft, held back the panicked immigrants till the wounded were safe.

when Mr. Wood and Henry hit the water, they swam for the shore, just a few hundred yards off. Henry said he felt fine (wasn't true) and swam back to help with the wounded. they separated, and Henry went back.

fire was raging, people trapped under ruins begging for help. tried fighting it with buckets, no luck. ditched those, grabbed axes to cut folks out. one guy, a striker, stuck but unhurt, begged for someone to shoot him when fire started forcing us back. we had to leave him, his pleas ringing until flames did their work.

everyone who could fit moved onto a wood-flat, cut it loose, floated down with the burning steamer toward Ship Island. docked it there, but no shelter, food, or help for injuries. just the sun beating down. eventually, a steamer picked us up, took us to Memphis. they didn’t waste time — help poured in. by then, Henry was unconscious. docs checked him, saw nothing to do, focused on those who had a chance.

forty wounded laid on pallets in a big hall, Henry among them. Memphis ladies brought flowers, fruits, all sorts of comforts. docs and med students on watch. town chipped in whatever else needed. Memphis knew the drill, seen many disasters like the 'Pennsylvania's.'

first time i saw that hall, rows of more than forty, heads wrapped in raw cotton. grim scene. stuck around six days, nights. was tough. every day, they'd quietly move the dying to a separate room, so others wouldn’t see death up close. they'd be carried out with a screen of helpers, but everyone knew what it meant. always a shudder as they passed, like a wave through the room.

i watched too many folks get taken to the 'death-room' and not come back. our chief mate made that trip a few times. his injuries were brutal, mostly bad scalds. covered in linseed oil and raw cotton, he looked nothing like a human. he'd lose his mind from the pain, yelling and shrieking, then collapse into exhaustion. sometimes he thought he was back on the forecastle, barking orders at an imaginary crew. ripping off his bandages, he'd show the charred skin underneath. it was hard for the other patients, so the docs tried to give him morphine, but he refused. said it killed his wife and he wouldn't touch it. suspected it was in his meds and water, so he stopped drinking altogether. after two days without water, he almost gave in but stayed strong and tossed it away. three times he was carried off, thought to be dying, but he'd always come back, cursing and demanding to return. he survived and went back to work as a steamboat mate.

but he was the only one who came back from that room alive. dr. peyton, a top-notch doc, did everything he could for henry, but henry's injuries were too far gone. on the sixth day, henry's mind wandered, and his hands fiddled with the covers. it was his time. we took him to the death-room for the last time.

CHAPTER 21

A Section in My Biography

eventually, i got my pilot license. worked small jobs at first, then steady gigs followed. life was smooth, and i figured i'd spend my days on the river, die at the wheel. then the war hit, and everything stopped. had to find a new path. became a silver miner in nevada, then a reporter, then a gold miner in california, back to reporting in san francisco, then a correspondent in the sandwich islands, europe, and the east. lectured a bit, then started writing books in new england.

that's twenty-one years summed up quick since i last sat in a pilot-house.

CHAPTER 22

I Return to My Muttons

after being away twenty-one years, i got the itch to see the river, the boats, and any of the old crew still around. decided to head west in april with a poet and a stenographer tagging along.

was thinking about taking notes, planning to print 'em later. But if folks recognized me on the river, it'd be tough to sneak around, talk, and dig for info like i wanted. Old-time steamboat folks used to fill strangers with wild, colorful stories and give pals the boring facts. So, figured it'd be smart to use fake names. Seemed like a good plan, but man, keeping track of those names was a pain. How do criminals remember their aliases? It's a mystery. I couldn't even hold onto mine without any crime to worry about. 

Left on the Pennsylvania Railroad, 8 A.M. April 18.

'EVENING. About clothing. Elegance fades the further you get from New York.'

Found that in my notes. Doesn't matter where you're headed, it's the same. North, south, east, west, you can guess your distance by how much style's missing in the new passengers' clothes—not just the women, men too. Maybe it's about how they carry themselves. Even with clothes from top New York tailors, you can still tell they're not New Yorkers. There's a kind of innate style New Yorkers have that clothes can't fake.

'APRIL 19. This morning, hit the land of full goatees—sometimes with a mustache, but not often.'

Weird seeing so many of that outdated style. Like bumping into someone you thought was long gone. Goatees spread far, with a solid belief in biblical creation that scientists haven't shaken.

'AFTERNOON. Loafers at the stations have both hands in pockets; no exceptions here. Important geography note.'

If loafers defined a place, it'd be even more important.

'Elsewhere, station-loafers sometimes scratch a shin with a foot; not here. Ominous.'

Eventually, we hit the tobacco-chewing zone. Used to cover the whole Union fifty years back. Not so much now.

Then, boots started showing up. Not many, but more further down the Mississippi. They vanished from other places with the mud; probably will from river towns too, once they get decent roads.

Got to St. Louis at 10 p.m. Tried giving a fake name at the hotel with a poor attempt at casualness. Clerk paused, giving me that look you give a decent person in a sketchy situation, and then said—

ok so this guy thinks he's clever. claims he's been a clerk in New York, but i'm not buying it. we head to the supper room. ran into some folks i've seen around before. funny how those fakes use my name and get away with it, but the second i'm up to something, bam—busted.

looks like we gotta hit the river tomorrow if more folks like this keep showing up. kinda sucks cause we wanted a week in St. Louis. the Southern hotel was nice—much better than that overdone Palmer House in Chicago. sure, the billiard tables are ancient, but that's part of the charm. no river guys around either. used to see them all the time, showing off, throwing around money. not anymore. twenty-one years and that whole scene's just gone.

up in my room, there's this guy, Rogers—or whatever he's calling himself—crying cause he can't stand the water. funny thing, that water's still the same muddy mess it was years ago. bishop once told me it's got an acre of land in every glass. let it settle, and you can separate the dirt from the water. dirt's filling, water's refreshing. locals mix it up and drink it like it's nothing. might taste weird at first, but once you're used to it, you'll like it. good for the steamboats, good for drinking, but not much else unless you're baptizing.

next morning, drove around town in the rain. city felt the same, but it wasn't. st. louis, like london or pittsburgh, can't make new stuff look new. coal smoke ages it fast. city's doubled since i lived here, now 400,000 people. business district looks the same, but less smoke now. used to be a thick black cover, now it's thinned out. no one seems to mind.

outside the center, changes pop up more. new houses are modern, with green lawns, unlike the old blocky ones with stone frames and matching windows. forest park is new, big and mostly natural—nice touch. other parks like tower grove and botanical gardens show st. louis was ahead of other cities in this.

first time here, could've bought st. louis for six million. biggest mistake not doing it. now, seeing this spread of buildings, it stings. seemed dumb not to buy, but back then, had my reasons.

years ago, a scotsman, charles augustus murray, said streets were narrow, badly paved, poorly lit. they're still narrow, some paving's bad, but lighting's improved. back then, only big deal building was the catholic new church, which folks admired despite its odd steeple. now, st. louis has grand public buildings, and that little church isn't special anymore. murray would've expected this, though. he saw st. louis's potential.

as we drove further, city's growth became more obvious. changes kept showing progress and energy.

the big change hit the levee hard. used to be packed with wide-awake steamboats, now half a dozen asleep. it's sad. the lively steamboatmen are gone, absorbed into the crowd, working mundane jobs like everyone else. those empty wharves used to be alive with commerce. now, just a tired guy sleeping off whiskey. where'd the bustling scenes go? 

towboats and railroads took over. the bridge overhead chipped in too. old steamboat guys tell me the bridge doesn't pay, but that's no comfort. the riverfront's rough now: broken sidewalks, plenty of mud. but the hustle of drays and freight—gone. a few sleepy bars hang on, but their crowds have thinned.

steamboating on the mississippi sprang up around 1812, hit its stride in thirty years, and in thirty more, it faded. not dead, like an old-timer who can't jump anymore, but compared to its prime, it's done.

it wiped out keel-boating by slashing trip times to new orleans. then railroads killed passenger traffic, and towing fleets dragged freight cheaper than any steamboat could compete with.

what's left? some freight and passenger traffic for a few big companies. they run it tight, still make money. cities like st. louis and new orleans seem fine, but the wood-yard men—gone. they once lined the river, selling wood by the cord. now, everything burns coal, and wood-piles are rare. where'd those guys go?

wanted to hit every town between st. louis and new orleans, hopping on the short packet lines. sounds easy, right? maybe twenty years ago. now, not so much. boats are few and far between.

thought i'd start with the old french spots, st. genevieve and kaskaskia, sixty miles down from st. louis. only one boat heading that way—a grand tower packet. figured one was enough, so we checked it out. what a wreck. looked like it had more in common with dirt than a boat. could've taxed it as real estate with all that grime. hurricane deck could fetch a hundred and fifty an acre in new england. even had wheat sprouting from the cracks. soil on the boiler deck was thin but alright for grazing. talked to a colored boy on watch—no one else around. said the boat would leave once it got its trip. if not, it'd wait.

'got any of the trip?'

'bless you, no, boss. ain't unloaded yet. just got in this morning.'

not sure when it’d leave. maybe tomorrow, maybe not. no good. gave up on the idea of sailing a farm downriver. next option: the 'gold dust' vicksburg packet at 5 P.M. headed to memphis, decided stopping off wasn't gonna work. boat was clean and comfy. set up on the boiler deck and grabbed some cheap reads. bought 'em from an old irishman with a kind face and a smooth tongue. he’d been in st. louis for thirty-four years, never crossed the river. started on a grand speech, clearly not his first rodeo. turned out to be better company than the books he sold. mentioned irishmen and beer, and he said—

‘they don't drink it, sir. give an irishman lager for a month, he's done for. lined with copper, beer eats through it. whiskey, though, that polishes the copper and saves him.’

8 o'clock, on the dot, we backed out and crossed the river. Pitch black, then bam—blinding white light from the forecastle, lit up everything like noon. Big change from the old days—those flickering torches dripping pitch are history. Now, just a couple of guys and some steam get the stage down from the derrick fast, no need for a whole crew. Why nobody thought of this when the first steamboat was built beats me. Humans can be slow on the uptake.

We finally got going at 2 a.m. I was up by six, rounding a rocky point with an old stone warehouse—more like its ruins. A few rundown houses by the leafy hills, no signs of life. I thought I'd forgotten the river. Nothing looked familiar. Annoyed and confused.

We dropped off a well-dressed couple and two young girls with fancy bags. Odd place for them—no carriage waiting. Off they went down a country road.

Turns out they were headed to a big town hidden behind a new island two miles down. Couldn't remember the town, lost my temper a bit. Guessed St. Genevieve—nailed it. This weird river had built up a useless island, cutting off the town. Used to be a prime spot, all French back in the day.

Went up to the hurricane deck, looked at the pilot-house.

CHAPTER 24

My Incognito is Exploded

Checked out the pilot on watch, didn't recognize him, so I went up. We eyed each other, then I sat on the bench. Everything looked the same, except this big tube under the breast-board. Wondered about it, then asked.

'It's for hearing the engine-bells.'

Another invention that should've come 50 years ago. While I was thinking, the pilot asked—

'Know what this rope's for?'

Dodged that.

'First time in a pilot-house?'

Sidestepped it.

'Where you from?'

'New England.'

'First time West?'

Jumped over that.

'If you're interested, I can explain all this stuff.'

I said sure.

"this," putting his hand on the backing-bell rope, "sounds the fire alarm. this one," touching the go-ahead bell, "calls the texas-tender. then this," pointing at the whistle-lever, "calls the captain." and he kept going, touching each thing, spinning his calm web of lies.

never felt more like a passenger. i thanked him for each new 'fact' and jotted it down. the pilot really got into it, piling on the old-school stories. sometimes i thought his imagination might snap, but it held up fine. he drifted into tales about the river's wild quirks, backing them up with huge examples. like—

"see that tiny rock sticking out of the water? when i first came here, that was a solid rock ridge, over sixty feet high, two miles long. all washed away but that." [sighs]

had a strong urge to take him out, but regular killing seemed too kind.

once, an odd boat with a big coal box on a beam went by. he pointed at it like it was boring, said it was an "alligator boat."

"alligator boat? what's it for?"

"to dredge out alligators with."

"are they that thick?"

"not now, government keeps them down. but they used to be. not everywhere; but in spots like plum point, stack island—places they call alligator beds."

"did they actually block navigation?"

"years ago, yeah, in very low water; barely a trip without grounding on alligators."

felt like i had to get out my tomahawk, but held back and said—

"must've been dreadful."

piloting's got its headaches, man, especially with water shifting all over. you can spot wind-reefs and sand-reefs no problem, but alligator reefs? those are tricky. most times, you can't even see the water, and if you do, it's probably moved by the time you get there. only a few pilots can judge alligator water right, like Ben Thornburg and Beck Jolly. those guys could spot it a mile and a half off, like recognizing whiskey from far away. they made bank for it too, pulling in fifteen hundred a month. nights when others would lay up, they'd keep going unless it was foggy. rumor was they could smell the best alligator water, but i keep to what i know, not hearsay.

now, dredging alligators? once you dredge one, it's over. he won't come back even for pie. they scooped 'em up and hauled 'em to Orleans for the Government. made soldier-shoes out of their hides. best shoes you'll find—last five years, no water absorption. alligator fishery's a Government deal, like live-oaks. cut a live-oak, get fined fifty bucks. kill an alligator, you might get hanged if you're a Democrat. the buzzard's sacred in the South, the alligator's sacred to the Government. hands off.

getting aground on alligators? hasn't happened in years. they keep the alligator boats for police duty now. the gators know the boats like burglars know cops. soon as they see one, they clear out.

after wrapping up the gator stuff, he slid into some history. he went on about these old steamboats he knew, really going deep on one wild ride from his favorite—the “cyclone.” 

‘that boat was the “cyclone”—last trip she made, she sunk that trip. captain was tom ballou, biggest liar ive ever met. couldnt tell the truth even if the sun was shining. he'd make your skin crawl. i finally left him; couldn't take it. saying goes, “like master, like man,” and if you hang around guys like that, folks start wondering about you too. he paid well, but what's that when your name's on the line? so, i walked, kept my name clean. never looked back. reputation's everything, right? tom had more selfish parts than any seven men, all crammed in his skull. head tilted back so much, folks thought it was vanity, but it was spite. just seeing his foot, you'd think he was nineteen feet tall, but nah, he was five ten. take the lies and malice out, and he'd be gone.

the “cyclone” was a dream to steer, smooth as silk in the water. set her right, and she'd hold course on a star all night. steering her was easier than counting votes in a rigged election. last trip, just at dawn, they pulled the rudder for repair; i didnt know and backed her out easy, weaving downriver fine. twenty-three miles and four nasty crossings later—'

‘without a rudder?’

‘yep—old tom showed up complaining about running in the dark—’

‘dark? you said—’

‘never mind that—dark as egypt then, but soon the moon—’

‘you mean the _sun_, it was dawn. was this before you ditched tom for lying, or—’

‘long before. as i was saying, he—’

‘was this the trip she sunk?’

‘no, months later. so, the old man—’

‘then she had _two_ last trips, cause you said—’

he stepped back from the wheel, wiping sweat, and said—

‘here!’ (calling me by name) ‘_you_ steer and spin some yarns—you're better at it. trying to pass as a stranger—i knew you after seven words. figured you were trying to draw me out. well, i let you, didnt i? now take the wheel; next time, play fair, and you won’t have to work your way through.’

that was the end of the fake name gig. barely six hours out of St. Louis. didnt take long, but i got what i wanted. been itching to take the wheel since the start. forgot the river a bit, but steering a steamboat? nah, thats still there, and i love it.

CHAPTER 25

From Cairo to Hickman
