the stretch from St. Louis to Cairo is something else. two hundred miles of varied beauty. spring had the hills all fresh and green, setting off the river real nice. perfect day to start, clear skies, good breeze. the boat eating up miles like nothing.

ran into a railway at Chester, Illinois. Chester's moving up with a penitentiary, too. Grand Tower had a railway. Cape Girardeau had one. Grand Tower gets its name from a big rock on the Missouri side, looks like nature having some fun. near it, the Devil's Bake Oven and the Devil's Tea Table. odd names, but they fit with the look. there's also the Devil's Elbow and Race-course downriver.

Grand Tower seemed busier than before, but needed some fixing up. Uncle Mumford, our second officer, said high water had it looking rough. funny thing, he said this place makes the best lime out West but skips on the whitewash. like how dairy farms never have milk for coffee, or sugar plantations lack sugar. he joked, folks who make lime lean more religious than whitewash. place is a coal hub too, doing well.

Cape Girardeau sits on a hillside, looks good. big Jesuit school by the river. Mumford said it's got a strong rep in Missouri. there's another college up high, new building with towers like giant casters. he called Cape Girardeau the Athens of Missouri, full of religious schools. pointed out the town's religious vibe, but to me, it looked like any hill town. sometimes folks see what they want to see.

uncle mumford's been working the river for thirty years, solid as a rock. practical, level-headed, seen all kinds of things. got his opinions, and a touch of poetry mixed in. speaks well, voice like a growl, and he's got a few choice words when needed. he's an old-school mate, cussing his way through the work like it softens the heart of any ex-steamboatman missing the old days. 'get up there! gonna take all day? why didnt you say you were petrified before you signed on?'

steady with his crew. kind, fair, but firm, so they stick with him. still wears the old mate's slouchy gear, but next trip, anchor line's suiting him up in a blue naval uniform with brass buttons. he'll be a whole new sight.

uniforms on the mississippi. wild change. should've happened fifty years back. passengers used to mistake mates for cooks, captains for barbers, and got an earful for it. but now, no more mix-ups. and the staff looks sharper too.

steered down below cape girardeau. 'steersman's bend' they called it; easy sailing, plenty of water. about the only spot new guys got to steer through in low water.

places like thebes and commerce, unchanged. the grand chain, still a line of sunken rocks ready to snag steamboats on bad nights. lots of wrecks there, including my first friend, the 'paul jones.' she went down with a gray mare and a preacher. mumford told me. disaster made sense with them on board. he added—

'plenty scoff at that, call it superstition. but they never traveled with a gray mare and a preacher. i did once. we grounded all over, hit rocks, lost a man in a fight, blew a boiler, broke a shaft, and took on water. painted the mare blue and tossed the preacher overboard. saved us. preacher admitted his fault. i remember it clear as day.'

that preacher and gray mare combo bringing trouble sounds nuts, right? but it's true. tons of evidence to back it up. i remember a captain warned about taking a gray mare and a preacher with him. he ignored it. maybe the same day, maybe the next, but he got drunk and fell down a hatch. dead. that's the real story.

hat island's gone now. every bit of it. can't even recall exactly where it was, just somewhere between st. louis and cairo. bad area back then. a farmer on the illinois side said he could see twenty-nine steamboat wrecks from his house. between st. louis and cairo, the wrecks are about one per mile. that's two hundred wrecks total.

noticed big changes from commerce down. beaver dam rock's in the middle of the river now, making a big fuss; used to be near the shore. a big island that was mid-river is now hugging the missouri shore. boats avoid it. jacket pattern island's just a wedge now, won't last long. goose island's mostly gone, just a patch left. graveyard, where we used to creep through wrecks, is way off the channel now, harmless. one of the two sisters islands vanished; the other moved a mile to the missouri side, but still illinois land. folks ferry over to work and pay illinois taxes. weird setup.

near the river's mouth, some islands disappeared, washed away. cairo's still there, easy to spot from afar. had to steer around to reach it. night hit as we left the upper river, merging with the ohio floods. no worries, though. a rock that was a problem moved upstream or maybe the land shifted. mississippi's fair like that; takes one farm, builds another nearby. keeps folks civil.

heading into cairo, almost hit a steamboat ignoring our whistle, cutting across our path. strong backing saved it, but would've made a good story if not.

cairo's got a different vibe now, all built up and solid. can't help but notice the change from dickens's old take on it. last time i saw it, there were bricks going up, back when colonel, now general, grant was drilling his men there. uncle mumford thinks the libraries and sunday-schools helped cairo grow, along with those brick masons. it's sitting pretty at the crossroads of two big rivers, so it's got a solid railroad and river trade going.

woke up, and we'd passed columbus, kentucky, heading toward hickman. nice spot on a hill. hickman used to move a lot of tobacco, pulling it from all over and shipping it out by boat. uncle mumford says they built a railway to boost the trade, but thinks it backfired—trade skipped hickman and just ran along the rail line instead.

CHAPTER 26

under fire

talk shifted to the war as we moved into old battle territory. just left columbus, so folks brought up belmont. some of the boat's crew served in the mississippi fleet. heard they were out of place at first, but got used to it. one pilot got his start at belmont, steering a confederate boat. always wondered what a newbie felt like up in the pilot house, bullets flying, no one there to judge if he panicked. his story filled that gap for me.

THE PILOT'S FIRST BATTLE

he said—

november 7th, fight kicked off at seven a.m. i was on the 'r. h. w. hill.' took troops from columbus, then artillery. my partner wanted to see the fight, told me to join him. said no, i'd watch from the pilot-house. called me a coward and left.

it was a brutal sight. general cheatham told his men to ditch their coats and follow him, hell or victory. heard it all from the pilot-house. general pillow, white hair and white horse, led his men like a kid. then the federals pushed them back; rebels ran for cover. i was dangling my legs out the pilot-house window, heard a bullet whiz past. didn't think, just fell back onto the floor. cannon-balls ripped through the chimney, one clipped the pilot-house corner. shells everywhere. things got real hot—wished id stayed home.

laying on the pilot-house floor while shots whipped past, i figured getting behind the stove was smart. wrong. a minie-ball sliced through it, barely missed my head, and took a chunk outta my hat. time to move. captain was up top with some red-headed major from memphis, saying he wanted out but thought i was dead. decided to pull the bell and back us up. peeked out, saw fifteen holes in the windows i hadn't even noticed. shots hitting the water like a hailstorm. definitely time to bail. slid down the guy wire headfirst. before i hit the deck, captain said we gotta leave. climbed back up, back on the floor. they were dragging my partner up, thinking i was a goner. he saw me and yelled, "oh, hell, he ain't shot," shook off the soldiers, and ran. we stayed till three, then left safe.

next time i saw him, i asked, "where'd you vanish to during that mess?" he admitted, "down in the hold."

honestly, i was scared stiff, too rattled to think straight, but kept it to myself. next day, General Polk called me in, praised my bravery. i didn't argue, just nodded along. wasn't true, but not my place to correct him.

soon after, i got sick, needed the Hot Springs. got letters from commanders wanting me back, but i wasn't up for it. stayed quiet, kept the reputation.

Mumford once told me that pilot might've 'gilded his scare,' but his actions later kinda backed it up.

headed down through the chute of Island No. 8, struck up a chat with a passenger. sharp-looking guy, lived near Island No. 10, famous from the war. talk shifted to 'feuds'—this part of the South, they last forever. he said—

Old stories here are full of feuds, but the Darnells and Watsons had the worst. Nobody remembers what started it. Maybe a horse, maybe a cow. Didn't matter. Both families were rich, so it wasn't about money. Could've been settled easy, but pride got in the way. Words turned to blood, and it went on for sixty years. They'd shoot each other whenever they crossed paths, like some twisted ritual. Didn't matter if it was a man or a boy; anyone could get it. They'd even take their guns to church, sitting on opposite sides, pretending to pray but ready for a fight. 

About twenty-five years back, one side caught a young guy, nineteen, and killed him. Don't recall if it was Darnells or Watsons, but he rode up to a steamboat, spotted the enemy, and ducked behind a woodpile. They surrounded him, shots flying everywhere. He hit a couple of them but ended up in the river. They kept shooting from the bank till he was dead. Windy Marshall, captain of the boat, told me the whole thing. He saw it happen.

years ago, the darnells got so thin on the ground, the old man and his sons figured they’d leave the country. tried to catch a steamboat just above no. 10, but the watsons caught wind. showed up just as the two young darnells were heading up with their wives. fight broke out then and there, didn’t make it any further—both killed. after that, old darnell tangled with the ferry guy. ferry man didn’t make it either. but his buddies filled old darnell full of lead—ended him.

guy who shared this story was raised in comfort, educated, college man. his sloppy grammar wasn’t ignorance, just careless habit. you see it a lot in the west, especially in towns. heard a supposedly educated guy say, “never mind, it don’t make no difference, anyway.” someone local heard it too, didn’t flinch. didn’t even register till reminded later. tells you how common it is—familiarity’s dulled the ears.

nobody’s perfect with grammar; nobody’s ever written it perfectly. not fair to demand it from the valley folks, but they oughta avoid knowingly trashing it.

found the river changed at island no. 10. the island i remembered was three miles long, quarter mile wide, thick timber, close to the kentucky shore. now, you need a spyglass. just a small tuft left, and it's a mile away on the other shore. in war, it was key—fortified, controlled the spot. kept union forces apart till they joined across missouri. now the island’s part of the neck, river’s clear.

here, the river zigzags—kentucky to tennessee, back to missouri, then kentucky, then tennessee again. missouri just pokes into tennessee a bit.

new madrid's looking rough, but still the same old town. frame houses still sitting in that same flat plain, surrounded by the same forests. quiet like it's always been. heard the high water came through and messed up the place. didn't expect that, since the riverbank's usually fifty feet high there. back in the day, flooding was a no-go. but 1882 flood hit hard. gonna be remembered for a while. water everywhere from cairo down south, levees busted all over. at its worst, the mississippi stretched seventy miles wide. lives lost, property wrecked. crops gone, houses floated off, folks and cattle stranded on high ground waiting for rescue. everything underwater for ages. without quick help, the poorer folks would've been in big trouble. water's been dropping, but banks still flooded mostly.

CHAPTER 27

some imported articles

saw two steamboats at new madrid. two at once! rare sight on the lonely mississippi now. this huge, empty flood's got a mood of its own—impressive but kinda heavy. mile after mile, that brown water just keeps flowing between thick forest walls, with barely anything moving to break the stillness. day turns to night, night back to day—unchanging peace and quiet, like a taste of eternity or some heaven the preachers talk about.

after 1812, english tourists started coming over. first a few, then more. each one wrote a book—mostly calm and fair, but our folks didn't always see it that way. looking at those books, the mississippi's pretty much how they saw it back then, unchanged in a lot of ways. tourists' feelings were different at first, since they had to come up with their own, unlike places where you just borrow emotions from the past. making an emotion from scratch is tough—easier to spin up facts. captain basil hall, R.N., wrote about it fifty-five years back...

caught my first look at this thing i’d wanted to see forever. all that hassle getting here felt worth it right then. stood there watching the river roll by till dark. took another dozen visits to really get how big it all was.

mrs. trollope’s words, a few months later, 1827, hitting the mississippi—

first sign of land was this huge river dumping its muddy waters into the gulf. never seen anything so bleak. dante would’ve had a field day here. only thing sticking up is a ship mast from an old wreck, a grim reminder and a warning.

then charles augustus murray, near st. louis, seven years on—

only after you’ve gone fifty, hundred miles upstream and use your imagination do you grasp its power. it’s shaping a vast valley, dragging off chunks of forest and soil, making islands for future folks. think about it—this river’s run two, three thousand miles already and still has thirteen hundred to go before the ocean.

captain marryat, R.N. writing in 1837, three years after murray—

never seen a century of chaos like mississippi's record. river fits the crimes. it's not a pretty sight like other rivers. can't stroll its banks or swim safely. it's a wild, dangerous torrent, filled with dirt. swimmers don't last without help from a log. people said it wouldn't float a swimmer or let a body rise. fish are rough, like catfish. banks have alligators, panthers hidden in cane-brakes. river tears through useless trees, dragging forests with it. blocks its path sometimes, making new channels. floods everything when mad. uprooted trees float as traps for steamboats. boats get pierced and sink fast. no charm to this river. dumps mud into the gulf, polluting it. it's a devil, tamed only by steam.

rough writing for a penman, but it shows the visitor's awe of the 'sewer'. stats are off; catfish ain't so bad, and no panthers are man-proof.

alexander mackay, a lawyer, sees it differently — no catfish hangover here. he says:

'the mississippi! felt huge emotions being on its waters. as a kid and later, i dreamed of this mighty river, flowing with power through endless lands. it collects waters from everywhere on its way to the ocean. finally here, steaming upstream. looked at it with respect, like everyone does with nature's wonders.'

all about emotions. tourists note the river's loneliness and desolation. captain basil hall saw it flooding, said—

sometimes we floated for miles without a single house in sight. if you were an artist looking for inspiration about a flood, you'd find plenty here.

two hundred years back, la salle, the first bold foreign explorer, finished his long voyage down this great river. parkman tells us:

they got close to their goal. april 6th, the river split into three channels. la salle took the west, d'Autray went east, tonty the middle. as they drifted down, the water turned salty, and the sea breeze hit. then the gulf of mexico spread out before them, vast and empty, like it was when chaos first gave it life.

on solid ground, la salle raised a column with france's arms. the french lined up, chanting while the new england indians watched. muskets fired, cheers went up, and la salle claimed the river and lands for the king. the column read:

LOUIS LE GRAND, ROY DE FRANCE ET DE NAVARRE, REGNE; LE NEUVIEME AVRIL, 1682.

new orleans wanted to celebrate this big anniversary, but the flood hit, and all their energy and money went into dealing with that mess.

CHAPTER 28

uncle mumford unloads

we spent the day drifting downriver, almost alone. before, at this water level, you'd see lumber rafts, coal barges, and trading scows, maybe a traveling theater troupe too. but they were all gone. later, we saw one steamboat resting at the obion river's mouth. through the spyglass, i saw it was named after me. first time i saw that, so i had to mention it, even if i'm late in noticing.

big change at island 21. it was a large island, used to be mid-stream. now it's stuck to the main shore, out of the island business.

heading into Plum Point, it got dark. no big deal now. the government's turned the Mississippi into a lit-up path. lamps at the top and bottom of every crossing. never pitch black. always a light somewhere. almost too many. they even lit up crossings that aren't tricky. a steamboat can handle those on its own once it's through. but it's easier for a pilot to track a lamp than darkness. saves time and money for the boat too.

romance is gone from piloting. snags arent the threat they used to be. snag-boats patrol, yanking out the river's hazards. used to be if you lost control at night, it was nerve-wracking. now hit the electric lights, turn night into day, and you're safe. Bixby and Ritchie mapped the crossings, set courses, and made a lamp for it. you can run through fog now with confidence.

with all these lights, snag-free paths, instant daylight, and charts, piloting's almost as easy as driving a stagecoach. romance factor's dropped to a third.

and now, times keep changing. Anchor Line's put captains above pilots with better pay. pilots gotta stay on watch even when docked. we were once the river's elite. no more naps while loading freight. gotta stay alert in the pilot-house. government took the romance, company took the prestige.

plum point looked like always at night, but now there's beacons for crossings and a bunch of lights from the U.S. River Commission's fleet and a village they set up for their folks. the engineers are trying to remake the Mississippi, which is a hell of a task. they're putting wing-dams to push the current, dikes to narrow it, and more dikes to keep it there. they're clearing timber and sloping banks down, covering them with stones, and protecting shores with piles. anyone who knows the Mississippi knows you can't really tame it, can't tell it where to go, can't stop it from doing what it wants. but folks who know better stay quiet, 'cause those West Point engineers are top-notch, and they think they can handle it. Captain Eads worked miracles with his jetties at the mouth of the river, so maybe it ain't smart to bet against them. still, some might say they'd have better luck trying to boss around comets.

i checked with Uncle Mumford on this and similar stuff. here's what he said, straight from the notes. i left out some bits like 'where in blazes are you going with that barrel now?' that didn't add much to the point. but if i wasn't sure, i let it stay.

UNCLE MUMFORD'S IMPRESSIONS

Uncle Mumford said--

been on this river for thirty years now, watched it, learned it. maybe west point couldve taught me more, but i doubt it. they got their books and schooling, but the river dont care about that. you take one of those european rivers, it's like a playground. wall it, dike it, make it behave. but the mississippi? nah, it does what it wants. they came in here thinking they could control it. but like ecclesiastes says, the river laughs at their plans.

up at devil's island, they tried to make the water go their way with a stone wall. river didn't care, just pushed through. maybe they can fix it up there, but here? down in the lower river? those pegs they drive to keep the banks safe just end up cutting into someone else's land. gonna peg all the banks? might as well build a new river.

theyre pegging bulletin tow-head now. waste of time. if the river wants that land, it's taking it. down a ways, theyve driven piles through a dry bar, trying to do who knows what. whatever it is, it ain't gonna stick. milliken's bend? theyre trying to redirect the river back to vicksburg, like it's some ancient path. think they can turn the whole river upstream. gotta respect the ambition, even if it's a fantasy.

government's pouring money into the mississippi now—snag-free, lit-up, safe as can be. but back when there were thousands of boats, it was all wild. now there's hardly any boats left, and theyre making it all perfect. by the time they're done, the river'll be so tame, it'll feel like sunday school. but right now? _get that hogshead ashore, quit fooling around!_

went down to new orleans, got into some real debates with river folks, planters, journalists, and the river commission guys. opinions everywhere. here's the breakdown:

1. some think the commission should just keep the channel deep, protect shores, all that.
2. others argue, spend the money on the levees, keep 'em strong.
3. then there's the crowd saying higher levees just mean higher river bottoms—so the whole levee idea's a bust.
4. some push the idea of diverting floodwaters into lake borgne to ease the river.
5. others want to boost the river in dry times with northern lake reservoirs.

talk to one guy, he’s sure of his theory. next guy, he’s sure of the opposite. you learn quick, like a reformed crook finding faith—nobody agrees. and you catch their ideas like a cold. facts won’t save you; you’ll still get infected. time to hoist your quarantine flag.

they preach like doctors—cure one theory, leave you with another. a lake-borgne believer hits you with stats, knocks out your old beliefs, but hooks you on his. i’ve caught all five theories, bad too, but can’t say which hit hardest. down there, mississippi improvement’s huge. every guy talks it when he’s not talking war. each theory’s got its fan club, but no clear winner on numbers.

everyone does agree on one thing: congress needs to cough up some serious cash, and it'll do wonders. they did—maybe enough, not too much. we’ll see if the promise holds.

you gotta give it to edward atkinson, his opinions on big national matters mean something. check the appendix for his take on mississippi river improvement.

sometimes, a few figures hit you like lightning, more than words ever could. like this bit from the ‘cincinnati commercial’…

towboat "Jos. B. Williams" is moving down to New Orleans, hauling thirty-two barges loaded with six hundred thousand bushels of coal. At 3 cents a bushel, that freight's hitting $18,000. If we look at rail, eighteen hundred cars needed, each holding three hundred and thirty-three bushels. Rail would run $180,000, so river's saving $162,000 and a whole summer. Fourteen to fifteen days by river, versus a hundred trainloads taking ages.

when a river saves that kinda money and time, even folks who don't trade get why keeping it in shape matters.

CHAPTER 29

A Few Specimen Bricks

we cruised through Plum Point, passed Craighead's Point, and slipped by Fort Pillow. Fort's got a dark history from a massacre in the war. American massacres aren't common like in some other histories, but this one stands out. Even Boston's doesn't compare; gotta dig back to Coeur de Lion to match it.

river's got its quirks too. used to run above Island 37, by Brandywine Bar. Changed its mind, cut through Devil's Elbow to Island 39, reversed some flow, shaving off fifteen miles in 1876. Now, it's Centennial Island.

Island 37 was a hideout for "Murel's Gang"—robbers, horse-thieves, counterfeiters. We heard a lot about Jesse James heading to St. Louis. Papers were all over his assassination. But Murel, he was bigger, bolder, planned insurrections, aimed for New Orleans. James hit small—cars, coaches. Murel was a different beast, preaching sermons with ten hundred men ready to act on his word. James played small-time; Murel was the real deal.

this guy was something else. dressed up as a preacher, gave these sermons that had folks so caught up, they'd forget their horses. meanwhile, his buddies would swipe them. but horse thievery? just the tip of it. the real money was in tricking slaves into running away. they'd convince a slave to run, promising a cut of the sell money and a trip to freedom later. the poor soul would get sold, escape, and it’d happen over and over. by the time they’d squeezed all they could, they'd off him, dump the body in the Mississippi.

even if someone got close to nailing them, they had a loophole. they'd hide the runaway till a reward was posted, then claim they found him. legally, it was breach of trust, not theft. civil courts? useless, cause no one paid up. this Murel guy had an army of a thousand sworn buddies, always ready to back each other up. they split into two groups: the brains, about four hundred, who planned everything, and the muscle, around six hundred fifty, who did the dirty work and got peanuts for it. these strikers were expendable, tossed to the law or the river if needed. they'd hang out on the Arkansas side, hiding the slaves in the swampy areas.

Murel's gang was a slick operation. Everyone knew he was trouble, but nobody could pin him down. Until Stewart came in. This guy was chasing after his slaves that Murel had nabbed. He got tight with Murel, took an oath, and joined the gang's top brass. Then he flipped, spilled the whole deal—names, plans, everything. Murel ended up with a 14-year sentence. Stewart's intel shook things up so much that folks tried to ruin him, even kill him. He had to leave the South. But turns out, he was right all along. Some people knock him for breaking his oath, but nobody doubts his info now.

Here's a bit of what Murel told Stewart when they were on the road. Murel and his crew aimed big—riling up the blacks against the whites, taking New Orleans, and claiming the territory. He gathered his guys in New Orleans, hashed out their plot, then hoofed it to Natchez after selling his horse. Four days on foot, no luck finding a new ride. Fifth day, he spots a traveler on a good horse. Decides he's taking it. Pulls his pistol, makes the guy get off, and leads him away. Has him strip to his shirt and drawers, turns him around. Guy asks to pray, Murel says no time. Fires the shot.

man, i found myself in a real mess that day. ripped open his belly, grabbed the guts, and dropped him in the creek. pockets had some cash, $400 and change, and a bunch of papers i didnt bother with. sunk the papers and his hat too. boots were nice and new, fit me well, so i traded out my old shoes and dumped them in the creek. packed up his clothes—good stuff—in his bag. got on a horse, finest id ridden in days, and headed for Natchez, feeling way better about my situation.

me and Crenshaw picked up four good horses, aimed for Georgia. met a young guy from South Carolina just before Cumberland Mountain. Crenshaw knew his story quick—guy went to Tennessee for hogs, but prices were up. we figured he was an easy target. Crenshaw gave me a look; i got it. he'd traveled these roads, i hadn't. when we got near a cliff, Crenshaw asked for my whip, which had a lead weight. handed it over, and he knocked the guy off his horse. we grabbed his pockets, found $1262. Crenshaw knew where to stash him, so we dragged him to a crevice and dropped him in. took his $200 horse too.

got held up a bit, and while we waited, our friend found out a negro we had was advertised in a nearby village. things got dicey, but we moved the negro to a creek by our friend's farm, and Crenshaw shot him. gutted him, sunk him.

Crenshaw sold another negro three times on the Arkansaw River, made over $500 each time, then had him hidden away in a swamp. that was the end of it; sold him for nearly $2000 total and made sure no one would ever find him. his remains are probably long gone by now, feeding whatever's in that creek.

as we got closer to Memphis, we were right where one of the big Civil War river battles happened. i served under two guys from that fight. Mr. Bixby was head pilot for the Union, and Montgomery led the Confederates. both were tough and smart, saw plenty of action, and earned their reputations.

as we got close to memphis, we started thinking of reasons to stay with the 'gold dust' all the way to vicksburg. we were settled in nice, didn’t want to switch it up. i had some important stuff to handle in napoleon, arkansas, but maybe i could work it out without leaving the 'gold dust.' mentioned that, so we decided to stick around.

the boat would stay in memphis till ten the next morning. memphis sits pretty on a bluff over the river. streets run straight and wide, though not paved to impress. what does impress is the sewer system—top-notch now, thanks to lessons learned from a yellow-fever outbreak years back. back then, folks were dropping in hundreds, thousands even; the population shrank to a quarter of what it was. business nearly stopped, and the streets looked like a ghost town.

here's a snapshot of memphis back then from a german tourist, ernst von hesse-wartegg, in 'mississippi-fahrten':

'by august, yellow fever was at its peak. hundreds died daily. the city became a graveyard, two-thirds of the people fled, leaving the poor, old, and sick. houses were shut, lamps outside marked death inside. sometimes, several dead in one house; black crape at the windows. stores closed since owners left or died.

'a slight ailment, then fever, delirium, and—yellow death! sick and dead lay on streets, food spoiled fast, turned black in the air.

'cries from houses, then silence; men came with coffins to carry them away. nights were quiet, only doctors and hearses moving, and distant trains thundering past the city without stopping.'

but memphis is alive now. over forty thousand people and growing, business booming. we drove around, checked out the park with friendly squirrels, saw nice rose-covered homes, and had a good breakfast at the hotel.

memphis is bustling: big in wholesale trade, foundries, machine shops, making wagons, carriages, and cotton-seed oil, soon cotton mills and elevators. last year, cotton receipts hit five hundred thousand bales, up sixty thousand from the year before. five main railways run out, with a sixth on the way.

this memphis ain't like the one tourists wrote about ages ago. back in the days of Mrs. Trollope, it was mostly just a long street with log houses and some cabins by the woods. a pig here, a lot of mud there. she wrote about stopping at a hotel, definitely not the one we had breakfast at. she said the table was set for fifty, everyone ate fast and silent, just knives, forks, and coughing.

that 'etc.' she used? doesn't hide much. she also described a steamboat dinner with some rich planters, all titles and no manners. no table courtesies, just grabbing food, strange talk, spitting everywhere, eating with knives, then cleaning teeth with pocket knives. not exactly a dinner to enjoy.

CHAPTER 30

Sketches by the Way

the river below memphis was huge, overflowing and covering miles of land, ruining hard work. a sad sight over hundreds of miles. beacon lights were often in deep water. keepers had to row in, rain or shine, sometimes it was the women doing the job if the men were away. the government paid a bit for the work, ten or fifteen bucks a month.

the ship island area is still woodsy. the island's now part of the mainland, with wagons where steamboats used to go. no trace of the 'Pennsylvania' wreck anymore. one day a farmer might dig it up by accident.

we were entering the migrating negro region. couldn't travel when they were slaves, so now they move around. pack up, catch a steamboat, and go. doesn't matter where, just want to be on the move. how far depends on their cash. fifty miles or less, doesn't matter.

for a couple days, we kept answering these hails. you'd see a bunch of old cabins, high-water stains all over, and mostly just black folks around; no white folks. ground's dry in spots, but mostly flooded. trees down, and what cattle, mules, and horses there are, they're just gnawing on bark and leaves. no better food in sight. sometimes just one cabin and a family, all piled up on what they could save. rusty gun, some bedding, tinware, an old mirror, a rocking chair, and a pack of sorry-looking dogs tied up with string. they won't leave the dogs behind. but those dogs, they aren't having it. they get dragged aboard, four legs stiff, sliding all the way, heads nearly pulled off. and every now and then, a kid gets left on the bank, but not a dog.

pilot-house chatter was the usual. island no. 63 used to have a nice passage behind it. story goes, jesse jamieson on the 'skylark' had this old washed-up pilot with him. left him at the wheel at 63 to take a break. old guy just kept looping through the chute and back around, over and over. three hours later, he handed the boat over right where he started. there's a guy on shore watching, thought he saw a whole parade of skylarks.

then there's the 'eclipse,' known for speed. one day it passed, but an old guy on shore didn't notice. someone asked him if any boat went by. he said, "yeah, just loafin' along." they told him it was the 'eclipse,' and he changed his tune right quick. "must've been sparklin' by!"

another tale, bit more violent. early in the high water, A's fence rails ended up on B's land, and vice versa. A said, "let's just use each other's." B didn't like that. one day, A goes to get his rails and B threatens him with a revolver. A says he's unarmed. B, aiming to keep things fair, tosses the gun, then pulls a knife and slashes A's throat. A finds the gun, shoots B dead, and somehow survives his own injuries. wild folks down here.

more gossip, then everyone went below for afternoon coffee, leaving me at the wheel. something made me think about our last hour in st. louis, where i spent time on this boat's hurricane deck. a stranger joined me, a young guy from wisconsin. he'd never seen a steamboat till a week ago. he claimed he'd learned everything about his boat from stem to rudder-blade on the trip down from la crosse. asked where i was from. told him new england. 'oh, a yank!' he said, chatting without a care if i agreed. then he insisted on showing me around, naming parts of the boat and explaining their uses. before i could protest, he was off, misnaming things and enjoying his little game at my expense. his imagination ran wild, and he kept stepping aside to hide his laughter. i let him finish his comedy, and when he was done, he offered to fill in any gaps in my 'learning.' i said i'd seek his wisdom and left. later, i saw him alone, laughing himself sick. he wasn't seen publicly for days after.

what reminded me of this while alone at the wheel was seeing him at the pilot-house door, inspecting me with a wounded look. he just stood there, silent and reproachful, then left. came back again with the same look, and finally said, 'you let me learn you all about a steamboat, didn't you?' i admitted it. 'yes, you did--didn't you?' he said. tried to say something more, but words failed him. he just swore and left. saw him a few times later during the trip, but he avoided me. if he hadn't been so eager with his prank, i might've steered him clear of that silly impoliteness.

i set my alarm for the four o'clock watch. summer sunrises on the Mississippi, man, can't see too many of those. first thing is the silence. it's deep. makes you feel like you're miles away from all the noise of life. dawn sneaks in, black forest turns gray, river stretches out like a mirror. no wind, no leaves moving, just perfect stillness. then a bird starts up, then more. you don't see 'em, just hear a whole world of song around you. light gets stronger, greens turn from dark to light, until they're just mist on the horizon. river's a mirror for it all. then the sun throws pink, gold, purple around, and you know it's something to remember.

early morning, we hit the Kentucky Bend country. place with a past. Captain Poe's boat sank quick here one night, snagged at the head. he axed through the roof to get to his wife, but the boards were rotten. first swing went right through, and well, it was tragic.

that bend's filled up now, cut-off did it. same thing took Walnut Bend and dumped it into the quiet.

stopped by Helena and a new place, Arkansas City. popped up thanks to a railway. asked a local what it was like. he said, 'it's a hell of a place.' pretty spot-on. rows of shabby houses, mud everywhere, left from the overflow. ponds in the streets, scows grounded where the waters dropped. but it's growing, got good land, an elevator, and a big cotton-seed oil mill. first time i saw one of those.

cotton-seed used to be worthless, now it's $12-13 a ton. All of it's used. They make oil from it — colorless, tasteless, almost no smell. Folks say you can tweak it to mimic other oils and make it cheaper than the originals. Some smart ones shipped it to Italy, fixed it up, slapped on an olive oil label, and brought it back. Got so big, Italy had to slap a tax on it to protect their own oil biz.

helena sits pretty on the Mississippi. Last hills on that side. Normally nice, but the flood or seep water hit it hard. Streets got muddy water, buildings stained. Scows left around, sidewalks on stilts still there. Ground-level walks are wrecked. Two guys on them, you'd think a cavalry charge was coming. Mud's deep, lots of stagnant water. Floods here almost as bad as fires.

we had a good time on this sunny Sunday. Two hours on land while the boat unloaded. Back streets had few white folks, but plenty of black women and girls dressed bright and fancy. Big contrast to the mud and puddles.

helena’s the second biggest town in Arkansas, pop. around five thousand. The land's real fertile. Good cotton trade, 40-60k bales a year. Big lumber and grain too. Foundry, oil mills, machine shops, wagon factories — $1,000,000 in manufacturing. Two railways. It's the commercial hub of a rich area. New Orleans 'Times-Democrat' says $4,000,000 comes in annually.

CHAPTER 31

A thumb-print and what came of it

we were near Napoleon, Arkansas. Got me thinking about my task. Time, noonday; bright and sunny. Bad timing — not a daytime kind of task. The more I thought, the more it bugged me. Finally asked myself: is it smart to do this in daylight? Little discomfort and you get night for it, no nosy eyes. That settled it. Clear question and answer cuts through most problems.

got my friends in my room, told them sorry, but we should get off and stay in Napoleon. They were not happy. Language got rough. Their main point? "but you agreed to stay on this boat." Like deciding to do one dumb thing means you gotta do another by sticking to it.

i tried all sorts of ways to calm them down, and it mostly worked. felt like i had their attention, so i dove into the backstory to make it clear this mess wasn't my doing:

late last year, i was in munich, bavaria for a bit. november had me at fraulein dahlweiner's pension, 1a, karlstrasse. but i worked a mile away at a widow's place who took in lodgers. she and her kids would chat german with me every morning, as requested. one day, wandering the city, i found myself at one of the government's corpse watch stations. they keep bodies there to make sure they're actually dead and not just in a trance. weird place, spacious room, thirty-six adult bodies laid out in rows on slanted boards, wrapped in white shrouds. faces pale as wax. alcoves had kids, faces and hands showing through the flowers. each had a ring on a finger, wire leading up to a bell in a watch-room. there's a guy there always ready for action if a corpse moves and rings the bell. imagined myself as that guy, alone on some stormy night, jumping out of my skin at the bell's ring. asked what happens if it rings? got scolded for poking around in such a serious place. left feeling a bit sheepish.

next morning, i told the widow what happened. she lit up and said, "come with me. got a lodger who used to be a night-watchman there."

he was alive, but barely looked it. propped up in bed, face colorless and sunken. widow tried to introduce me, but he waved us off. then she mentioned i was an american. his mood flipped, suddenly lively, eager even. soon it was just him and me.

i started in my clunky german. he replied in smooth english. german didn't get another word in.

me and this consumptive guy got tight. showed up every day, talked about all sorts of stuff, except wives and kids. mention one, and his eyes'd light up real soft-like for a sec, then that hard look returned. he'd go silent, totally checked out, like nothing else existed. id eventually leave without a goodbye.

after two months, Karl Ritter goes, 'I'll tell you my story.'

A DYING MAN'S CONFESSION

he said, 'i never gave up till now. but now, it's time. decided last night. you're gonna visit your river soon, right? when you do, you'll hit Napoleon, Arkansas. i need you to do something there for me. you'll get it after hearing my story.

we'll keep it short. you know why i came to America, and why i ended up in the South. but you dont know i had a wife. beautiful, kind, pure. and our little girl, just like her. happiest days.

one night, war nearly over, i wake up tied and gagged, room stinks of chloroform. see two guys. one says, 'told her i'd do it if she made noise. and the kid...'

other guy, half-crying, 'we were just gonna rob them, not hurt them.'

'plans changed when they woke. you did your part, now help rummage.'

they're masked, in ragged clothes. one guy's missing a thumb on his right hand. they search the cabin. main guy says, 'waste of time—he'll tell us where it's hid. undo his gag.'

other guy agrees, 'no clubbing.'

they approach, then voices and hoofbeats outside. they freeze, listen. a shout—'Hello, the house! Show a light, we need water.'

'captain's voice!' main guy says, and they bolt through the back, lantern off.

the group outside shouts again, rides off. never heard more.

couldnt break free. gag was tight, no sound. listened hard for my wife and kid, but nothing. silence just got heavier. imagine that for three hours. clock ticking felt like years. kept struggling. finally, dawn, got loose and stretched. room was a mess—robbers tore through it looking for cash. spotted a document, blood on it. stumbled to the other end. my family, gone. their troubles over, mine just starting.

law? nah, it wont heal this. laws cant settle this score. id collect my debt myself. how? no faces, no voices, but i was sure. had a clue—worthless to most, but not to me. first, i figured those robbers were soldiers, not just tramps. one said, "the captain's voice, by G--!" my target. nearby regiments, cavalry too. heard Captain Blakely passed by that night. kept quiet, but i knew where to look. described the robbers as tramps to mislead folks. only i suspected the soldiers.

at home, made a disguise from old clothes. bought blue goggles in town. when Company C moved to Napoleon, i was ready. hid my cash, left at night. by the time they arrived, i was already there. new trade: fortune-teller. mingled with all the companies, but focused on Company C. did favors, took risks, even let them joke at my expense. became their favorite.

found a soldier missing a thumb early on—man, that lit a fire under me. when i realized he was the only one without a thumb in the whole group, i knew i was onto something. his name was Kruger, a German. nine Germans in the unit. watched to see who he hung around with, but he seemed to keep to himself. i got close, made sure we became buddies. sometimes i wanted my revenge so bad it hurt, but i held back, didn’t beg him to name the man who killed my family. kept my mouth shut, kept telling fortunes when i could.

my setup was real basic: some red paint, a bit of white paper. painted the thumb, got a print, studied it that night, told fortunes the next day. why this charade? well, an old French prison-keeper once told me the lines on a thumb never change, from birth to death. each thumb’s lines unique. these days we use photos in the Rogues' Gallery, but he collected thumbprints. said disguises could fool a camera, but not a thumb. he proved it, too.

kept telling fortunes. every night, alone, i’d study those thumbprints with a magnifying glass. imagine the intensity, comparing them to the thumb-and-finger-marks from that unknown murderer, stained with the most precious blood to me. kept muttering, 'will they never match?'

but the payoff came. the forty-third guy, Private Franz Adler, matched. didn’t know his name, voice, face, or nationality before, but now i knew it all. trusted the Frenchman’s method. but needed confirmation. had a print of Kruger's left thumb. took him aside, away from everyone, and said gravely—

'there's a dark part of your fortune i couldn’t share in public. you and Private Adler murdered a woman and child. you're being hunted: both of you will be killed within five days.'

he fell to his knees, scared out of his mind, repeating over and over, half-crying like i remembered from that night—

'i didn't do it; i swear i didn't; tried to stop him, as god is my witness. he did it alone.'

that’s all i needed. tried to shake him off, but he clung, begging me to save him from the killer. he said—

got this guy, Kruger, all panicked. he's got ten grand stashed, says it's from stealing. wants me to help him out, offering me the whole pot—even though most of it's his cousin Adler's. they've hidden it together but Kruger moved it without telling Adler. he's plotting to run off with a woman who's supposed to bring the gold. too heavy to run with, he says. if he can't tell her where it is, he's got a plan with his watch—slip it to her, and she’ll find a note inside with the details. tries to give me the watch, shaking with fear.

then Adler shows up, not far off. i tell Kruger to keep his watch, no harm will come. told him to keep quiet about it, especially to Adler. he left, nervous but thankful. then i spun Adler a long fortune, so long i couldn't finish. promised to tell him the critical part later, when he'd be alone on guard—no eavesdroppers. they had these picket-watches, just for show, no real threat nearby.

around midnight, i head out with the countersign, making my way to where Adler's on watch. dark as anything, i almost trip over him before we speak. i tell him it’s just me, the fortune-teller. got close and drove my dirk into him. that was the tragic end of his fortune, alright. as he fell, he grabbed my goggles, and his horse dragged him off, dead.

i ran through the woods, got away, but left those goggles behind, clutched in his hand.

that was about fifteen, sixteen years ago. since then, i've wandered, working sometimes, broke other times. always tired, mission done that night. only thing that's kept me going is this thought every day: 'i have killed him!'

four years back, my health started going downhill. ended up in Munich, aimless. broke, so i found work. stuck it out for a year, then got the night watchman gig in that dead-house. fit my mood. liked it there. liked the quiet with the dead. i’d wander through the corpses, staring at their faces for hours. the later it got, the more i liked it. sometimes dimmed the lights to let my imagination run. those rows of the dead, they got to me in a weird way. 

two years ago, one cold stormy night, i was half-asleep in the watch-room. wind moaning, shutters banging away, barely heard it as i drifted off. then that damn dead-bell rings out above me! scared the hell out of me; first time i ever heard it.

i snapped awake, rushed to the corpse-room. halfway down the line, a body was sitting up, head wagging side to side. real nightmare. i got closer... it was Adler!

know what hit me first? “you got away once, not happening again.”

poor guy was terrified. waking in that silence, staring out at those dead bodies. his face lit up when he saw me. more so when he saw the cordials i had. then the horror when i hid them and said—

“speak up, Franz Adler—call on these dead. maybe they’ll listen and help; but not here, not from me.”

he tried to talk, but the shroud was tight. tried to raise his hands, but they were bound on his chest. i said—

shout, franz adler; wake up the whole street and get help quick. shout—don't waste time, you got none. what, you can't? shame, but it doesn't always bring help. remember when you and your cousin murdered a woman and child in arkansas? my wife and kid. they screamed, but it didn't help them, did it? your teeth are chattering, why not shout? loosen the bandages, you could. oh, hands tied. can't help yourself. funny how things repeat. my hands were tied that night. you didn't think to untie me, and i won't untie you. shh, someone's coming. footsteps close. one, two, three. it's just outside. now's your chance! shout, man, shout! last shot between you and forever. too late. it's gone. think on it—you've heard your last footstep. strange, isn't it, to hear something so normal and know you won't again.

watching your pain was something else. i thought up a new torment, threw in some lies—'poor kruger tried to save them, and i helped him rob you. got him out safe.' saw surprise and triumph on your face, through the hurt. made me uneasy. i asked—'he didn't get away?'

you shook your head no.

'what happened?'

your face showed more satisfaction. tried to say something—couldn't. tried to communicate with your hands—failed. then you tilted your head toward the corpse near you.

'dead?' i asked. 'caught and shot?'

shook your head no again.

'how, then?'

you tried with your hands again. i watched close. bent down, watched harder. you twisted your thumb, poked weakly at your chest. 'stabbed?'

nodded yes, with a wicked smile that lit up my brain. i yelled—'did i stab him, thinking he was you? meant only for you.'

your nod was full of joy, as much as you could muster.

'oh, miserable me, killed the one who stood by my darlings. would've saved them if he could! miserable, miserable me!'

thought i heard a muffled mocking laugh. looked up, saw my enemy sinking back.

He took his sweet time dying. Guy had this crazy resilience. Just wouldn't quit. Got myself a chair and a paper, sat down next to him. Occasionally sipped some brandy, partly for the cold, partly cause every time I reached for the bottle, he thought it was for him. Read out loud about folks getting yanked back to life with some booze and a warm bath. He hung in there for three hours and six minutes from when he rang his bell.

Folks say no one's ever rung the bell in those Bavarian dead-houses over 18 years. Well, let 'em believe it. Doesn't hurt anyone.

That room's chill got to me, worsened my own sickness, which had been on the mend. That man killed my family; in three days, he'd add me to his list. But, oh man, catching him trying to escape his grave and shoving him back—worth it.

Was laid up for a week after, but once I was up, checked the dead-house records for Adler's place. Dump of a lodging-house. Figured he'd have Kruger's stuff, being his cousin. Wanted Kruger's watch. But Adler's things were sold off while I was sick. Just some letters left, nothing worthwhile. Tracked down Kruger's only living son through those letters. He's 30 now, shoemaker, living in Mannheim with his kids. Didn't tell him why, but I've been covering two-thirds of his expenses since.

About that watch—funny how things go. Chased it all over Germany for over a year, spent a lot and got annoyed. Finally found it. Opened it—empty. Should've known that paper wouldn't stick around. Gave up on that ten grand; wanted it for Kruger's son.

Last night, when I accepted I was dying, started burning useless papers. And what do you know, from Adler's stuff, out falls that scrap I've been after. Recognized it right away. Here's what it says, translated:

'Brick livery stable, stone foundation, middle of town, corner of Orleans and Market. Corner toward Courthouse. Third stone, fourth row. Stick notice there, saying how many are to come.'

There you go—keep it safe. Kruger said that stone's removable, north wall, fourth row from the top, third from the west. Money's behind it. Last line's just to throw off anyone who finds it. Probably fooled Adler too.

now when you head down the river, make sure you find that hidden cash. send it to Adam Kruger at the Mannheim address i gave you. it'll set him up for life. knowing i've done right by the son of the man who tried to save my family—that's peace for me, even if my hands got it all wrong before.

CHAPTER 32

The Disposal of a Bonanza

'that's Ritter's story,' i told my buddies. they sat quiet for a long time, taking it all in. then they erupted with questions and comments about the wild twists in the tale. this went on until we ran out of steam. finally, we all fell into a lull. after ten minutes, Rogers muttered, 'ten thousand dollars.' and added, 'it's a lot of money.'

the poet asked, 'sending it all to him right away?'

'yeah,' i said. 'why ask?'

no answer. after a bit, Rogers hesitated, 'all of it?'

'yeah, all of it.'

i would've said more, but a thought cut me off. Thompson spoke, but i missed it. Rogers replied, 'seems plenty to me. don't see what he's done for it.'

soon the poet said, 'really, more than enough. five grand—he'd never spend it all! might even wreck him; you should think on that. he'd waste it, close shop, maybe start drinking, neglect kids, spiral down.'

'right,' Rogers jumped in. 'i've seen it. give a guy like that money, and it'll ruin him. no question. if it doesn't, i don't know people.'

'give him even a third,' Thompson added, 'in less than six months—'

'six weeks,' i interrupted. 'unless three grand's locked up safe, he wouldn't last six weeks.'

'no way,' said Thompson. 'edited books for folks like him. they get a royalty check, two grand maybe—'

'what's a shoemaker doing with two grand?' Rogers cut in. 'he's happy now, working hard, living honestly. you throw fifteen hundred at him, say—'

'it's like fifteen hundred devils!' i snapped. 'even five hundred would rot his values, kill his work ethic, drag him down to the gutter.'

"Why're we forcing this on ourselves, guys?" the poet cut in, all serious. "He's happy where he is. Honor, charity, true kindness all say leave him be. That's real friendship. Sure, we could do something flashy, but nothing kinder or wiser." 

After more talk, we all felt uneasy. We knew we should send the poor shoemaker something. We hashed it out and decided on a chromo.

Once that was settled, a new issue popped up: those two guys thought they'd split the money with me. Not my plan. Told 'em if they got half between 'em, they'd be lucky. Rogers shot back, "none of us would've had any if it wasn't for me. I kicked it off—otherwise, the shoemaker gets it all."

Thompson claimed he was thinking the same before Rogers spoke. I said I'd have figured it out on my own. Slow thinker maybe, but sure.

That turned into a fight; we all ended up pretty banged up. After patching myself up, I went up to the hurricane deck, still sour. Found Captain McCord and said, as nicely as I could, "I'm saying goodbye, captain. I want to get off at Napoleon."

"Go ashore where?" he asked.

"Napoleon."

The captain laughed but stopped when he saw I wasn't joking. "Are you serious?"

"Serious? Yeah."

He glanced up at the pilot-house. "He wants off at Napoleon!"

"Napoleon?"

"That's what he says."

"Great Caesar's ghost!"

Uncle Mumford came over. "Uncle, your friend here wants off at Napoleon!"

"Well, by—?"

I said, "Come on, what's this about? Can't I go ashore at Napoleon?"

"Don't you know? There's no Napoleon anymore. Arkansas River wiped it out, dumped it into the Mississippi!"

"Carried the whole town away? Banks, churches, everything?"

"Everything. Fifteen-minute job. Only left a shanty bit and a brick chimney. We're floating right over what used to be the town center; that brick chimney's all that's left. Those woods used to be a mile back."

Looking back, I recognized it. "Most amazing thing I've heard, by far."

Thompson and Rogers showed up with their bags, heard the captain’s news. Thompson handed me a half-dollar, "For my share of the chromo." Rogers did the same.

seeing the Mississippi roll right over where a big town used to be is a trip. twenty years ago, it was the county-seat, had a big marine hospital, and more fights than you could count. even knew the prettiest girl in the Mississippi Valley there. but now? gone. just a bit of a shanty and a chimney left.

so, Island 74's a mess. Arkansas and Mississippi can't agree on the river line. Arkansas had it, then some river cut-off left it stuck in between. no taxes, no state. one guy owns it, living like he’s got no country.

Island 92 shifted too. Arkansas island now joined with Mississippi. some guy set up a whiskey shop, making bank off Mississippi folks but under Arkansas rules where licenses didn't matter.

river's quiet. nothing moving, just trees and the odd cabin. folks keep moving them back as the river eats the shore. like at Pilcher's Point, cabins moved 300 yards in three months, but the river's still winning.

Napoleon used to sneer at Greenville, Mississippi. but now Napoleon's gone, and Greenville's booming. three thousand people, $2.5 million annual trade. town's growing.

on the boat, talk’s about the Calhoun Land Company. Colonel Calhoun, statesman’s grandson, set up a Boston syndicate to buy 10,000 acres in Arkansas for cotton. they’re going cash-only, supplying workers at a small profit, encouraging savings. if it works, they're planning a bank in Greenville, lending at 6%. seems like it might just take off.

planters and steamboat guys gripe about the same thing. planters own the land but no cash. they have to put up their land and crop as collateral. so the commission dealer fronts the money but wants a fat cut — 10% interest and another 2.5% for setting up the deal. planter has to buy everything through this dealer too, paying extra for commissions. when the crop ships, more commissions, insurance, the works. in total, dealer's taking about 25% of the crop's value. edward atkinson asked what the state can do when folks face 18-30% interest plus buying crops before planting at 100% profit margins. 

a cotton planter figures his profit like this: one guy and a mule can work ten acres, make ten bales worth $500. costs him $350 to do it, nets $150, or $15 an acre. used to be cotton-seed wasn't worth much, especially if you had to haul it far. but now? in 1600 lbs of crude cotton, 400 lbs is lint at 10 cents a pound. 1200 lbs of seed worth $12-$13 a ton. maybe we'll find a use for the stems too. atkinson points out there's 1500 lbs of stems per bale, rich in nutrients. grind them up with ensilage or cotton-seed meal, and it's good feed for livestock. stems used to just be a pain.

planters still sore about the past, keep it all business with former slaves. they won’t run a store to help them out, so they let that go to a shrewd dealer who pushes the workers to buy on credit. end of season, they’re in debt, discouraged, and likely to leave, hurting both them and the planter. hope is the calhoun company will lead by treating workers well, showing that method is best for everyone. if it works, others might follow.

barkeeper's got a lot to say. watches folks, doesn't drink himself. tries to make his keep, but not enough folks buying. says folks in Mississippi and Louisiana would rather buy veggies up north than grow their own. they come to the landings and grab fruit from him. figures they only know cotton. says most don't know squat about growing fruit and veg. 'a nigger will go to H for a watermelon' — not sure what 'H' means, but seems a long way for fruit. buys melons cheap upriver, sells 'em for ten times that. why mix fancy drinks for the boat hands? they won't take anything else. want a big drink, don't care what's in it, just want their money's worth. offer a plain shot of good brandy for cheap, won't touch it. but throw together a big, colorful mix and they're sold.

all the Anchor Line bars rented to one firm. they supply the booze, pay the barkeepers a salary. some boats get good stuff, where passengers want and can afford it. others? just the deck hands and firemen drinking. 'brandy? sure, got lots, but you'd better have your affairs in order first.' not like the old days. back then, everyone took steamboats, everyone drank, everyone bought rounds. now most travel by rail, and the rest don't drink. back then, barkeepers owned their bars, were showy and sociable, decked out in jewels, made a killing. a steamboat bar was a goldmine inheritance. now? just covers room and board, if you're lucky. times have changed. up on the Upper Mississippi, some lines don't even have a bar. sounds like a story, but it's the cold truth.

CHAPTER 34

Tough Yarns

stack island. remember that place. also Lake Providence, Louisiana — first real Southern town heading downstream. flat and low, trees draped in Spanish moss. Uncle Mumford calls it 'restful, pensive, Sunday vibe.' he's right.

met this guy, Mr. H., on the boat. steamboat mate, so he knows the river. he's from Arkansas City, heading to Vicksburg to join his boat. serious dude, not your typical river man. he had this thing about mosquitoes in Arkansas. said the reputation scared people off, hurting property values. made them out like they were harmless, almost like talking about family. but when he got to the mosquitoes at Lake Providence, he changed his tune. called them “colossi,” said they could take down a dog or a man if they wanted. even joked about needing a mosquito policy with your life insurance there. apparently, he’d seen them try to vote—backed off that one a bit, but swore he'd seen them around polls, “canvassing.”

another guy with Mr. H. had his own mosquito tales. H. kept cutting him off, telling him to trim the stories down. “cut it down, cut it down,” he'd say. said he once heard such a big lie from this guy that his ear swelled up for months. people came just to see him fan himself with it.

CHAPTER 35

Vicksburg During the Trouble

used to sail by Vicksburg down the river. now can't do that. a cut-off turned it into a country town like Osceola. no current in front anymore, just a big island. you come down the river on the other side and turn back up if the water's high. low water? gotta land way below the town.

vicksburg's still got the marks of those war days. earthworks, trees busted up by cannonballs, caves in the clay banks. those caves saved lives during the big bombardment from may 8 to july 4, 1863. folks who didn't fight—mostly women and kids—used them for shelter. not living there constantly but running when the shells started flying. the caves were just holes, tunnels in the clay, branching out like a Y.

city had 27,000 soldiers and 3,000 non-combatants. cut off from the world—gunboats in front, soldiers and cannons in back. no trade, no travel, no news, no steamboats coming or going. everything was quiet. flour cost $200 a barrel, sugar $30, corn $10 a bushel, bacon $5 a pound. rum? $100 a gallon. streets were dead; no noise, no movement. at 3 am, silence so deep you could hear a sentry's footsteps forever away. then, boom—artillery shakes the ground. sky's a mess of red lines from shells, iron rain falling. streets empty at first, then full of women and kids running to the caves, soldiers shouting 'Rats, to your holes!' and laughing.

shelling goes on for hours, then stops. silence again. people peek out, stretch, breathe the air, chat with neighbors, maybe head home or stroll around if it stays quiet. they'll be back in the caves when it all kicks off again.

just 3,000 cave folks—small village. they'd get to know each other pretty well after a while, sharing stories and all.

history's got all the pieces you need. now, could someone who wasn't there in Vicksburg paint a clearer picture than a local who lived it? seems unlikely, but let's think about it. first time on a ship, everything's new and sharp, sticks in the memory. you can make a landsman feel it with you. but after ten voyages, it all blurs together, loses its edge. nothing left to excite a landsman.

i talked to a couple from Vicksburg once. they lived through it but told their story flat, like they were over it. six weeks of bombings wore them down. what was once amazing just became life. their story wasn't going to wow anyone. the man said something like this:

man, it felt like every day was Sunday. just a week of Sundays for us, you know? nothing to do, time dragging on. then out of nowhere, the sky would explode with fire and thunder and iron. at first, we’d scramble for cover like mad. i remember forgetting the kids once, Maria grabbed them both and fainted once she got to safety. a few weeks later, she was running through a shell shower and got hit with dirt from a shell burst. lost her wig but she stopped to grab it before moving on. guess we were all getting used to it. 

we got pretty good at recognizing shells by sound. if it wasn’t a direct threat, we’d just keep talking. when it got too close, we’d freeze, then keep chatting once it passed. sometimes, a big shell would hang up there, and we'd all just nod and say “see you later” before finding cover. even the ladies would stroll around, casually keeping an eye on the sky, stopping only if they were unsure about a shell. if the coast was clear, they’d keep going.

the streets weren’t littered with paper and trash like normal places. ours had iron scraps everywhere. some folks would collect the shell fragments and make little yard monuments out of them—sometimes a ton of it. no glass left in windows; they couldn’t take the pounding. just empty holes like skull eyes.

we had church on Sundays. not many folks at first, but more came over time. sometimes, service would pause for the noise outside. it was a strange mix, church music with explosions. once, leaving church, i was shaking hands with a friend, telling him to visit our cave after the bombardment. we had a pint of whiskey, rare as anything. just as i said that, a shell hit, took his arm right off. and you know what stuck with me? thinking “at least the whiskey’s saved.” sounds mean, but with whiskey scarce as diamonds, you’d get it. never had another drop during the siege.

caves got crammed sometimes, felt like a sauna. packed with twenty, twenty-five folks. air thick enough to choke a candle. imagine a kid born in there—like being born in a suitcase.

twice we had sixteen in ours. a dozen more times. felt like drowning in heat. eight was the norm, but the hunger, sickness, fear, none left as they came. only three of us survived the next couple of years. then a shell blew, blocked us in. digging out was a fight for air. after that, we dug two openings. should've done it sooner.

mule meat? yeah, last resort. not bad when you're starving.

one guy kept a diary—six days, not weeks. first day, eight pages. second, five. third, one, loose. fourth, a few lines. fifth and sixth, a couple lines. seventh, gave up. life in Vicksburg became routine.

Vicksburg's war tale is gripping. siege, mines, assaults, famine. held out longer than any river-town, saw it all.

most beautiful cemetery's here. gateway inscription: "HERE REST IN PEACE 16,600 WHO DIED FOR THEIR COUNTRY IN THE YEARS 1861 TO 1865." high ground, broad terraces, winding paths, wild-wood untouched. everything shows the government's touch—solid and neat.

drove out to the surrender monument. new one's metal, old marble one got hacked up. brick foundation's failing, it'll fall eventually. overlooks hills and ravines, covered in flowering weeds. old marble piece's in the cemetery now.

on the way back, met an old guy with a bomb-shell in his yard since the siege. "dog wanted a fight with it, but not me. told it to stay put or blow up, i had business in the woods."

vicksburg's got these big business streets and nice houses. runs trade for the yazoo and sunflower rivers. they're pushing railways out in all directions through rich farmland. town's got a bright future. seems like all the river towns are betting on railroads now. they're acting on it. next twenty years could be big — more people, more money, more ideas. but if history's any hint, they'll find ways to slow themselves down. back in steamboat days, they messed up with wharfage fees so high, small trade wasn't worth it. boats would've brought trade, but towns pushed them away with costs. 

we wanted to check out the yazoo and sunflower, especially with the big flood up there. but we couldn't wait around for a new orleans boat back, so had to skip it.

picked up a story on the boat that night. doesn't fit here, but it's good. a passenger, college professor, told it. started talking horses, then astronomy, lynchings in vicksburg, dreams, superstitions, and ended up arguing free trade after midnight.

chapter 36: the professor's yarn

back in the day, i wasn't a professor. just a young land-surveyor, open world ahead. had a contract to survey a mining-ditch route in california. on a sea trip, three or four weeks. lots of passengers, but i kept to myself, reading and dreaming. three gamblers on board—rough types. never talked to them, but saw them often. they gambled in a stateroom, door cracked for smoke and curses. couldn't stand them, but had to deal.

there was this other passenger, john backus, who seemed keen on being friendly. he was hard to shake without stepping on his feelings, and i wasn’t out to do that. there was something endearing about his simple country ways. first time i laid eyes on him, i pegged him as a grazier or farmer from the ohio backwoods just by his looks and clothes. when he confirmed he was a cattle-raiser from ohio, i felt a bit pleased with myself for calling it right.

he’d join me on my morning walks, chatting away about his life, business, family—everything about the backus clan. in return, he learned my trade secrets, my plans, probably more than i’d usually share. once, i mentioned triangulation, and he latched onto it, calling me "triangle" from then on.

he loved cattle. mention a bull or cow, and he’d light up, talking breeds and their virtues. i’d just nod along until i managed to steer the chat to science, where i’d get my turn to shine.

one day, he hesitated a bit and asked, "triangle, can we chat in my stateroom?" curious, i followed him. he checked the coast was clear, locked the door, and said, "got a proposition. it’s business, right? we both know there’s money in californy cattle. i got ten grand here, saved over the years. you survey the line to make sure those ‘gores’ hit good land. i stock ‘em, cash rolls in, and we split it."

i had to cut him off. "look," i said, "i can’t do that."

not my kind of surveyor. let's move on, backus.

felt bad for him. he was all tangled up, stammering apologies. didn't even realize he'd stepped in it. tried to steer him clear, chat him up about cattle and butchery. we were docked at acapulco, and just then, they started hoisting cattle onboard. backus lit up, memory of his blunder gone.

'look at that!' he yelled. 'triangle, what would ohio folks say? eyes popping, guaranteed.'

everyone on deck, even the gamblers. backus knew them all, always pushing his cattle talk. i slipped away, watched as the gamblers closed in on him. backus pulled back, they stayed close. uncomfortable scene. but as they passed, i heard him say, annoyed—

'won't do it, gents. told you before, i ain't risking it.'

felt better. his head's on straight.

on the way to san francisco, gamblers kept at him. gave him a nudge once. he just laughed.

'their game, not mine. my folks drilled it into me, steer clear.'

nearing san francisco, stormy night, strong wind. went below. saw a figure leaving the gamblers' lair. backus. heart sank. rushed below, couldn't find him. back on deck, caught him slipping back into that den. had he caved? worried he had. what for? his coin maybe. peeked in, and there he was—gambling. worse, they had him on champagne. he called it 'cider,' all impressed.

'never tasted nothing like it,' he said. rascals exchanged looks, filled glasses, but tossed theirs. backus drained his.

couldn't watch. walked off, tried to lose myself in the sea sounds. no use. kept checking back. always saw him drinking, them faking. worst night ever.

only chance we had was to hit our anchorage quick. that'd shut down the game. i pushed the ship along with everything i had, even prayers. finally, we shot through the golden gate, and my heart jumped. rushed to check on backus. hope faded fast—his eyes were red, face sweaty and flushed, and he was slurring, swaying with the ship's roll. another drink down as the cards came out.

he peeked at his hand, and for a moment, those dull eyes sparked. the others noticed, gave each other a look.

'cards?'

'none!' backus snapped.

hank wiley tossed one card, the rest three each. bets were small till now. backus kicked it off with a ten-dollar eagle. wiley paused, then matched it and added ten more. the other two folded.

backus threw in twenty more. wiley matched, added a hundred. reached for the pot.

'don't touch,' backus said, dead serious.

'what? you're covering?'

'cover? yeah, and i'll add a hundred on top.'

he dug out the cash from his coat.

'want to play like that? i see your raise and add five hundred!' wiley grinned.

'five hundred more,' backus said, throwing the bills onto the pile. the three barely hid their glee.

all pretense gone, shouts flew, and the stack of gold climbed. ten thousand sat there. wiley tossed a bag of coins, taunting—

'five thousand more, country boy—what now?'

'i call!' backus slammed his shot-bag on the pile. 'what've you got?'

'four kings, you fool!' wiley crowed, grabbing for the pot.

'four aces, you idiot!' backus roared, pulling a revolver. 'i'm the real gambler here, been waiting for you all trip!'

anchor dropped, trip over, rumbledy-dum-dum.

the world can be rough. one of the gamblers was backus's 'pal'. he was supposed to deal backus four queens but didn't.

a week later, i ran into backus, dressed sharp on montgomery street. as we parted, he laughed—

'oh, about those gores, forget it. i don't know a thing about cattle. only learned a bit in jersey before we left. cattle talk was just for show. don't need it now.'

next day, we left the 'gold dust' crew, hoping to see them again. fate had other plans.

three months later, august 8, while writing, i saw a new york paper:

A TERRIBLE DISASTER.

SEVENTEEN DEAD IN AN EXPLOSION ON STEAMER 'GOLD DUST.'

'NASHVILLE, Aug. 7.--A dispatch from Hickman, Ky., says—

steamer "Gold Dust" blew her boilers at 3 today, right after leaving Hickman. 47 folks scalded, 17 missing. Boat got to the eddy above town, and town folks helped get cabin passengers, officers, some crew, and deck passengers ashore. They got them to hotels and homes. 24 injured ended up in Holcomb's dry-goods store first, got treated before moving to better spots.

List showed 17 dead, including the barkeeper. Among 47 hurt: captain, chief mate, second mate, second and third clerks, Mr. Lem S. Gray, pilot, and some crew. Private telegram said none seriously hurt except Gray. Letters later said Gray was improving, but more letters got less hopeful, then news of his passing. Good man, deserved better.

CHAPTER 38

The House Beautiful

we took a Cincinnati boat to New Orleans. Mr. Dickens didn't buy the "magnificent" or "floating palaces" talk about Mississippi steamboats. Folks here saw them as magnificent compared to what they knew. If Mr. Dickens was thinking of crown jewels or the Taj, sure, maybe not magnificent. But here? They were palaces. For most around Baton Rouge to St. Louis, they fit the dream of what grand was.

every town lined up along that stretch of river had a standout house, the big shot's place. picture this: sprawling lawn with a white fence, looking decent. brick path straight to the door of a two-story frame house, all white and with columns pretending to be fancy Greek work, but nah, just painted pine. iron knocker, tarnished brass knob, needs some elbow grease.

inside, bare wood floor in the hallway. parlor's about fifteen by fifteen, if you're lucky a bit more. ingrain carpet, mahogany table with a green-shaded lamp on a multicolored yarn mat. books neatly piled, like Tupper, marked up; 'Friendship's Offering' with cheesy pictures; even 'Ivanhoe' might show up. albums full of cringe poetry. some goody-goody reads like 'Shepherd of Salisbury Plain.' current issue of Godey's 'Lady's Book' has those fashion plates with identical women, tiny feet peeking out.

new-fangled stove replacing the old fireplace, pipe awkwardly through a board. mantel loaded with fake fruit, plaster or wax, trying hard to look real. above that, 'Washington Crossing the Delaware,' with a young lady's needlework version nearby--poor George wouldn't cross if he saw it. piano, pretending, smothered in sheet music: 'Battle of Prague,' 'Bird Waltz,' and more. a guitar leans there, begging for a go at 'Spanish Fandango.'

artwork's all over. some motto in yarn or grasses, ancestor of 'God Bless Our Home.' black-framed sketches by the young ladies—grim landscapes. some Napoleon and Moses prints on the walls. big family portrait in oil: dad with a book, mom with a guitar, kids with toys, all raw and red.

other side, grandparents in a stern portrait from way back. under a glass dome, a stiff flower bouquet in wax. corner shelves filled with old-timey bric-a-brac: shells with carvings, quartz with gold, arrowheads, moccasins, alum baskets, bug collections. bellows toy dog, some candy rabbit, pewter medals. daguerreotypes of everyone, stiff and shiny.

a special spot for a watercolor by a niece who visited once, now gone. horse-hair chairs and a sofa that won't stay put. window shades scream with color, tin boxings up top all flashy. bedrooms got rag carpets, corded beds sagging in the middle, cane chairs, tiny mirror, old bureau. maybe a wash set, maybe not. brass candlestick with a tallow candle. no bathrooms, and no one's coming who'd expect one.

that was the home of the main guy from new orleans to st. louis. when he got on a big fancy steamboat, it was like stepping into a different world. chimney tops looking like red feathers, pilot house, decks all dressed up with white wooden designs. gold acorns on the cranes, deer horns above the big bell. maybe some wild painting on the paddle-box. big boiler deck painted blue with armchairs. inside, it was all white, like snow, with porcelain knobs and pictures on each stateroom door. gold-touched patterns overhead, chandeliers everywhere, showering glass drops. colored skylights casting rainbows. the ladies’ cabin had a soft pink and white carpet with huge flowers. and the bridal chamber—whoever came up with that idea was something else. every stateroom had clean bunks, maybe a mirror, a small closet, sometimes even a washbowl and pitcher. but usually everyone cleaned up at the barber shop's row of bowls, with shared towels, combs, and soap.

take that steamboat, and you've got her at her peak. now cover her with old dirt, and you've got the cincinnati steamer. not outside, just inside; she had good officers, except the steward.
