clean her up, repaint her, and she’d be just like the most praised boats of the old days. steamboat design here hasn’t changed, nor has the furniture.

CHAPTER 39

manufactures and miscreants

where the river used to twist near vicksburg, it's now straight, thanks to a cut-off; what was seventy miles is now thirty-five. this change pushed delta, louisiana, out into the country, ending its river town days. now a sand-bar with young trees hides it, soon to be a forest.

we passed grand gulf and rodney, known from the war, and hit natchez, the last of the hill-cities. baton rouge is next, but it’s just on high ground. natchez-under-the-hill hasn't changed much in twenty years; still small and worn. it had a wild reputation back in the keel-boat and early steamboat days with all the drinking and fighting. but natchez-on-top-of-the-hill has always been nice. even mrs. trollope had to admit it back in 1827.

Driving through Natchez, you get to see these bluffs — little breaks in the flatness where the town sits high. Natchez stands out like an oasis with its green hill, pawpaw, palmetto, and orange trees. It's the northernmost spot where oranges can ripen outside. Everywhere else we passed looked pretty rough.

Now, Natchez, like Vicksburg and New Orleans, has railways stretching out, and they’ve got ice-factories. Thirty tons a day. Back in my time, ice was a luxury; only the rich had it. Now, it's for everyone. I saw one of these factories in New Orleans. Nothing too fancy — just a big building with steam machinery and these big iron pipes coated in thick, white ice. They shouldn’t stay frozen in that warmth, but the inside's too cold.

In the floor, they had tin boxes filled with water, packed with salt and other stuff, hit with ammonia gas. I didn’t really get how it worked, but the water froze, and the guys stirred it to get the air out. Once frozen, they dipped the boxes in boiling water to free the ice, then slid the blocks out. These were crystal-clear, some with flowers or dolls inside for decoration. They could sell the ice by wagon throughout New Orleans, even in small amounts, at six or seven bucks a ton and still profit. Up north, we don't get deals like that unless we take a lot more ice at once.

the Rosalie Yarn Mill in Natchez is running 6,000 spindles and 160 looms, with a crew of 100. Natchez Cotton Mills started up four years ago with 4,000 spindles in a 50 x 190 ft building. Cost 'em $105,000, all local cash. Two years on, they upped the investment to $225,000, added a story, stretched it to 317 ft, and cranked capacity to 10,300 spindles and 304 looms. Got 250 folks working there now, most from Natchez. They process 5,000 bales of cotton a year, pumping out 5 million yards of brown shirtings, sheetings, and drills. Stock's tight, $5,000 a share, but none floating around.

river changes are wild, but i never thought id see Natchez and the other river towns turn into factory hubs and rail centers.

talking factories reminds me of a convo i overheard on a Cincinnati boat. Woke up, voices buzzing. Two guys talking about the big flood, then straight to business. Sales guys—one from Cincinnati, the other New Orleans. Fast talkers, worshipping the dollar.

'this stuff,' says Cincinnati, waving a knife of fake butter, 'our house made it. Smell, taste, test it. Feels like butter, right? Nope—oleomargarine! Can't tell the difference. We stock most Western boats; real butter's rare out there. We're taking over that market—and hotels too. Soon, no butter outside big cities in the Mississippi and Ohio Valleys. We're churning out oleo by the ton, dirt-cheap. Butter's over. Oleo's the future. Orders are flying in from every town i hit.'

then New Orleans jumps in, 'yeah, it's a good fake, but not the only one. They turn cotton-seed oil into olive oil now.'

Cincinnati nods, 'yeah, big bucks for a while. Shipped it to France and Italy, brought it back as "genuine" with U.S. customs stamp. But France and Italy caught on, taxed it heavy, couldn't keep up.'

'oh, they did?' New Orleans leans back, 'wait here a minute.'

heads to his state-room, comes back with a couple of long bottles, and yanks out the corks–says:

"there now, smell them, taste them, check the bottles, look at the labels. one's from europe, the other's homegrown. european olive-oil, american cotton-seed olive-oil. tell 'm apart? you can't. nobody can. folks can send their oils to europe and back if they want—it's their choice; but our firm knows a better way. we make it all in our factory down in new orleans: labels, bottles, oil, the works. ok, not the labels: we buy those cheap overseas. see, there's this tiny speck in a gallon of cotton-seed oil that gives it a smell, or a taste, or something—get rid of that, and you can turn the oil into anything. nobody can tell the difference. we know how to take that bit out—and we're the only ones who can. we produce olive-oil that's perfect—undetectable! we're doing great business, too—i could show you my order-book for this trip. maybe you'll butter everybody's bread, but we'll cotton-seed his salad from the gulf to canada, and that's a sure thing."

cincinnati lit up with admiration. the two swindlers swapped business cards and got up. as they left the table, cincinnati asked—

"but you need custom-house marks, right? how do you manage that?"

didn't catch the answer.

we passed port hudson, where two of the war's fiercest episodes happened—the night battle between farragut's fleet and the confederate land batteries on april 14th, 1863; and the big land battle two months later, eight hours of intense fighting, ending in the union's retreat with heavy losses.

CHAPTER 40

castles and culture

baton rouge was decked in flowers, like a bride—no, more like a greenhouse. we were in the deep south now—no holding back. magnolia trees in the capitol grounds were beautiful and fragrant with dense foliage and huge blossoms. sweet scent, but you want some distance—too strong up close. not good for bedrooms—might suffocate you in sleep. we were definitely in the south; sugar region starts here, plantations—vast green fields, sugar mills, and quarters clustered together—were visible. tropical sun above, tropical heat in the air.

and here begins the pilot's paradise: wide river to new orleans, water from shore to shore, no bars, snags, sawyers, or wrecks in the way.

sir walter scott's probably to blame for the capitol building; can't imagine this little fake castle being built if he hadn't driven folks mad with his medieval tales. the south still hasn't shaken off his books' influence. admiration for his heroes and their over-the-top 'chivalry' stuff lingers here, where you can already smell the practical nineteenth-century air of cotton factories and trains; traces of his flowery language hang on too. it's sad, this whitewashed castle with turrets, all fake inside and out, pretending to be what it's not, built in an otherwise respectable place; even sadder to see it being restored now, when it would've been easy to let dynamite finish what a kind fire started, and use that money to build something real.

baton rouge doesn't own the fake castle scene. here's a pic from an ad for the 'female institute' in columbia, tennessee. ad says:

'the institute building is famous for its striking and beautiful architecture. visitors love its old castle look with towers, turreted walls, and ivy-covered porches.'

teaching in a castle sounds as dreamy as running a hotel in one.

on its own, a fake castle's probably harmless. but as a symbol of misguided medieval romanticism in our no-nonsense, practical age, it's a mistake.

check out this bit from a kentucky 'female college' prospectus. 'female college' sounds okay, but if we're shortening for brevity, 'she-college' would be even shorter and means the same, assuming either means anything at all—

'the president's southern by birth, upbringing, education, and sentiment; all teachers are southern in sentiment, except those born in europe. believing the southern way as the top civilization on this continent, we train young ladies in southern ideas of delicacy, refinement, womanhood, religion, and propriety; so we offer a first-class female college for the south and look for southern patronage.'

{missing illustrations by the ad writer:

knoxville, tenn., october 19 — just after ten this morning, general joseph a. mabry, thomas o'connor, and joseph a. mabry jr. were all killed in a shootout. yesterday, mabry went after o'connor at the fairgrounds over an old property feud. o'connor wasn't armed then, but mabry was, and he made it clear he wanted o'connor dead. 

today, o'connor was at the mechanics' national bank, saw mabry on the street, grabbed a shotgun from inside, and shot mabry in the side. fired again for good measure as mabry went down. then mabry's son charged in, shot o'connor in the chest, but o'connor still managed to shoot back, hitting mabry jr. both died right there. all of this went down in just two minutes.

a bystander got hit in the thigh, another in the arm, and four others had their clothes shot through. the street packed with people. the mabrys were just cleared of another murder a few days back. o'connor was a big shot at the bank, richest guy in the state.

last month, in somerville, tenn., professor sharpe took matters into his own hands after hearing his brother-in-law, captain burton, wanted him dead. burton already had a record. sharpe got a shotgun, found burton playing billiards, and put an end to him. locals backed the professor since the law wasn't going to help.

around the same time, two young guys in north carolina had a falling out over a girl. they met on the road, one with a club, the other with an ax. the guy with the club never stood a chance. got disarmed and killed with the ax.

about the same time, two young guys from virginia, working as clerks in a hardware store in charlottesville, got into a scrap. peter dick tossed pepper in charles roads's eyes. roads wanted an apology, but dick wouldn't give it, so they figured a duel was the only way. problem was, no pistols and too late to find any. one suggested butcher-knives. the other agreed. roads ended up with a nasty cut to the gut that might be bad news. no word if dick's been picked up yet. he says he's real sorry. someone from the _philadelphia press_ mentions they're trying to keep it quiet.

from baton rouge to new orleans, sugar plantations line the riverbanks, stretching back to the cypress forests. it's not lonely anymore. houses line both sides, turning the river into a broad street. it's a homey area. now and then, a big manor house pops up, hidden among trees. heard from travelers fifty years back—mrs. trollope noticed the landscape's charm:

'the banks' flatness above new orleans was broken by palmettos, ilex, and oranges, which kept us interested for days.'

captain basil hall talked about:

'sugar planters' homes with bright gardens and neat slave-villages gave the river a thriving feel.'

same picture today, minus the fresh paint. war's blight left cabins unwhitewashed and mansions looking worn. twenty-one years ago, everything along the 'coast' was as neat as tourists described in 1827.

tourists got fooled with wild tales and then mocked. they told mrs. trollope scary stories about alligators eating a woman and five kids. not sure why jokers like that felt sensitive, but they were. can't quite get why capt. basil hall's honest work got such a rough reception.

CHAPTER 41

the metropolis of the south

getting back to new orleans feels like slipping into an old pair of boots. familiar roads, same old sights. when you fly into london, you see those upper bedrooms from the train, like a peek into lives you'll never know. same deal here when the river's high. water's up near the levee, and from the boat, you look down into people's windows. just a thin wall of dirt between them and a whole lot of trouble.

those salt warehouses at the city's edge? still there, same as always. but back when the war started, the owner went to sleep with a pile of cheap salt. woke up, and bam, it's worth its weight in gold. war does funny things to prices.

wharves are still there, ships too, but the steamboats? mostly gone. city looks the same, but it's spread out more. streets still got their dust and trash, gutters still holding that lazy water, sidewalks crowded with barrels. commercial buildings got that same dusty look.

canal street's put on a bit of a show now. more folks, streetcars zipping by, and folks up on the verandas dressed to impress. architecture? not really new orleans' strong suit, except in the cemeteries. custom house looks like a prison, built before the war. fire's the great architect elsewhere, like in boston, where the burnt parts came back better than ever. but new orleans? they're just starting. new cotton exchange might change things. solid, no nonsense. might teach the city a thing or two about building with some style.

city's full of sharp folks, always thinking ahead. but what's funny is, the buildings don’t match that energy. it's like the city's wide awake, but the architecture's still snoozing. used to be, gutters were nasty, breeding all sorts of sickness. now, they've got big machines flushing them out a few times a day. some gutters even have a constant flow, never still. new orleans says it's one of the healthiest spots in the country, outside of those yellow fever hits. ice is everywhere now, made right there in town. business is booming on the river, ocean, and railways. back when we visited, it was the best-lit city in the country, all electric. more lights than new york, and better too. the main streets and five miles along the riverfront were as bright as day. new clubs are popping up, and spots like West End and Spanish Fort are buzzing. telephones are all over. journalism's stepped up big time. papers used to be so-so, now they’re top-notch. they'll pay whatever it takes to get the news. the writing’s real literature. like in 1882, the 'Times-Democrat' did a massive report on the Mississippi Valley's business, from New Orleans to St. Paul. that issue was 40 pages long, with 280 columns, holding over 420,000 words. that's nearly three times the words in this book. but then you look at the city’s architecture, and, well, it doesn’t match up.

just talking public buildings here. homes in New Orleans? no complaints. they're still wooden, at least in the American parts of town, and they all look comfy. the rich ones are big, usually painted white, with wide porches supported by fancy columns. these houses sit in big yards, surrounded by roses and lush greenery. they fit right in with the scenery, easy on the eyes, and really homey.

even those big green cisterns grow on you. they seem weird at first, like some mansion-brewery combo, but folks need rainwater since wells aren’t an option. can't have cellars or graves either, since the ground’s all made-up. so they do without, and people get by just fine.

they bury their dead in vaults, above ground, like little houses. sometimes they look like temples, usually marble, and they line the paths and roads in the cemetery. when you walk through them, all in a row, "city of the dead" makes sense. cemeteries look nice, kept up well. going from the busy streets to a cemetery, you might think if folks lived as clean as they do after they’re gone, their neighborhood would impress the business world. fresh flowers sit in vases, placed by loved ones, changed daily. for a cheaper, lasting memory, there's the "immortelle" — a wreath or cross made of black linen rosettes, sometimes with a yellow one in the middle. you hang it up, and it stays there, keeping your grief in mind, tough as iron.

chameleons, little reptiles, scuttle along the marble fronts, catching flies. color changes aren't as wild as you'd think. they change when someone hangs up an immortelle, but that's just courtesy.

im trying to get sentimental about graveyards, but can't. it’s all grotesque, really. maybe graveyards made sense ages ago, when nobody knew burying bodies spread disease. but now, even kids know a buried body starts a deadly career the moment it’s underground. grim thought. take the relics of St. Anne up in Canada. after nineteen hundred years, they cure the sick, but right after her death, they probably made thousands ill. so, these miracles are just payback. St. Anne’s a slow payer, but better late than never. most saints don’t pay at all. if you find one like St. Anne, you’ll find a hundred and fifty who dodge the debt. and they never pay more than the principal. they can’t bring back the dead they killed, just heal the living. that part always stays unpaid.

Dr. F. Julius Le Moyne, after fifty years in medicine, said burying bodies from infectious diseases loads the air and water with all kinds of germs, not just from rot but from the diseases themselves.

gases from buried bodies will rise through gravel, just like coal-gas. no stopping it. back in 1853, during the new orleans epidemic, mortality in one district hit 452 per thousand. that district had three big cemeteries. digging up old plague burial grounds can bring disease back, like when they opened the ones in eyam. 1828, professor bianchi showed plague returning in modena after digging disturbed graves from 300 years back.

dr. charles w. purdy, in a talk about cremation, said funerals cost more than public schools or the gold and silver yield of 1880. costs don't even cover land and tombs or property value drops near cemeteries. cremation could be as grand or as simple as you want. for the poor, it’s cheaper until they start copying the rich.

a friend of mine, working odd jobs, barely makes $400 a year. a funeral for him is a huge financial hit. he lost a child, searched for a coffin he could afford, ended up with a plain one for $26. would’ve been cheaper if it were anything but a coffin. that $26 will affect them for months.

CHAPTER 43

the art of inhumation

ran into an old friend on the street, hadn’t seen him in years. looked younger, happier. asked him where he found all that cheer. he laughed, pointed to his hat with 'undertaker' inside. then he put it on with a tilt and laughed again.

that's the deal, man. back when you knew me, times were tough running an insurance gig. all over the place. big fire? great, we're busy for ten days, then it's crickets till the next blaze. not enough fires in this town to keep a guy going. but this undertaker business? whole different thing. folks don't wait around to kick the bucket. they just do it. no slow weeks here. started small with a couple coffins and a rented hearse. now, business is booming. five years back, attic life. now? got a nice place with a fancy roof and all the bells and whistles.

do coffins pay well? oh, you bet. here's the thing: nobody haggles over a coffin. nobody shops around. when it comes to coffins, people want the best they can afford. pine, walnut, mahogany, iron casket with silver and bronze—there's always a step up. and they pay. they always pay. undertaking? it’s the most reliable business out there.

rich folks? they want the best and they pay for it. poor folks? if you play it right, they'll spend big too. take this widow, for example. comes in, tears flowing, checks the stock. asks the price of a nice one.

“thirty-nine bucks, ma’am,” i say.

“that's steep, but pat's going out like a gentleman. i'll take it.”

“sure thing, ma’am. it's a decent one. not fancy, but we gotta live within our means.” as she's leaving, i casually mention, “this one with the white satin lining is stunning, but sixty-five dollars is a bit much. still, i told mrs. o’shaughnessy…”

“what, bridget o’shaughnessy bought that for her husband?”

“yep.”

“then pat's getting the same, extras and all. i'll find the money.”

working with the livery stables, i cant skip mentioning Mrs. O'Shaughnessy. she spent fifty-four bucks on hacks for Dennis's funeral, throwing a duke's worth of style into it. used to be her thing, but in this town, that's over. Irish folks went too far, leaving themselves broke after funerals. priest stepped in, stopped the madness. now, it's two hacks max, sometimes just one.

"so if you're so cheery usually, what about during an epidemic?" i asked.

he shook his head. "nah, epidemics don't bring in the cash. not like regular times. you know why?"

i had no clue. "what's up?"

"two things: Embamming and Ice."

"how's that work?"

"normally, a person dies, we ice 'em up for a day or three, waiting for family. ice melts fast, and we charge like it's gold. same for Embamming. families want the best, even if there's only a couple ways to do it. grief's like that, doesn't think straight. wants immortality for the dead, pays up. in no time, a body becomes worth six hundred bucks easy. nothing beats it unless you're trading rats for diamonds in a famine. but epidemics? they skip the Embamming. hurts business bad—our little trade joke, 'health' kills us. gotta run. drop by if you need anything—or, you know, just passing by."

he exaggerated himself, if at all. i didn't add to it.

anyway, on inhumation—i prefer cremation. told my pastor once, and he said, all serious, "i wouldn't worry with your odds." like he knew anything—my family's against it.

CHAPTER 44

City Sights

new orleans tells a story in every corner. the old french part, which was spanish before, doesn’t look a thing like the american side. you got these blocks of houses, plain and serious, with plaster outsides that time has painted in warm, rich colors. they fit right into the scene like a sunset. try to copy that look, and you’ll come up short.

and those iron railings, man, they’re something special. delicate and intricate, with monograms woven in steel. hand-made back in the day, they’re rare now, almost like art pieces.

we wandered this historic part with Mr. Cable, the guy who wrote 'the Grandissimes'. he’s a guide with words, making the past vivid in his books. with him, you see the place clearer than just walking it alone.

the old St. Louis Hotel? not much to look at, now a bunch of municipal offices. like New York’s Academy of Music, if they ever cleaned it, you’d never know. surprised there aren’t veggies growing inside, but maybe the chairs block the sun.

checked out the Cathedral too, with its gentle light, and the bright square out front full of orange trees. then a drive past houses, drainage wheels, and fields with cows and kids. passed a cemetery with a famous pirate’s resting place. he was big until he joined politics and folks turned their backs. now, people remember the pirate, not the politician. respect came back, but only for what he was.

we took a drive across some swamp, following this raised shell road. canal on one side, dense woods on the other. you'd see these old cypress trees, looking like something out of a Japanese painting. once in a while, an alligator just cruising along the canal. and then, folks hanging by the bank, waiting for a fish to bite.

we ended up at the West End—bunch of hotels with big verandas, right by Lake Pontchartrain. had dinner there, over the water. main dish was pompano. that fish is something else, like a sin you dont regret.

every night, folks flock to West End and Spanish Fort. there's dining, bands playing, walks under electric lights, lake sailing. plenty to do.

later, i tried pompano again at an editor's dinner. best i've ever had. came with a pyramid of crayfish, big as your thumb, plus whitebait and some sweet soft-shell crabs. other dishes were good, but you find those anywhere. this stuff, only in New Orleans.

down here, they got this thing called the Broom Brigade. young women in matching outfits doing infantry drills with brooms instead of guns. saw them perform, and they nailed every move. they didn’t sweep, but you bet they could if they wanted. if they ever hit the streets, those streets would be spotless in no time, though the girls might not be.

drill was at the Washington Artillery building. checked out some war relics, and a painting of Stonewall Jackson and General Lee. both on horses, having a chat. painting's valuable for the portraits, but without a label, who knows. could be any meeting between those two.

one story, and it does the job. says clear enough, 'Lee and Jackson together.' artist would've liked to mark it as their last meeting, but you can't paint history that's not there. a good label beats a ton of gestures in a historical painting every time. in Rome, folks get all teary-eyed over 'Beatrice Cenci the Day before her Execution.' shows what knowing the title can do. without it, they'd just see 'young girl with a cold; young girl in a bonnet.'

those Southern tones, all half-remembered, still sweet to my ear. Southerners talk like music. maybe it's just me, being born down there. they skip the r unless it's at the start—'honah,' 'dinnah,' 'Gove'nuh,' 'befo' the waw.' print doesn't do it justice, but the ear gets it. when'd the r drop outta Southern speech, anyway? wasn't borrowed from the North or England. lots of Southerners toss a y in with a k sound—Mr. K'yahtah, playing k'yahds, riding in k'yahs. they still use 'Sir' too, not like the North where it's gone. polite 'Yes, Suh', 'No, Suh.'

but there's some rough spots. 'like' for 'as,' extra 'at' where it ain't needed. heard a well-spoken guy say, 'Like the flag-officer did.' his cook would say, 'Like the flag-officer done.' you hear 'Where have you been at?' and worse—'I was a-ask'n' Tom whah you was a-sett'n' at.' even the refined mix up 'will' and 'shall'; say 'I didn't go to do it' for 'I didn't mean to do it.' 'guess' ain't much used down South; they 'reckon.' no 'doesn't' either, just 'don't.' the rough ones use 'went' for 'gone.' almost as bad as Northern 'hadn't ought.' heard a local gem: 'He hadn't ought to have went.' quite the mix. one parent Northern, one Southern, bet on it. heard a teacher ask, 'Where is John gone?' so common, 'whither' would sound like putting on airs.

picked up a word in New Orleans, one you'd go there just to learn. it's 'lagniappe'—say it like lanny-yap. Spanish, they say. found it in the Picayune first day, heard it all around by day two, asked what it was day three, and by day four, i was using it like a local. it's like the thirteenth roll in a baker's dozen, a little extra, no charge. started in the Spanish part of town. when you buy something, you say, 'something for lagniappe?' and you get a little extra, like a bit of licorice or a cheap cigar. what they give the governor, who knows, maybe some political support.

in New Orleans, when you're drinking and someone says, 'just one more, for lagniappe,' you know what they mean. same when a guy's laying it on thick with compliments and says, 'oh, that's for lagniappe.' and if a waiter spills coffee on you, he might say, 'for lagniappe, sah,' and bring you a fresh cup for free.

CHAPTER 45

Southern Sports

up north, you hear about the war sometimes, maybe once a month, sometimes once a week. but really talk about it? not much. mostly cause out of six guys, four or five weren't even there. so odds are low it'll be a big topic. add six ladies, and they saw even less of it, so they're not gonna talk your ear off about it.

different story down south. every guy was in the war, every lady saw it. it's all they talk about. brings life to any dull conversation. mention the war, and everyone's got a story. it's like their a.d., everything's dated by it. 'since the waw,' 'du'in' the waw,' 'befo' the waw,' and so on. shows just how deep it hit everyone. gives a newcomer a real sense of what invasion means, more than any book could.

at a club one night, a guy leans over to me and says...

you notice we're always talking about the war. not cause we got nothing else, but cause it grabs us. in the war, everyone got a taste of all kinds of human stuff. so you bring up anything, and it reminds someone of a war story, and off they go. try to steer the conversation elsewhere, but it circles back. even the most random topic triggers war memories, and then the talk stops cause you can't ignore that big thing in your head.

the poet was a bit away, started talking about the moon. guy next to me goes, "watch, it'll turn into war talk soon." poet was surprised by how strong the moonlight seemed near the equator. thought it was brighter here than up North. got interrupted.

“let me explain,” someone said. “everything changed since the war. people here only see change for the worse. a young New-Yorker once said, 'what a wonderful moon you have down here!' and an old woman sighed, 'ah, honey, you shoulda seen it before the war!'” 

the moon topic died, but the poet tried again. talk shifted to if there’s a real moonlight difference north to south. that led to how we light up the dark. someone remembered when Farragut attacked Port Hudson, he didn’t use lanterns. painted the decks white for light. war talk was back, not even ten minutes gone.

i didn’t mind. war stories from those who lived it beat poets talking about the moon any day.

went to a cockpit in New Orleans on a Saturday. never saw a cockfight before. folks from everywhere, all kinds of languages. noticed something odd: no brutal faces. looked more like a prayer meeting. once it started, maybe a revival, if you blindfolded a stranger. the shouting was wild.

a black guy and a white guy were in the ring, everyone else watching. they brought the roosters in sacks, and when it was go time, the two handlers took them out, stroked 'em, poked 'em, then let 'em loose. the big black rooster went right for the gray one, hit him on the head with his spur. the gray didn't back down. then the crowd went nuts and stayed nuts. after they fought a while, i thought they’d drop dead any second. both were blind, covered in blood, and so tired they kept falling. but they wouldn't quit or die. the handlers kept picking them up, wiping them off, spraying them with cold water, even warming their heads in their mouths to keep them going — maybe. then they'd set them down, and the roosters would stumble around, find each other, take wild swings, and collapse again.

i didn't stick around to see the end. i watched as long as i could stand it, but it was too much. i admitted that and we left. later we heard the black rooster died in the ring, fighting till the end.

apparently, folks really get into this 'sport' if they’re familiar with it. never saw people enjoy anything more. didn’t matter if they were old or ten years old, they went wild. cockfighting's cruel, no doubt, but it's not as bad as fox-hunting — the roosters seem to like it. they give and get enjoyment, unlike the fox.

one day, we watched a mule race. enjoyed it more than any mule there. probably more than any animal race i've seen before. the grandstand was packed with new orleans' finest. that phrase ain't mine. southern reporters love it. been using it for generations. they use it countless times a day. a million if needed. it’s their go-to for respectable folks, and they never tire of it. it’s got this medieval vibe that suits their flashy souls. if they'd been around in old palestine, they'd call it 'the beauty and the chivalry of galilee' at the sermon on the mount. southerners are probably sick of it now, but change ain't coming soon.

new orleans editors keep it tight, no fluff. their correspondents, though? different story. in the appendix, i quoted a solid letter, but the average letter? different style. for example—

last april, the 'times-democrat' sent a relief-steamer up a bayou. landed at some village, the captain got some local ladies to join for a short trip. they came aboard, and the boat took off. that's all there was. the editor would've just listed stats and maybe tabulated them to keep it clear and save space. but the correspondent had other plans, diving into the details—

'on saturday, early morning, the beauty of the place graced our cabin, and proud of her fair freight the gallant little boat glided up the bayou.'

took 22 words to say the boat left with the ladies. wasted ten words and lost the tightness of the statement.

southern reporters? their issue is women. they throw them off balance. they're sensible until a woman shows up. then they become flowery and lose their way. you'd think they were new to writing reading that flowery bit. but he knows his pen when women aren't around. like here—

'at 4, clouds gathered southeast, a gulf blow hit, growing stronger. not safe to leave, so we delayed. oaks shook moss in the wind, bayou waves mocked the sea. a lull let us start homeward under an inky sky, heavy wind. as darkness came, most wished they were closer to home.'

that's solid writing. compact. could've gone overboard but didn't.

back to the mule. found a full race report. backs my theory—women and walter scott mess with southern reporters. report's great until women show up. then it spirals—

'it'll be a while before the ladies' stand looks as lovely as yesterday. new orleans women are charming, but never more than this time of year. spring dresses, fresh air, and an unspeakable sanctity. stand crowded, men below felt like they were at paradise's gates. colors of favorite knights on their shoulders, but with mules instead of horses, it was far from king arthur's days.'

thirteen mules lined up for the first race. quite a mix. some were slick and well-groomed, others looked like they skipped the brush for days. some mules acted like it was war, others thought it was just a game, and a few seemed to think it was a church gathering. chaos? sure. but plenty of entertainment in that chaos.

all the riders? young guys from high society. if you're wondering why the ladies of new orleans would bother with a mule race, well, it's a fashion thing. everyone involved is part of that scene.

it's a riot, really. the mule race is a big deal every year. some mules have gotten so fast they had to be pulled from competition because they turned it into a one-mule show, no variety left. every now and then, someone sneaks one back in with a new look and name.

riders dressed to the nines in bright silks and satins. after a couple of false starts, all thirteen mules took off together, each with its own plan for how to run the race. this led to a wild, hilarious mess on the track.

mile heat at 2:22. eight mules got left behind. i had money on a mule that might've won if the race was backwards. second heat was fun too, especially the 'consolation race for beaten mules.' but the first heat had that special chaos.

now, steamboat races? those are a real thrill. two boats neck-and-neck, every part straining, steam and smoke everywhere, river breaking into hissing foam. makes a horse race look dull. horse races might be alright if it weren't for the false starts. but hey, nobody's ever died at one ive been to, though a few have limped away.

mardi-gras is the big deal in new orleans, and we missed it. last time i saw it was 24 years ago, with the mystic crew of comus leading the parade. knights and nobles in flashy paris-made outfits, just for one night. they had giants, dwarfs, all kinds of odd creatures. it's gotten even bigger now, more money, more spectacle. the main guy is 'rex' and nobody knows who these folks are. they're big shots, but they keep it mysterious just for the fun of it, not to dodge the cops.

mardi-gras started with the french and spanish, but the religious part's mostly gone. now it's more about the medieval flair, monsters, and fairy tales. it's flashy, and it marks the shift from the party season to the holy days.

new orleans had mardi-gras to itself for a while. now memphis, st. louis, and baltimore have it too. it's probably not going further. it wouldn't last up north or in london. the magic's in the mystery, the romance, not the humor. take that away, and it's dead. up north, they'd mock it to death.

the french revolution and bonaparte did some damage, but they broke old chains. they turned slaves into free people and put skill above noble birth. bonaparte took the godly aura off royalty—made them just people, accountable like anyone else. those changes, despite the chaos, left the world better off.

sir walter scott really did a number on the South. he spun these tales that made folks fall for old fantasies and outdated systems. it's like he dragged us back into the past with all those fake glories and empty rituals. the harm he did might be more lasting than anyone else who's put pen to paper. even now, some of those ideas are still hanging on down here, not as strong as before but still there. we got this odd mix of modern progress and medieval nonsense. thanks to him, every Southern gentleman had a title and thought it meant something. he built that whole rank and caste thing, which wasn't just about slavery.

you could argue sir walter helped shape the South before the war, maybe even nudged us towards it. sounds harsh, blaming him for war, but his influence is hard to ignore. the Southerner of the Civil War wasn't quite like the one from the Revolution. sir walter's stories changed folks more than almost anything else.

you can see his fingerprints in the old lit mags too, all full of flowery speeches and romance — straight out of his playbook, but not as good. both North and South tried their hand at it, and for a while, you couldn't tell the difference. plenty of Southern writers made a name for themselves that way.

change came, and the game aint fair between North and South now. North ditched the old wordy style, but Southern writers hang on to it. They got talent, sure, but they're stuck in the past, writing in old forms and dead language. When a Southern writer uses modern English, though, their work takes off, like Mr. Cable and Uncle Remus. Instead of a few big names, the South should have a bunch more—once they let go of the past.

Look at 'Don Quixote' and 'Ivanhoe.' 'Quixote' killed the medieval nonsense, but 'Ivanhoe' brought it back. In the South, Scott's work wrecked what Cervantes started.

CHAPTER 47

Uncle Remus and Mr. Cable

we got up early to meet MR. JOEL CHANDLER HARRIS ('Uncle Remus') from Atlanta. Picked him out at the hotel by his description—short, red-haired, freckled. Shy too. Even after a few days, his shyness stays strong. It's clear he's got a kind heart and genius—anyone who read Uncle Remus knows that. Talking about him feels like talking to friends, which makes it alright.

kids were let down when they saw him. "Why, he's white!" they said. To cheer them up, we pulled out the book for a Tar-Baby story, but he was too shy to read aloud. Mr. Cable and I gave it a go to show him it's easy, but his shyness held. So we read Brer Rabbit ourselves.

Mr. Harris, the best at writing negro dialect, cant bring himself to read it. Mr. Cable, though, nails the French dialects and reads them perfectly. Hearing him read about Jean-ah Poquelin and other characters was a real treat.

so mr. cable found himself in a bit of a pickle. used some fancy french names in his books—thought he'd made 'em up or pulled 'em from way back when. turns out, folks in new orleans actually had those names and weren't too thrilled about being spotlighted like that.

same kind of deal happened to me and mr. warner with 'the gilded age.' we had this character 'sellers,' but warner wanted something more unique. he asked about 'eschol sellers.' i said not without a drink. but he’d met a guy with that name ages ago, figured he was probably long gone. so we used it. not long after, this real-deal aristocratic guy shows up ready to sue us to kingdom come. ended up changing the name to 'mulberry sellers'—after pulling back a ridiculous number of books. 

now, chapter 48. ran into horace bixby on the street—guy hasn't aged a day since i last saw him 21 years back. still captain of the fastest boat around, 'city of baton rouge.' same sharp look and sharp eye. we took a trip downriver to a sugar plantation. along the way, saw a bunch of old steamboats, all worn out and forgotten. never saw them before, which just shows how fast these riverboats come and go. life on the mississippi is quick like that.

six miles out of town, there's this beat-up old brick chimney poking above the magnolias and live-oaks. folks say it's a monument for the battle of new orleans, when jackson beat the british on january 8, 1815. problem was, the war had already ended. peace was a thing, but news hadn't hit new orleans yet. if they had cable telegraph back then, none of that blood would have been spilled, and jackson probably wouldn't have ended up president. we've gotten over the war of 1812's mess, but jackson's presidency? still dealing with those consequences.

warmouth plantation is huge, and their hospitality matches. first time i saw steam-plows there. traction engine rolls on its wheels to where it needs to be, then pulls this massive plow across the field using a wire rope. digs into the soil like a foot and a half deep. plow looks like an upside-down boat brace. when the guy steering it sits on one end, it tilts like crazy. it's a wild ride, like a ship at sea.

plantation’s got 2,600 acres, 650 in cane, and an orange grove with 5,000 trees. they use some modern, complicated methods for cane, but lost $40,000 last year. this year's crop might hit 1,200 tons of sugar, so last year's loss won't sting as much. those fancy methods get you 1.5 to 2 tons per acre, way better than in my day.

drainage ditches are full of fiddler crabs. they scatter when spooked. they're a pain, though, because they dig into the levees and wreck them.

the sugar house is a maze of tubs, tanks, vats, and pipes. making sugar is a crazy process. throw the cane into centrifugals, grind out juice, run it through the evaporator, then bone-filter, clarifying tanks, granulating pipe, and finally the vacuum pan. sounds simple, but it's tough. making sugar right? nearly impossible. check your own supply over the years, and you'll see most folks can't do it without getting sand in there.

we could've gone to see captain eads' jetties at the river mouth, where the river’s been narrowed and deepened to 26 feet. but water's high now, everything's covered, so no point going.

could've checked out pilot-town, that old place on stilts. folks say you get around by skiff or canoe, even for weddings and funerals. little kids there handle oars like other kids do bikes. 

but time was tight. headed home instead. sail up the river was nice, would've been all romantic if not for the tug's parrot. loudmouth with a laugh like nails on a chalkboard. he mocked every sentimental thing we said or sang. finally shut us up, and he cursed himself hoarse, happy about it.

guys moved up front to smoke and chat. some old steamboatmen shared stories. found out a pilot i knew turned spiritualist. gets letters from a dead relative through a medium named manchester. pricey post from paradise, cheap from new york. funny thing, i met manchester once. buddy wanted to talk to his uncle who got cyclone'd into a tree. uncle “replied” through manchester, but it was all nonsense. if manchester ain't the biggest fraud, i'd owe him an apology.

questions he asked were like: 

where are you? 
in the spirit world. 
happy? 
very happy. 

it just went on like that. when asked what they’d talk about after everyone joined them, no answer. spirits dodge “frivolous” stuff, apparently. buddy asked if his uncle would come back. he said no, even under oath. they don’t eat, drink, smoke, or read. all good folks go there, apparently.

Q. any tips on how to add some crime into my life so i can end up somewhere else?

A. no response.

Q. when did you die?

A. i didnt die. i passed away.

Q. alright, when did you pass away? how long you been in the spirit world?

A. we dont keep track of time here.

Q. even if youre fuzzy on time now, you had dates when you were alive. what day did you pass?

A. yes.

Q. then tell me the day of the month.

(there's a long pause while the medium struggles with his pencil, head and body jerking around. finally, he says spirits forget dates since they dont matter to them.)

Q. this spirit forgot the date of its passing?

A. that's right.

Q. interesting. what year was it?

(more fumbling and spasms from the medium. he says the spirit forgot the year too.)

Q. this is something. last question before we part for good; did you die naturally or by some disaster?

A. (after a long pause and more spasms.) _natural death_.

that was the end. my friend told the medium that when his relative was alive, he had an amazing intellect and perfect memory. strange he lost it all in eternal happiness.

this medium's got lots of clients still. he gets letters from spirits all over the spirit world and sends them through U.S. mail. these letters are filled with advice from spirits who know less than a tadpole. folks actually follow this advice. one client was learning from spirits (or the clever Manchester) to design a new railway car-wheel. it's basic work for a spirit but better than endless talk about 'how happy we are.'

CHAPTER 49

Pilot Life Episodes

during some tug-boat chatter, turns out four out of five of my old river pals who left became farmers. not because they were great at farming, but probably because they wanted a private life away from strangers, like being in the pilot-house. probably also because on those stormy nights, they'd see the warm lights of farmhouses and imagine the peace inside. over time, they dreamed of that life as the ultimate goal.

never seen these pilot-farmers impress anyone with their farming. doesn't really support them; they keep it afloat. every spring they vanish from the river, back when the frost hits, looking like they've been through it. they pilot through winter to cover the debts from farming season. so they're still tied to the river, just half the year.

one guy bought a farm but wasn't about to run it himself. smarter than that. handed it to an ag expert on a share deal—expert gets two-thirds of the corn, pilot gets a third. end of the season, pilot got zip. expert said the farm only managed two loads.

some pilots had stories, some lucky, some not. captain montgomery, i used to steer for him, led the confederate fleet at memphis. when his ship sank, he swam, fought soldiers, escaped. cool as ice, that guy. once on the 'crescent city' in new orleans, i was waiting for his orders in twilight. captain’s hat sat on the bell, but he wasn't in it. he was strict, so i held steady, knowing a crash was coming. just before we hit, he appeared, calm as ever, said, 'set her back on both.' too late, though; we hit. later, he only said i did right and to do the same next time.

another pilot i knew died honorably. his boat caught fire, but he stayed at the wheel till it was safe ashore. he got off last, clothes on fire. died a few hours later, only life lost.

Mississippi piloting's got some wild stories—six or seven about pilots going down with the ship and a bunch of close calls. But you won’t find a story about a pilot bailing to save himself if sticking it out would save others. That’s a big deal, and worth pointing out.

From early on, a 'cub' pilot learns to face down any danger and choose death over ditching the wheel when they might still help. They drill that in hard enough that even the green ones will hold their ground when the time comes. There's a grave in Memphis for one such guy—burned up saving folks on the White River. Told the captain he'd hit a sand bar to save everyone, even though a bluff landing meant losing lives. He made it, grounded the boat, but the fire caught him. He was urged to leave, but he said:

'I will not go. If I go, nobody will be saved; if I stay, no one will be lost but me. I will stay.'

Two hundred on board, and he was the only casualty. There used to be a monument for him in Memphis. I tried to find it once, but ran out of time.

Heard some tug-boat chatter that Dick Kennet got blown up near Memphis; others I knew died in the war, a couple shot at the wheel. A buddy I piloted with went to collect money in New Orleans and never came back—probably murdered. Ben Thornburgh and his wild 'cub' I used to bicker with are long gone. That 'cub' once let loose a passenger's bear just to see what’d happen. Got chased all over the deck for it, bear ripped his coat-tail right off. Everyone else hid while that bear roamed the boat.

One pilot friend dropped dead at the wheel in '69, heart gave out. Captain saw the boat veering, ran up, found him on the floor.

mr. bixby got caught in an explosion over at madrid bend. he made it out okay, but the other pilot didn't.

george ritchie had a similar scare near memphis. he got blown off the wheel and into the river. freezing water. he hung onto a cotton bale, mostly with his teeth, until some deckhands found him. they ripped open the bale, packed him in cotton to warm him up, and got him to memphis safe. he's one of bixby's pilots on the 'baton rouge' now.

then there's the strange tale of a steamboat clerk. back when i knew him, he was a young spender, full of life but likely to waste his potential. in a western city, a wealthy foreign couple had a girl living with them. this clerk, we'll call him george johnson, got involved with her. they got caught, lied about being married, and the old man forgave them. they carried on without hiding. later, the foreign couple died, leaving everything to "mrs. george johnson." no such person existed. they rushed into a real marriage to cover the lie, but it didn't help. relatives swooped in, exposed the lie, and took the fortune. the johnsons were left with nothing but their hasty marriage. real events, stranger than fiction.

as for captain isaiah sellers, we used to talk about him. a respected man on and off the river. tall, strong, and sharp even in old age. he was a legend among pilots, having started on keelboats before steamboats. his peers admired him like you admire someone from another era. he left a diary, but it didn't go back to his first steamboat trip in 1811. a 'st. louis republican' correspondent shared some diary bits when he passed.

in feb 1825, he hopped on the steamer "rambler" at florence, ala. that year, did three runs to new orleans on the "gen. carrol," doing the nashville to new orleans stretch. captain sellers introduced using a bell tap to signal heaving the lead. before that, the pilot just yelled down. worked fine then but imagine doing that on today's big boats.

by 1827, he was on the "president," a 285-ton boat, smithland to new orleans. next, the "jubilee" in 1828, doing his first piloting between herculaneum and st. genevieve. in 1836, he left pittsburgh on the "prairie," a 400-ton steamer, first to have a state-room cabin in st. louis. by 1857, he introduced the boat meeting signal, now standard, even law.

some river history notes from his log:

march 1825, gen. lafayette left new orleans for st. louis on the "natchez."

jan 1828, 21 steamers left new orleans for gen. jackson's visit.

1830, "north american" ran new orleans to memphis in six days, a record then. now, it's done in two days, ten hours.

1831, red river cut-off formed.

1832, "hudson" ran white river to helena, 75 miles, in 12 hours. stirred up talk.

1839, great horseshoe cut-off formed.

over 35 years, he's made 460 trips to new orleans, covering a million miles plus, averaging 86 miles a day.

captain sellers had this effect on groups of pilots. when he showed up, chatter stopped. the older pilots loved to show off to the newbies, bragging about river tales from way back. they'd puff up, making the new guys feel green. but when sellers arrived, he'd drop in with a few words about river changes no one else remembered. that shut 'em up quick, and the young ones loved it.

this old river guy kept popping up, spinning tales of islands long gone, leaving a wake of chaos. if you believed the pilots, he had a new island every time, always from some ancient river era no one else remembered. he was picky with details too, like never saying 'Mississippi' without a catch. he'd drop lines like 'when Louisiana was up the river farther,' and you got the sense he was messing with history on purpose.

he wasn't much of a writer, but he scribbled practical river notes, signed '_Mark Twain_,' and sent them to the 'New Orleans Picayune.' useful stuff on the river's state, but he'd sneak in stories like how the water hadn't been that high since 1815, or he'd mention a vanished island. pilots mocked those 'Mark Twain' notes hard.

one time, i took one of those notes—i was new at this—and turned it into a broad parody. showed it to some pilots, and they printed it in the 'New Orleans True Delta.' it wasn't my finest moment. it hurt a good man, Captain Sellers, who didn't deserve it. i didn't get how bad it was to be mocked in print till later.

Captain Sellers hated me after that, and i mean, it was an honor. he was a big deal, and while he loved many, he only stayed up nights to hate me. that's something.

never printed another word while he was alive. didn't sign 'Mark Twain' again either. when the news of his death hit the wires, i was on the pacific coast. fresh journalist and needed a pen name. grabbed the old mariner's discarded one. tried to keep it as a mark of truth, like it was with him. how well ive done, not my place to say.

the captain had pride in his work. loved it. ordered his monument before passing, kept it close till the end. now it stands over him at bellefontaine cemetery, st. louis. it's him in marble, at the wheel. ready for any critic, cause he would've stayed there till he burned, if duty called.

best view on our mississippi trip? coming into new orleans on a steam-tug. city curved like a crescent, lit by five miles of electric lights. stunning and beautiful.

CHAPTER 51

Reminiscences

left for st. louis on 'city of baton rouge,' hot day, but didnt get much done. wanted to talk to a hundred steamboatmen, got caught up in the town's social life instead. managed only short chats with a few.

on the pilot-house bench when we backed out and set off. boat took its time, black smoke rolling up. started moving fast soon enough. everything looked the same, felt like no time had passed since my river days. a 'cub' was at the wheel, figured he'd take control, and he did. captain bixby stepped in. the cub gave too much space between us and the ships, made me uneasy. saw it coming, knew the feeling from my own past. captain took over, brought us close, scraping by. same thing he did for me, years ago, first time out of new orleans. seeing it again, with someone else, was a real pleasure.

hit natchez in twenty-two and a half hours. fastest run ive had on that stretch.

next morning, took the four o'clock watch. watched ritchie handle crossings in a fog, using a marked chart he and bixby patented. proved its worth.

fog cleared a bit, saw a tree's reflection in the water, stronger and darker than the tree itself. those spectral trees in the fog, quite a sight.

had a hell of a storm at Natchez, then Vicksburg, then 50 miles below Memphis. those storms packed a punch i hadn't felt in a while. the third one came with a wild wind. we tied up to the bank when we saw it coming. everyone bailed from the pilot-house except me. wind bent the young trees, showing the pale undersides of leaves. gusts hit hard, shaking branches up, down, side to side, making waves of green and white. those waves raced like they do over a field of oats. everything was tinted dull by the heavy clouds. the river, the distance, even the white-caps looked dim under that dark sky. thunder was constant, explosions one after another, sharper, higher, painful on the ears. lightning as busy as thunder, enchanting and nerve-wracking. rain poured like mad, thunder cracked closer, wind got fiercer, ripping off branches, sending them flying. pilot-house rocked and creaked. went down to check the time.

folks brag about Alpine thunderstorms, but the ones in the Mississippi Valley? unmatched. maybe the Alps have a better show, but if they top this, i don't want to know.

saw a little towhead, half-mile long, popped up in just 19 years. if 19 years can make a towhead, why rush the world in six days? slow down, maybe we'd avoid all this fixing. rush a world or a house, and you miss a towhead or a broom-closet, and you've got to fix it later, no matter the cost.

we had a run of dark nights heading up the river. whenever we hit land and blasted the trees with the electric light, something cool happened. birds shot out from the green, like we flipped a switch, flying wild in the beams. sometimes a songbird would start singing. guess they thought it was real daylight. the trip was a joy on that well-run steamer, too fast though. we managed to catch up with almost all the old friends. one was gone; passed two years back. learned about him, made me see how small things stick. 

back when he was a blacksmith's apprentice and i was a schoolkid, two young brits showed up, dressed like royalty, and did a crazy Richard III swordfight for us village kids. that blacksmith got hooked, stage-struck for life. he vanished, popped up in St. Louis. i found him dreaming on a street corner, thinking he was Othello or some tragic hero. the crowd didn’t notice, but he believed they did.

i tried to bring him back to earth, but he told me, pretending calm, that he was with the Walnut Street theater. he was in Julius Caesar that night, wanted me to watch. said i wouldn’t miss it for anything.

i left shocked, thinking, 'we thought he was a fool, but in a city full of smart folks, his hidden talent got seen and praised.'

but at the theater, no sign of him, his name not even listed. next morning, he asked if i saw him.

'no, you weren't there.'

he was confused. 'yes, i was. i was a roman soldier.'

'which one?'

'didn't you see the roman soldiers, standing in line, marching in circles?'

'you mean the roman army? those six guys in nightshirts with tin shields, led by a stick-thin guy?'

'yep, that was it! i was the second to last. half a year ago i was always last, but i've moved up.'

they said the guy stayed a Roman soldier all the way to the end—thirty-four years. sometimes, they'd let him talk, but nothing big. "my lord, the carriage waits," was about his limit. give him more, and his memory just couldn't handle it. poor guy spent over thirty years trying to nail the part of Hamlet, thinking one day he'd get his shot.

all this because those young Englishmen stopped by our village so long ago. he could've been making perfect horseshoes instead of being a so-so Roman soldier.

a couple days after getting to St. Louis, i was on Fourth Street. an old guy sees me, stops, checks me out, looking angry, then says, "you got that drink yet?"

thought he was crazy at first. then it hit me who he was. tried hard to look embarrassed and said, "been slow, but i'm close. come help me out."

he lightened up, said make it champagne and he'd join. he saw my name in the papers, dropped everything to find me and get an answer or else. most of his anger was just for show.

that meeting took me back to the St. Louis riots about thirty years ago. spent a week there, boarding house, this guy was my neighbor. we saw the fights and killings. one night, joined 200 guys to arm up and face the rioters. drilled till ten. word came the mob was strong downtown, wrecking everything. we marched toward the chaos. hot night, heavy musket. asked my buddy to hold it while i got a drink, then just went home. figured he was armed well enough to handle things. would've got him another musket if i'd worried. left town early next day. if he hadn't seen my name in the papers and hunted me down, i'd never know if he made it out of the riots okay. should've asked back then, but with only one musket, he seemed better set to deal with it.

one monday, close to when we were headed to st. louis, the 'globe-democrat' published some sunday stats. showed 119,448 folks in st. louis hit church services, morning and evening, and 23,102 kids went to sunday school. so, 142,550 people out of 400,000 in the city kept the day religious. found these numbers in an associated press telegram and saved them. seemed like st. louis was more devout than back in my day. but when i dig into the numbers, feels like the telegraph got it wrong. can't be more than 150,000 catholics there; the rest are protestants. from those 250,000 protestants, just 26,362 went to church and sunday school. meanwhile, 116,188 catholics showed up.

chapter 52

a burning brand

suddenly thought, 'i haven't looked for mr. brown.'

gonna take a side road here from my main topic. got a secret i've held for nine years, kinda heavy now.

nine years back, i said with a lot of feeling, 'if i ever see st. louis again, i'll find mr. brown, the big grain merchant, and shake his hand.'

here's the story. a clergyman friend of mine stopped by one evening and said—

got a letter here that's too good not to share, if i can get through it without choking up. gotta give you some background first. this letter's from a guy who used to be deep in crime, rough start and all. but turns out, there's gold in him, and you'll see. he's writing to a burglar named williams, who's doing nine years in state prison for a big burglary job. williams was no small-time crook — harvard grad and from a good family. dad was a preacher. he got sick in jail, started thinking hard about his life, and turned over a new leaf. some local ladies heard about it, visited him, and helped him stick to his new path. he got sentenced, and in prison, he met jack hunt, the guy who wrote the letter i'm about to read. their friendship changed hunt. when hunt got out, he ended up in st. louis and wrote to williams. letter didn't get to williams — prison rules — but the warden didn't have the heart to trash it. it went around and eventually reached some ladies i mentioned earlier. a preacher friend of mine saw it too and couldn't stop talking about it. he got me a copy, imperfections and all, and here it is:

st. louis, june 9th 1872.

mr. w---- friend charlie, if i can call you that. i know you're surprised to hear from me, but i hope you ain't mad. gotta thank you for what you said to me in prison — it got me trying to be better. you probably thought i didn't care then, and at first, i didn't. but i knew you were the real deal, not a fake, and the boys knew it too.

i used to lay awake thinking about what you said, so i quit swearing before my time was up. saw it wasn't doing me any good. when my time was up, you told me if i quit stealing and lived straight for a few months, it'd be the best move i'd ever make. state agent gave me a ticket here, and on the car ride, i kept thinking about your words but hadn't made up my mind. got to chicago, and on the ride from there, i swiped an old woman's purse. soon as i did, i regretted it. i'd decided to go straight for a while, but forgot when i saw how easy it'd be. stayed close to her, though, and when she got off, i asked if she lost anything. she realized her purse was gone. i handed it back, and she called me honest. couldn't handle that, so i left quick.

got here with $1.25 and no work for 3 days. too weak for deck hand work on a steamboat. spent my last dime on a biscuit and cheese, feeling low. thought about going back to picking pockets, but then remembered you saying to call on the Lord when in trouble. tried praying, but all i managed was, "Lord, give a poor guy a chance to make it straight for 3 months, for Christ's sake, amen." kept thinking that over as i walked.

an hour later on 4th St., i heard a commotion. saw a horse running wild with a carriage and two kids inside. grabbed a piece of box cover, ran into the street, and smashed the horse over the head. board broke, horse slowed, and i pulled the reins till it stopped. owner came running, checked the kids, shook my hand, and handed me a $50 bill. remembered my prayer and was so stunned i couldn't let go of the reins or say a word. he noticed something was up...

coming back, he says, "my boy, are you hurt?" and i get this thought, maybe ask him for a job. so i did, told him to take back the bill and give me work. he says, "jump in here, let's talk." but he lets me keep the money. asked if i can handle horses. i said sure, been around livery stables, helped clean and drive. he needed someone for that, offered $16 a month and board. no hesitation, i took it.

that night, sitting in my room over the stable, thinking about my past and what just happened. got down on my knees, thanked the Lord for the job, asked for help to make it right, and to bless you for the push. next morning, did it again, got some new clothes and a bible. decided after what the Lord did, i'd read it every night and morning, ask Him to watch over me.

about a week in, Mr. Brown — that's his name — came by, saw me reading the bible. he asked if i was a Christian. told him no. he wondered why the bible, not papers or books. figured honesty was best, told him about prison, about you, how i almost gave up on work, and how the Lord got me this job. asked for a 3-month chance. he talked to me like a father, said i could stay. felt better than i ever had, no fear of anyone dragging up my past and running me off.

next morning, he calls me into the library, gives me another talk. advises studying a bit every day, offers to help an hour or two at night. gave me an arithmetic, spelling book, geography, and writing book. he tutors me every night. lets me join prayers in the house every morning, put me in a bible class at Sunday School. liking it a lot, helps me understand the bible better.

hey Charlie, so those 3 months on the square ended 2 months ago, and like you said, it’s the best work I’ve ever done. started another right away, hoping it’ll last a lifetime with God’s help. wrote to say I think God’s forgiven my sins and heard your prayers—since you said you’d pray for me. i love reading his word now, talk to him about my troubles, and he helps. i’ve had chances to steal but don’t feel like it anymore; prefer church over theater now, which wasn’t the case before. minister and others talk to me often, wanted me to join the church a month ago, but I held off, thought maybe I’m mistaken in my feelings. now I feel called and will join the first Sunday in July. wish I could write how I feel, but can’t yet—learned to read and write in prisons, still getting there. know I’ve misspelled and made mistakes, but you’ll forgive it, knowing I grew up in a poor house, never knew my parents, don’t know my real name. hope you won’t be mad, but I took your name—since you won’t use it when you get out, and you’re the one I admire most. doing well, saving $10 a month, got $25 of the $50 in bank. if you ever need it, let me know. wish I could send some now. included a receipt for a year of Littles Living Age; didn’t know what you’d like, Mr. Brown thought you’d enjoy it. wish I was closer, could send you food on holidays; it’d spoil in this weather, but I’ll send a box next Thanksgiving. next week Mr. Brown’s taking me into his store as a porter, will move up as I learn. forgot to mention my mission school, sunday school class—went out two Sundays, picked up seven kids, got them to come in. two knew as much as I did, so put them where they could learn. don’t know much myself, but they can’t read, so it works. make sure by picking them up every Sunday before school. got 4 girls to come too. tell Mack and Harry if they come here after their time, I’ll find them jobs. sorry for the long letter and mistakes, wish I could see you, writing isn’t like talking. hope the warm weather helps your lungs, was worried when you were bleeding. give my regards to all the boys, tell them how I’m doing—everyone here’s kind, Mr. Brown will write you sometime. hope you’ll write back one day. from your true friend

C---- W----

you know me as Jack Hunt.

sent you Mr. Brown's card. Send my letter to him.

now, here's something. that was raw power — didn't need frills. kind of writing that hits you deep. guy reading it had a shaky voice, even after practicing. wanted to see if he could hold it together for his prayer-meeting. turns out, not really. but he went ahead anyway. got through mostly okay, but the audience? they lost it from the start and stayed that way.

word about the letter spread around town. another minister borrowed it, used it in a sermon, and twelve hundred folks just lost themselves in it. then my friend tried it on his Sunday crowd. same reaction. house cried like one person.

so he takes this sermon on his summer trip up north. gets asked to preach in a packed church. big names in the crowd too. and this letter? worked its magic again. had everyone, including Dr. Holland, in tears. page, the philanthropist, was so moved he wanted to visit the prison to meet the writer.

now, here's the kicker. if only page and another guy had stayed away, that letter might've been legendary. but turns out, it was a sham. a masterful fraud, honestly. the best kind of swindle — just about perfect.

we didn't figure it out right away, though. weeks later, my friend and his preacher pals kept using it. i wanted it in a magazine, but only written copies were allowed, sent everywhere, even to the Sandwich Islands.

one day, Charles Dudley Warner drops a bomb at church. asks the preacher, "you sure that letter's legit?" first time anyone doubted it, and it hit hard. preacher asks, "why doubt it?"

Warner says, "it's too perfect for an amateur. i think it’s an educated man’s work."

the literary artist spotted the gears turning behind the words. if you check out the letter, you'll see it too—it's in every line.

so the clergyman, now suspicious, wrote to a minister in the town where williams was jailed and converted. he wanted more info, and asked if a writer (me) could print the letter and tell its story. soon, he got this reply—

Rev.--------

dear friend, about that convict's letter, no doubt on its authenticity. williams, who it was written to, was in our jail and claimed conversion. Rev. Mr.----, the chaplain, had strong faith in the change—strong as you can in these cases.

the letter went to one of our sunday-school teachers—either from williams or the prison chaplain. she's worried about all the attention, fearing it might breach confidence or harm williams. i can't authorize publication, but leaving out names and places, and sending it overseas, you might take the risk.

it's a powerful letter no christian genius could write, let alone a non-believer. it shows grace working in a wicked heart, proving its origin and rebuking weak faith.

someone mentioned 'Mr. Brown' from St. Louis was from Hartford. do all your Hartford folks serve as well?

P.S.—williams is still doing a nine-year stint in the state prison. he's been ill, maybe consumption. this lady likely corresponds with him and keeps tabs.

this letter came days later—williams's stock went up again. Mr. Warner's suspicion got buried, as it should. the suspicion was all internal evidence, which is a wide field where anyone can play: see the note above, calling it a wonderful letter no unsanctified person could write.

now i had the green light to print, with names and places hidden, and sent abroad. picked an australian magazine—far enough—and started my article. ministers got the pumps going again, using the letter.

meanwhile, Brother Page stirred things up. he didn't visit the prison but sent the famous letter to the chaplain there with questions. he received a reply four days after that upbeat letter; before my article was done, it landed in my hands. the original's right here, stuffed with solid internal evidence—

STATE'S PRISON, CHAPLAIN'S OFFICE, July 11, 1873.

hey bro. page—got that letter you sent me. not sure it's real. says it's for a prisoner here, but no record of it coming through. we read all incoming mail before the inmates see it, and we'd remember this one. also, charles williams isn't the saint he's pretending to be. guy's a crafty one, not the christian type, uses a fake name. good to know you. working on a talk about prison life, might bring it to your area.

so, that story's done. my article? went up in smoke. had better info now, but everyone around me who wanted it published before is saying wait. "too fresh" they say. all copies of that famous letter vanished, except mine. town had a laugh, but some places didn't find it funny. mentioning the ex-convict's letter was risky.

quick backstory. 'jack hunt', who supposedly wrote the letter, never existed. williams, the burglar—harvard guy, preacher's son—wrote it to himself. slipped it out of prison, got it to his supporters. they wouldn't question the letter's truth. the real point? to stir up some sympathy and get him pardoned.

the clever bit is buried at the end: "hope the warm weather's helping your lungs... worried you'd die when you were bleeding." just a quick line, but meant to catch the right eye and push for his release.

first time i heard that letter, nine years back, thought it was something else. it made me want to meet mr. brown of st. louis. but turns out, mr. brown was as real as 'jack hunt'. all another trick by williams—thief, harvard grad, preacher's kid.

CHAPTER 53

my old home

we hopped on a fast boat from the st. louis and st. paul packet company, headed up the river.

as a kid, first time i saw the missouri river's mouth, it was 22-23 miles above st. louis. bank erosion's moved it down eight miles since. pilots say it'll cut through and move five more miles in five years, putting it just ten miles from st. louis.

around nightfall we rolled past Alton, Illinois. Big town now. Then before dawn, we hit Louisiana, Missouri. Used to be just a sleepy little place but now it's buzzing with trains. All those towns are train centers these days. Funny thing, couldn't even recognize it. Back when I left the rebel army in '61, did my retreat there. Not by the book, but it worked. Looking back, my retreat was better than any advance I managed.

there's a railway bridge here, lit up like a Christmas tree. Looked pretty amazing.

by 7, we reached Hannibal, Missouri, where I grew up. Last saw it fifteen years ago, then a quick look six years before that. Those glimpses didn’t stick. All I remembered was how it was when I left 29 years ago. That image stuck like a photograph. Stepping ashore felt like coming back from another lifetime. Like those old prisoners seeing their city after years locked away. New houses stood firm, but through them, I saw the ones I knew, clear as day.

it was Sunday morning, streets empty, everyone still in bed. Walked through, seeing what was, not what is. The ghosts of old places shook hands with me. Climbed Holiday's Hill for a view. Town was laid out beneath me, every spot familiar. Felt a tug at the heart, realizing many I knew are gone, some maybe up above, others elsewhere. It all pulled me back to being a kid, until I remembered... fifty old houses down there, each with someone who wasn't even born when I left or an old face that was once young.

from up there, the view of the river and Illinois was stunning. Might be one of the best on the Mississippi, but saying that’s risky with the whole stretch offering views. Maybe I’m biased, but it felt perfect. Unlike old friends who’ve aged and worn down, this view stayed young and fresh, unchanged, and didn’t weight me down with life’s scars and stories.

so i’m out early, just walking, when i run into this older guy. we chat about the weather, then drift into other stuff. i don’t recognize him. turns out he’s been here 28 years, so he came after my time. started asking him about some folks from way back, like a sunday school buddy. 

‘graduated with honors somewhere east, disappeared, didn’t make much of himself, probably went downhill.’

‘he was smart, had promise back then.’

‘yeah, but you see what happened.’

asked about another bright kid from school.

‘same story. honors, but life beat him down. died out west, a beaten man.’

another kid?

‘he’s a success, always was, always will be.’

another guy who came to town to study.

‘switched from law to medicine or the other way, started drinking, gambling. ended up in mexico, died broke and alone.’

‘shame, he was such a good-natured guy.’

brought up another boy.

‘he’s fine. still here, got a family, doing well.’

same with others. asked about some schoolgirls.

‘first two are married here, got kids. the other died long ago, never married.’

i mentioned an old flame with some feeling.

‘she’s alright. married three times, buried two, divorced one, now looking at marrying again in colorado. got kids scattered all over.’

others? ‘killed in the war.’

another boy.

‘now there’s a story! everyone thought he was a fool, but he’s the top lawyer in missouri now.’

‘seriously?’

‘yep, it’s true.’

‘how’d that happen?’

‘well, if you send a fool to st. louis and don’t tell them he’s a fool, they’ll never know. if i had a fool, i'd ship him there. think about it, isn’t that wild?’

‘yeah, it’s something. maybe folks here were wrong about him, not st. louis.’

‘nah, we knew him from birth. if you got fools to offload, st. louis is the place.’

talked about a bunch of folks i used to know. some are dead, others moved, some did well, some didn't. for about a dozen, the story's good: 

'still doing well, still around, and the town's full of their kids.'

asked about Miss----.

'died in the asylum a few years back. never left once she went in; always suffered; never got her mind back.'

if that's true, it's a tragedy. thirty-six years in a madhouse all because of some young fools' prank. i was a kid then, saw those girls sneak into her room at night. one wore a shroud and a doughface, tapped her on the shoulder, she screamed, had fits, and lost her mind. seems crazy now that folks believed in ghosts not too long ago. but they did.

after asking about others, i asked about myself:

'oh, he did alright—another damn fool. would've done better if they sent him to st. louis.'

glad i told this blunt guy my name was smith.

CHAPTER 54

Past and Present

left alone up there, i started picking out old houses in town, remembering who lived there. came across Lem Hackett's dad's place (not his real name). took me back a generation, to a time when life's events weren't from big laws but from special orders. they had clear purposes—punitive, admonitory, and usually local.

when i was a kid, lem hackett drowned on a sunday. he fell out of an empty flat-boat, playing around, and sank fast like he was full of rocks. sermon that night said it was judgment. we knew. storm hit hard, lightning flashin, thunder crackin, like heaven was mad about lem. i sat up in bed, thinking they were watching us, maybe me more than anyone. figured id be next, fire and all, right there with lem. felt guilty for being mad at him, but couldn't shake it. every flash, thought i was done for. started thinking about other boys, maybe they'd take the heat off me. jim broke a window, tom swore more than anyone, and john fished on sunday but didn’t catch much. tried to act like i was just worried for them. while doing all this, i left my candle burning, drawing attention to myself. real dumb move, so i put it out fast.

last night was rough, maybe the worst ive had. felt guilty for stuff i knew i did wrong, and even for things i wasnt sure about. like some angel had a list of my mistakes. then it hit me. id messed up bad by blaming those other guys. figured they were probably dead from lightning by now.
