i got so scared thinking about it, decided id change. join the church, stop sinning, help people, even if they didnt need it. figured id go full-on saint mode, maybe even become a missionary if being good didnt take me out early.

but when morning came, those boys were fine. started to think it was all just about Lem Hackett, not me. world looked safe again, so i kinda let the reform plans slide. felt chill until the next storm hit, three weeks later.

that storm was weird. 'Dutchy' drowned that day. he was in our sunday-school, smart kid, but didnt know when to come in out of the rain. he was crazy good at memorizing scripture. recited three thousand verses one sunday, then drowned the next day.

we were swimming in this muddy creek with these green hickory hoop poles sunk deep. we'd dive and hold onto the poles to stay under. Dutchy wasnt great at it, kept getting laughed at. finally asked us to count fair for once, no tricks. said 'all right, Dutchy', but we exchanged sneaky winks.

dutchy took the plunge, and instead of counting, the boys followed one of their own, hiding behind a blackberry bush. they were already cracking up thinking about dutchy coming up to silence—no applause, just empty air. they were muffling laughs, just waiting.

time ticked. one kid peeped through the briers and said, surprised, "why hasn't he come up yet?" and just like that, the laughter died.

"man, that dive was something," one of them said.

"forget that," another replied, "this joke's even better."

after a few more comments, they stopped talking, just peering through the vines. soon their faces shifted from uneasy to scared. water stayed calm. hearts pounded, and we all ended up standing on the bank, looking from each other to the water.

"someone's gotta go down there!"

clear as day, but no one wanted that job.

"draw straws!"

we did, hands shaking like we were lost. my lot, so down i went. water was muddy, couldn't see squat, but i felt around. suddenly, a limp wrist. no response. i let go quick, scared stiff.

dutchy got caught in the hoop poles, tangled up. i surfaced with the bad news. some of us knew he might be saved if pulled out fast, but no one thought of that. brains just blank. little ones cried, and we scrambled into clothes—wrong ones, inside-out, whatever. then we bolted to raise the alarm, never looking back at the scene. more pressing things: ran home, ready to lead better lives.

night fell. then came that storm, wild and baffling. had me dazed. couldn't make sense of it. like, if a kid who knows three thousand verses isn't good enough, what chance for anyone else?

i never doubted the storm was because of dutchy. the thought that bugged me was, if he wasn't enough, turning a new leaf wouldn't matter. i'd fall short of dutchy, no matter what. i turned it over anyway, fear made sure of that. but in a month, sunshine and cheer had me back to my old ways, lost but comfortable.

as these memories faded, breakfast time rolled in. snapped back to the present and headed down the hill.

on my way to the hotel, passed by the old place from my childhood. back then, folks living there now would’ve been worth something, but now same as me. times change. they’re colored folks now.

after breakfast, wandered out to see how sunday-schools look these days. wanted to compare today’s kids with the ones i grew up with. used to go to a little brick church called ‘Old Ship of Zion’ back in my day. found the spot, but the church was gone. replaced with some fancy new building. kids looked sharper and dressed better now, didn’t see any familiar faces. had an odd feeling, like i’d tear up if i was the type. thought about those old friends, some i loved, some i didn’t, but all mattered in some way. where are they now?

felt a bit nostalgic, wanted to just stand there, but an old sunday-school mate spotted me. ended up talking nonsense to the kids to hide what i was really feeling. wasn’t good at impromptu speeches, so i tried to dodge it later, but got pulled up front in another sunday-school. used the chance to look at the kids. couldn’t remember those silly speeches from back then, wish i could, would’ve given me more time to look. ended up dragging out my talk just to get a good look at them, and admitted that was why.

didn’t spot the Model Boy. ours back then was too perfect, a real prig. mothers loved him, sons hated him. heard what he’s up to now, but it didn’t impress me, so i’ll skip the details. he did well in life.

spent three days in town. mornings felt like i was still a kid, seeing all those young faces in my dreams. come bedtime, i felt ancient. reality hits hard when you're face-to-face with old friends who've aged just like you.

at first, i got thrown off. met some young women who seemed unchanged, but turns out they were the daughters or grand-daughters of those i remembered. someone tells you a fifty-year-old's a grandma, no biggie. but when it's a girl you knew, that's a different story. takes time to wrap your head around everyone aging with you.

noticed women changed more than men. some guys looked barely touched by thirty years, but their wives, man, they'd aged. good women, though. wears you down, being good.

wanted to see this saddler, but he's been dead for years. guy used to run down the street, coat flapping, whenever a steamboat came. everyone knew he wasn't expecting anything, but he loved the act. made it look like he was waiting on a mountain of saddles. local paper mocked it, called the town 'stavely's landing.' as a kid, i envied his hustle—the show of it, the thrill.

then there was a carpenter i idolized. a master liar, though i swallowed every word. full of drama and mystery. once, while planing a board, he sighed and muttered, "o god, it is his blood!" hooked me right there, thinking he was deep into some dark past. finally, he whispered to me—

"can you keep a secret?"

i swore i could.

"a dark and dreadful one?"

assured him i could handle it.

"i must relieve my burdened soul, or i shall die!" he said.

he warned me to be 'silent as the grave.' then he confessed he was a 'red-handed murderer.' held out his hands, looked at them, said—

"look—with these hands i have taken the lives of thirty human beings!"

he got all fired up telling me his stories, ditching the general stuff and diving right into the grit. started with his first murder. told me every detail about how he covered his tracks, then onto the next and the next. he always used a bowie knife, and man, when he whipped it out to show me, it made my skin crawl.

after that first session, i went home with six of his crazy secrets rattling in my head. my dreams were on overdrive. kept going back to him every chance i got, like on saturdays. whole summer spent with this guy, hooked on his tales. each time he had something new and chilling. names, dates, places—he laid it all out. eventually figured out two things: his victims were all over the world and all named Lynch. every saturday more were added, till my curiosity got the better of me. asked him why all Lynches.

he said he'd never told anyone, but he'd trust me. shared the story of his tragic life. he'd loved this girl, she loved him back, but a rival, Archibald Lynch, threatened to kill her if he couldn't have her. didn't take it seriously, married her anyway. right at the altar, Lynch killed her with a knife. husband swore to wipe out every Lynch alive, using the same knife, leaving a cross mark on each.

said the cross was known worldwide, wherever a Lynch went, so did he. warned me to keep quiet. said one day, a Lynch would turn up dead, marked, and he'd disappear. he'd been reading some book, 'Jibbenainosay,' got his head all twisted by it. but i didn't know the book then, so i bought his stories, thinking it was all real.

we had this Lynch guy in town. thinking about what might happen to him kept me up at night. felt like i had to save him, but also needed sleep. finally decided to warn him, told him to keep it quiet, advised him to 'fly.' expected he'd take it seriously. but he laughed, dragged me to the carpenter's shop, mocked the guy, slapped him, made him beg. then Lynch just walked away, leaving me to see my hero was just a fraud.

the carpenter tried to bluster and threaten Lynch, all big words and no bite. didn't impress me at all anymore. felt ashamed for ever believing in him, stopped going to his shop. big loss, since i thought he was my greatest hero. he had some talent, though. his made-up murders were so vivid i still remember them.

hannibal's changed a lot since then. it's a city now, with all the fixings—mayor, council, water-works. probably got a debt too. fifteen thousand people live there, place is booming. streets are paved, which is rare enough around those parts. got half a dozen railways and a fancy new depot that cost a hundred grand. back in my day, the town was quiet, just a daily packet landing a passenger, buying a catfish, and leaving with another. now there's a big lumber business and lots more trade. money's flowing.

Bear Creek, funny name since it had no bears, is buried under heaps of lumber now. used to nearly drown in it every summer, only to be saved by some random person. can't even find enough water there now to drown a fly. it used to spread chills and fever like crazy. one summer, everyone got sick and the town had to be rebuilt. people say the gap between Lover's Leap and the hill was made by glaciers, but that's wrong.

there's a cave near Hannibal, wanted to check it out again but ran out of time. back then, the owner turned it into a tomb for his fourteen-year-old daughter. put her body in a copper cylinder with alcohol, hung it in the cave. tourists would pull it out to look and talk about it. strange times.

Bear Creek's changed. The slaughter-house is gone, and so is the old calaboose. Someone once asked if I remembered when Jimmy Finn, our town drunk, burned to death in there.

Now, history gets twisted over time and muddled by foggy memories. Jimmy Finn didn't burn there; he died in a tan vat, delirium tremens mixed with what folks called "spontaneous combustion." That was Jimmy's natural way to go. The real calaboose victim wasn't from town. Just a wandering tramp, soaked in whiskey. I know more than I like to about that night; it's not a fun tale to tell. 

That poor tramp shuffled the streets one cold night, pipe in hand, begging for a match. No one gave him any, not even a shred of kindness. Kids taunted him, me included, until something in his voice—some plea—cut through my shame. I fetched him matches and slunk off to bed, conscience heavy.

Later, the marshal locked him up in the calaboose. At two a.m., church bells clanged fire. Everyone rushed out. The tramp had set his straw bed alight. The oaken walls caught fast. When I got there, a crowd stood frozen, staring at the jail windows. He was there, behind the bars, screaming for help, lit up like a silhouette against the flames. The marshal was nowhere, the key with him. We improvised a battering ram, the blows promising rescue. But those beams wouldn't break. 

They said he clung to the bars even in death, the fire consuming him. I can't confirm. After I saw his face, I couldn't watch anymore.

saw that face every night after. felt guilty like i handed him the matches to burn himself. thought id hang if they found out. memories from that time are burned in deep, entertaining now but back then, distressing. if anyone mentioned it, id listen hard, always worried id be suspected. my guilty conscience caught suspicion in things that didnt mean anything. made me panic. hated hearing "murder will out". heavy load for a ten-year-old.

forgot one thing though—talked in my sleep. one night woke up, my brother was staring at me, moonlight shining. asked him what was wrong.

"you talk too much in your sleep."

sat up quick, heart racing.

"what did i say? tell me."

"not much."

"thats a lie. you know everything."

"everything about what?"

"about that."

"what? you're crazy. anyway, you're awake. im sleeping now."

he slept. i lay there sweating, mind spinning. how much did i say? what does he know? uncertainty's a killer. got an idea—test him with a made-up story. woke him up.

"imagine a drunk guy comes to you—"

"i dont get drunk."

"not you, the guy. say he borrows a knife or pistol, and you forget to say its loaded, then—"

"how do you load a tomahawk?"

"not the tomahawk, the pistol. listen, this is serious. someone died."

"here?"

"yeah, here."

"ok, go on."

"so, he shoots himself, not knowing. would that be murder?"

"no, suicide."

"no, i mean you giving him the pistol. would that be murder?"

he thought, then said, "maybe guilty of something, maybe murder, not sure."

that didnt settle it. had to lay out the actual case, but carefully. asked if he knew how the guy got burned in the calaboose.

"no."

"not the slightest idea?"

"none."

"swear on it?”

"yep, wish i might drop right here."

"so here's the deal. this guy needed matches for his pipe. a kid got some for him. then the guy torched the calaboose and burned himself up with those matches."

"really?"

"yep. now, would you say that kid's a murderer?"

"let's think. was the guy drunk?"

"yeah, he was."

"really drunk?"

"yep."

"and the kid knew?"

"he knew."

long pause. then the verdict—

"if he was drunk, and the kid knew, that kid killed the man. clear as day."

felt like getting a death sentence. wondered what my brother would say next. i was right—

"i know the kid."

couldn't say a thing; just shuddered. then he added—

"knew halfway through it was Ben Coontz!"

felt like coming back from the dead. asked—

"how'd you guess?"

"you talked in your sleep."

thought, 'that's a skill to work on.'

brother kept going—

"you mumbled 'matches' in your sleep; made no sense then. but when you mentioned the calaboose and matches here, remembered you said Ben Coontz a few times. pieced it together."

told him he was sharp. then he asked—

"gonna turn him in?"

"nope. think he learned his lesson. i'll watch him, that's fair; but if he straightens out, i’m not betraying him."

"how kind!"

"i try. it's all you can do in this world."

once the weight lifted, fear vanished.

before leaving Hannibal, noticed something—time's weird stretch out there. a friend's coachman, real low-key guy, taught me. supposed to pick me up at 7:30, didn't show till ten. said—

"time's slower in the country, boss. you'll be on time. sometimes we leave early for church, end up in the middle of the sermon. can't predict it."

lost two and a half hours; learned something worth more.

CHAPTER 57

an archangel

from St. Louis north, you see folks working hard. no dreaming, just doing. shows in how solid everything looks, life feels good.

Quincy's a prime example—bustling, nice-looking, organized city; still into art, books, and the like.

but marion city... yeah, that's a whole different story. supposed to be a big deal; they even slapped 'city' on it right from the start. didn't pan out. when i first rolled through 35 years back, it had a street and maybe six houses. now? one house, barely standing, ready to slip into the river. too close to quincy. plus, it sits low in a mud flat, below high-water mark. quincy's perched on a hill.

quincy looks like a new england town — wide, clean streets, neat houses, lawns. big mansions, commercial buildings. got fairgrounds, a park, drives, library, reading rooms, couple colleges, fancy churches, grand courthouse on a square. 30k people call it home. factories buzz here, manufacturing's big.

la grange and canton, they're growing. alexandria? under water when i asked, but it'll pop up in summer.

keokuk, easy to spot. lived there back in '57 — real estate boom was wild. everyone bought, sold, except widows and preachers. they held on, got left when the market tanked. town lots sold high, like they were covered in greenbacks.

15k folks there now, growing steady. we hit it at night, couldn't see much. shame, since keokuk's known to be pretty. was nice back then, probably still is.

they finished the canal over the rapids, a big project from my time. eight miles long, 300 feet wide, no less than six feet deep. solid masonry, war department style, lasts like a roman aqueduct. cost a cool four or five million.

after catching up with old friends, we headed upriver. keokuk was a haunt for henry clay dean. met him once, folks talked about him. started poor, uneducated, taught himself on keokuk's curbstones. book in hand, lost in study, even with the city noise. learned everything, stored it all up there.

dressed worse than a wharf rat, ragged, mismatched, dirty. you wouldn't guess the brains he had from his getup.

he was a natural talker, picked it up in the field more than the classroom. when he'd hit the campaign trail, he'd draw farmers like bees to honey from miles out. politics was his game. he never needed notes—volcanoes dont. in '62, Claggett, son of Keokuk's famous Mr. Claggett, told me this about Dean—

back in '61, Keokuk was boiling with war fever. big rally planned at the Athenaeum. a stranger was supposed to speak, but he never showed. crowd packed in, sweating, getting restless. a manager, desperate, found Dean on the street, took his book, pushed him on stage to save the day.

as the crowd grumbled, eyes hit the empty stage. out walked Dean—looking like a scarecrow. mismatched socks, old trousers too short showing bare ankles, vest open, shirt wrinkled, cap askew. he stood there, just looking. first silence, then snickers, then full-blown laughter.

Dean didn’t flinch. stepped back, tossed his cap, started talking. no one listening at first. then, bam, he landed a hit. silence fell over them. he started firing off words like bullets, heating up, louder and faster. the crowd woke up, clapped, but he didn’t stop. unwound his neckerchief, tossed it, coat next, then the vest. he was a volcano, spitting fire and smoke, shaking the place with each word. crowd erupted, on their feet, waving handkerchiefs.

'when Dean came,' Claggett said, 'they thought he was a lunatic; when he left, an archangel.'

burlington's got that hill city vibe, like a lot of places up here, but it's got its own shine. 25,000 people hustling around with factories doing just about everything. it's a sober kind of place right now, because there's this big bill on the table about banning anything that isn't water. most folks with sense are on board, but the judges aren't sold.

it's got all the modern city kit too, like real fire services. new orleans, big as it is, still runs on the old independent system.

burlington's got that forward motion you feel in these river towns. they just built an opera house that's a real step up from the usual rundown theaters you see in towns this size.

we didn't get off in muscatine, but i saw it from the boat. used to live there ages ago. it's grown since then. back in the day, i had a run-in with a lunatic out in the fields who thought he was the devil's kid. managed to talk my way out of that one. he left for his dad's place, and i hope he's still there.

muscatine's sunsets, though. never seen anything like them anywhere else. the river turns into a canvas, and the colors just explode. it's the real sunset land here. i've heard the sunrises are just as good, but i can't say.

CHAPTER 58

up here, the big towns come one after another, with farms in between. the boat keeps pushing into the northwest, and with every mile, you just get more impressed. these folks are independent thinkers, well-read and always learning. every weak spot's got a school, a college, a library, and a newspaper. they run under law. you don't worry about a future for people like this.

this region's fresh out the gate, still finding its feet. it's done a lot while still cutting teeth. foreign tourists have missed it — they stick to the same old route from st. louis to new orleans, write their books, thinking they've seen it all. but those books don’t even touch on these upper river towns, because when they showed up, these towns weren't even on the map. the last tourist in 1878 did the same routine trip, not knowing anything north of st. louis.

but up here? towns popped up overnight. places like muscatine, winona, moline, rock island, la crosse, burlington, dubuque, davenport, st. paul, and minneapolis all sprang to life with thousands of people each. never made it into the travel guides, though.

i'm older than this region. when i was born, st. paul had three people, minneapolis one. that one guy in minneapolis? he watched it grow by almost sixty thousand before he passed. like a frog with a crowd.

the numbers i gave for st. paul and minneapolis are already old. i just saw a paper saying st. paul's at seventy-one thousand, minneapolis seventy-eight thousand. by the time this reaches you, those numbers will be dust.

caught a glimpse of davenport, another beauty on a hill, like the rest. comely towns, built well, clean, easy on the eyes. davenport's grown fast — in 1834, no one there, now thirty thousand. more kids in school now than the total population not long ago. it's got factories, papers, schools, telegraphs, alarms, fire departments, churches. two bishops call it home, too.

across from davenport is rock island. a big railroad bridge ties them. rock island's a gov't park now, loaded with forests and drives. in the middle, ten huge stone buildings, the gov't's workshops. it’s a national armory and arsenal.

we're moving up the river, through scenery you just cant beat. pass Moline, big on manufacturing; then Clinton and Lyons, all about lumber. Dubuque comes next, sitting on rich mineral ground. tons of factories, even a plow factory selling everywhere. this guy on the boat said, "find me a place that plows right, and if our mark ain't there, i'll eat that plow. no sauce needed."

this river stretch is loaded with Indian history. Black Hawk was a big name here. a bit down from Dubuque, there's Tete de Mort, where the French trapped some Indians long ago. starve or jump, their only choices. Black Hawk took on white ways later, buried near Des Moines in a mix of Christian and Indian style—military uniform and a cane instead of a horse.

above Dubuque, the Mississippi's olive-green, beautiful with the sun on it. not the clearest now, since it's flood time and muddy. the bluffs, man, they’re something else—steep green slopes topped with rocky crowns, all rich browns and greens. the river snakes through, dotted with islands and villages, rafts slipping by, and steamers disappearing around bends. it’s peaceful, like a dream, until the train crashes through, shattering the quiet with its noise. brings you right back to reality, reminding you of that cursed stock that tanks right after you buy it, and climbs only when you sell. still get chills thinking how close i came to being stuck with it. having a railroad on your hands must be a nightmare.

you can see the locomotive from the steamboat deck almost all the way from St. Louis to St. Paul—eight hundred miles. these railroads really hit steamboat business hard. our boat's clerk used to work on steamboats before the railroads. back then, people kept pouring in and freight was so much, boats couldn't keep up. captains were full of themselves—big shots, like Uncle Remus would say. the clerk summed up how it was back then:

'boat lands—captain up on the hurricane roof—stiff as a board—ramrod spine—kid gloves, fancy hat, hair parted at the back. guy on shore tips his hat and says—

'“got twenty-eight tons of wheat, cap'n—would be a favor if you can take it.”

'captain says—

'“i'll take two” and doesn't even bother to look at him.

'nowadays, captain tips his old hat, grins ear to ear, bows, and says—

'“glad to see you, Smith, glad to see you—looking good—haven't seen you looking so well in years—what you got for us?”

'“nuth'n”, Smith says, keeps his hat on, and turns away.

'eight years ago, captain was the boss. now, it's Smith's time. back then, every boat was packed—people stacked on the cabin floor, immigrants and harvesters loaded below. to get a top stateroom, you had to have noble blood or know the guy who shined the captain's shoes. but now, plenty of staterooms, no harvesters—there's a machine for that now, and they got on the train, not a boat.'

up here, we saw acres of lumber rafts coming down, not floating leisurely with wild crews playing fiddles and drinking. instead, pushed by a big stern-wheeler, crews were quiet, all business, no romance about them.

somewhere in the night, we navigated tight island chutes with electric light. behind us, solid black; ahead, a narrow water elbow, curving between dense foliage. every leaf and ripple stood out, lit like midday. strange, fine, striking.

passed Prairie du Chien, another of Father Marquette's spots, and after more hours through beautiful scenery, we hit La Crosse. a town of twelve or thirteen thousand, electric-lit streets, buildings impressive enough for any city. a solid town, and we used our hour well, exploring even if it was too rainy.

CHAPTER 59

Legends and Scenery

picked up more passengers at La Crosse, including an old guy who came here with the early settlers, knew every inch of the place. proud of it too. he said—

you head out towards St. Paul, and you might start thinking the Hudson's got competition. there's Queen's Bluff, standing tall at seven hundred feet, just as striking as anything. then there's Trempeleau Island, which is basically a huge mountain with steep sides and a bunch of old Indian stories. used to have rattlesnakes too. catch the sun right, and that view will stay with you. past Winona, there are the prairies, and then you hit the Thousand Islands. these islands are green, really green, like a thousand plush cushions floating on a mirror when the water's calm. then the bluffs rise up, rugged and dark, framing everything perfectly. you need that strong frame to make the picture pop.

the old guy threw in some Indian legends, but they didn't pack much punch.

after that history detour, he got back to the scenery. he knew the names like they were old pals, rolling them off his tongue like it was nothing. every now and then, he'd drop a big word or some flashy speech that made you think he was showing off a bit.

but let's hear him out—

'ten miles past Winona, you hit Fountain City, tucked at the foot of cliffs reaching up towards the sky, untouched by anything except maybe angels' wings.

'then it's smooth sailing through silver waters, nature's finest on display, till you get to Mount Vernon, six hundred feet up. there's an old hotel ruin there, back when Mount Vernon was a thing. now it's just empty.

'we keep moving. Chimney Rock flies by, six hundred feet of it, and right before Minnieska, there's a striking five-hundred-foot promontory. looks like a perfect cone, all wooded up, and the views from the top are something else. the whole scene is wild and lonely, a real memory-maker.

'next up, there's Lion's Head and Lioness's Head, carved by nature. the river widens, and suddenly the valley opens up, with rugged hills and green forests. Wabasha, City of the Healing Waters, sits in the prairies, and there's Lake Pepin, nature's masterpiece. you could stare at it for hours, never getting tired.

'and we keep on, till we see the Sugar Loaf and Maiden's Rock. legend has it, if you're out there at twilight, you might hear Winona's song in the breeze.'

then you see frontenac, big with tourists every summer. red wing's next, and then diamond bluff just standing there, looking all impressive. after that, it's prescott and the st. croix. soon, st. paul's in sight, a young giant of the north. it's pushing forward, leading with progress, cutting through with commerce and culture. it leaves jails, gallows, and pulpits behind.

'ever travel with a panorama?' 

'i used to.'

so i was right.

'still do it?'

'no, it's parked till fall. now i’m working on a tourist's guide for the st. louis and st. paul packet line.'

'you mentioned maiden’s rock and winona. is she the maiden? what's the story?'

'yeah, tragic legend. one of the most famous on the mississippi.'

we asked him to tell it, and he switched back to lecture mode, smooth as anything.

'near lake city there's maiden's rock. a beautiful spot, the sioux loved it for fishing and hunting. many families came, including one from wabasha's tribe. winona was a maiden promised to a lover from her band. but her parents wanted her to marry a famous warrior. they set the wedding date, she was heartbroken. she pretended to agree, went to the rock with them to gather flowers. once there, she stood on the edge, cursed her parents for their cruelty, sang her death-dirge, and jumped.'

'jumped who—her parents?'

'yep.'

'well, that's dramatic. a new twist on the usual indian legends. plenty of lover's leaps out there, but this one's unique. what happened to winona?'

'she was shaken up but got away before the coroner showed up. they say she found her true love and wandered off. lived happily ever after, wiser from the ordeal.'

glad he described the scenery. helped me see what i could and imagine what night hid.

the lecturer had a point, this region’s loaded with Indian tales. but i told him, most folks just say that to sound interesting and then stop there. why? cause if you actually hear the tales, you’ll find they’re not that exciting. i showed him some of this stuff i’d been collecting, and he agreed it was pretty weak—except for that Winona story. he suggested i check out Mr. Schoolcraft's book from fifty years back, probably out of print now. he claimed it had some real creative tales. said Longfellow used it for Hiawatha. like the 'Undying Head'—couldn't recall all the details, but he thought it would boost my respect for Indian storytelling. he mentioned these stories were around here when he first arrived, and Schoolcraft’s contributors wrote them down straight from the source, no embellishments.

found the book. lecturer was right. some legends in it prove his point. i'll share two — 'The Undying Head' and 'Peboan and Seegwun, an Allegory of the Seasons.' the latter's in Hiawatha, but it's worth reading in its raw form to see how a real poem can work without fancy meter.

PEBOAN AND SEEGWUN.

an old man sat alone in his lodge by a frozen stream. winter's end, fire almost out. he looked ancient, frail, snowstorm the only noise.

one day, as his fire died, a young man showed up. bright eyes, youthful energy, smile on his face. walked light, wearing sweet grass instead of a warrior's frontlet, holding flowers.

'my son,' said the old man, 'good to see you. come, tell me your adventures. let's spend the night. i'll share my exploits, you share yours, and we’ll enjoy ourselves.'

he pulled out an old pipe, filled it with a special tobacco blend, and passed it to his guest. once done, they talked.

'i blow my breath,' said the old man, 'and the stream freezes.'

'i breathe,' said the young man, 'and flowers bloom.'

old man says he shakes his locks, snow covers everything. Leaves drop off, his breath blows them away. Birds fly off, animals hide. Ground turns hard as flint.

young man jumps in, says he shakes his ringlets, warm rain falls. Plants perk up like kids' eyes shining. Birds come back, streams melt, music fills the air.

sun rises, warmth spreads. old man's voice fades. robin and bluebird sing, stream murmurs, scent of herbs on the breeze. Turns out old man was Peboan, winter. Sun melts him away. Just a little white flower left behind.

'The Undying Head' is a long one, full of weird twists and fairy-tale stuff. Moves fast, even if it's not short.

---

we made it to St. Paul, end of the Mississippi line. Trip was two thousand miles from New Orleans, took about ten days on a steamer. Rail might be quicker. St. Louis to Hannibal by train takes seven hours for a hundred and twenty miles. Beats walking unless you're racing.

was late in the season in New Orleans, roses and magnolias dropping. In St. Paul, it's snow falling. Had hot gusts like from a volcano in New Orleans, icy ones like from a glacier here.

getting back on track. St. Paul's solid, built to last. Post office set up thirty-six years back; mail used to get taken to Washington on horseback to figure out what to do with it. Legend says so. Couple of frame houses went up that year, few folks moved in. 'Pioneer Press' gives numbers now: population at 71,000 in 1882. Over a million letters handled first half of the year. Nearly a thousand houses built in nine months, costing over $3 million. Letters up fifty percent from last year. New buildings last year cost over $4.5 million. St. Paul's strong in commerce, even with manufacturing. Last year did over $52 million in jobbing trade.

he's got a custom-house going and a fancy capitol to replace the one that burned down. he's the capital of the State, after all. churches all over, not the cheap kind either. the ones that the rich Protestant goes for, and the ones the Irish 'hired-girl' loves to build. she's got a thing for putting up these grand churches. it's good for architecture, but we usually enjoy them without thinking about her hard work. every brick's sweat and pain, but we just see the temple, not the builder.

libraries and schools are everywhere. St. Paul’s got three public libraries with about forty thousand books altogether. one hundred sixteen schoolhouses, paying out over seventy grand a year for teachers.

the railway station's huge, seemed too big at first, but now it’s clear they actually needed it that size. they'll fix the error.

town's on high ground, about seven hundred feet above sea level. you get a wide view of the river and lowland from the streets.

it’s a work in progress, still not done. streets are full of building materials, turning into houses as quick as they can clear space for more. everyone’s eager to build.

funny how whiskey's always the first pioneer of civilization, not the steamboat or railroad or newspaper. look at history, whiskey leads. then comes the missionary, immigrant, trader, gambler, desperado. then the smart guy with a land grant, bringing lawyers and a vigilance committee. they all bring the newspaper, sparking politics and a railroad. then a church and a jail, and suddenly civilization's rooted. but whiskey, it starts it all. a foreigner might miss this truth and look to the stars for symbols. but really—

westward the Jug of Empire goes.

St. Paul's site got its first cabin and whiskey in June 1837, thanks to Pierre Parrant, a Canadian. he sold whiskey to the Indians. and now look at the place.

everything about the energy and growth of St. Paul fits Minneapolis too, except Minneapolis is bigger.

these towns were ten miles apart a while back, but they're growing so fast, they're probably joined now with one mayor running the show. in five years, a solid strip of buildings will connect them, making it tough to tell where one ends and the other starts. combined, they could hit 250,000 people if they keep growing like this. they'll start rivaling new orleans in size at the head of mississippi navigation.

minneapolis sits at the falls of st. anthony, stretching 1,500 feet across with an 82-foot drop. this waterpower got turned into business gold but kinda ruined the view. thirty flour mills crank out two million barrels of top-notch flour yearly; twenty sawmills cut two hundred million feet of lumber. there's also woolen, cotton, paper, and oil mills; and factories for sash, nails, furniture, barrels, you name it. flour mills in minneapolis and st. paul use the 'new process,' rolling wheat instead of grinding.

sixteen railroads converge in minneapolis, with sixty-five passenger trains coming and going daily. journalism's big here too: three dailies, ten weeklies, and three monthlies. there's a university with four hundred students, teaching both sexes. sixteen public schools cost $500,000 in buildings, with six thousand kids and 128 teachers. seventy churches are up, with more on the way. banks hold $3,000,000 in capital, and wholesale trade hits $50,000,000 a year.

near st. paul and minneapolis, you find spots like fort snelling on a river-bluff, minnehaha falls, white-bear lake. minnehaha's famous enough. white-bear lake's a pretty spot, a summer getaway for the wealthy, with clubs, hotels, fancy homes, fishing, hunting. it's the main resort, with minor ones around. there's an indian legend tied to white-bear lake. can't resist sharing it—an island on the lake sees indians every spring for maple sugar. tradition says a young warrior loved the chief's daughter there, but her parents always refused him, calling him no brave.

moon came up over the sugar-bush again. young warrior grabbed his flute, walked out alone under that bright sky. feathers on his head-dress moved with the breeze. climbed up a tilted tree trunk, snow falling heavy off his feet. started playing his wild love song, but got cold. reached back for his blanket, and felt a hand—his love, his guardian angel. she sat beside him, and right then, they were happy. 

meanwhile, a big white bear, thinking winter was everywhere, wandered south. reached the lake that now has his name, moved quietly through the snow to the island. spring came, and those lovers met again, sitting in a big elm hanging over the lake. whispering so no one would hear. planned to get back to camp without raising suspicion. but then, she slipped, falling with his blanket into the bear's grasp. 

everyone rushed to the shore, unarmed, helpless. the bear held her, like it’s nothing new. warrior let out a yell, ran to his wigwam for his knife, back in a flash. leapt at the bear with panther fury. bear turned, brought them heart to heart, but warrior struck, knife opening crimson streams, and bear let go.

no sleep that night. band danced around the dead beast. warrior got another feather, and soon, a new treasure in his heart. their kids played on that bear skin for years, lake named after it. Kis-se-me-pa and Ka-go-ka never forgot that battle with the monster that nearly took them.

what a mess. she falls out of the tree with the blanket, bear catches both. then she's back in the tree, no blanket. boyfriend goes wild, comes back armed, climbs, jumps on bear. she jumps down too—into the bear's arms again, blanket included. boyfriend stabs bear, saves the girl. but the blanket? not a clue. whole story's about the blanket, and in the end, no idea where it goes. feels like even a long-dead guy could spin a better tale. 

hit chicago, a city that's always changing, always doing the impossible. can't keep up with it, every visit it's new. pennsylvania road got us to new york on time, and that was one hell of a trip, 5000 miles of pure enjoyment.

APPENDIX A

from the New Orleans Times Democrat, March 29, 1882.

it was Thursday, 9 a.m., when the 'Susie' left the Mississippi and hit Old River. floods pouring over levees at Chandler plantation, Pointe Coupee parish. stock packed on a flat-boat, no food, waiting for a tow. Turnbull's Island on the right, once fertile, now underwater. levee tops barely visible, mostly submerged.

trees look greener now with all the water, but it’s a never-ending flood plain. mile after mile, trees just standing in water up to their branches. you spot a water-turkey every so often, flying ahead into the quiet emptiness. sometimes a pirogue darts out, crossing the Red River towards the Mississippi, paddlers too focused to notice our boat. the boat’s puffing is like music in this strange silence. it’s not like a dark forest, more like a solemn stillness you can’t ignore. this morning, passed two families on a raft tied to willows. they seemed well-off, had some meal and hogs with them. their rafts were about twenty feet each, with a patch of earth for a fire in front of their makeshift shelter.

the Atchafalaya’s current is fast, like the Mississippi’s trying hard to find a shortcut to the Gulf. small boats are in demand; some get stolen by folks looking to sell them for a high price. heard from Mr. C. P. Ferguson, a planter near Red River Landing, that there’s a lot of suffering behind that area. they weren’t ready for a breach, and when it hit, they were helpless. on Thursday, some were rescued from trees and rooftops, but many are still stranded.

you don’t miss solid ground till you’re floating over it. at sea, you don’t expect land, but here, with leaves fluttering, shadowy woods, and barely visible rooftops, you do. even a graveyard, if it wasn’t underwater, would be a sight. the river’s only visible where the trees break. from Fort Adams to Rapides Parish, about sixty miles wide, mostly farmland, especially near the Mississippi and behind the Red. when we hit Red River proper, the current was strong, matching the Mississippi’s direction.

after a few hours, hit Black River. signs of struggle are clear. willows along the banks stripped bare. spoke to a guy who had 150 cattle, 100 hogs. when water came, tried moving them to Avoyelles, 35 miles away, lost 50 cattle and 60 hogs. Black River looks nice, even if the banks are under water. ash, oak, gum, and hickory make it dense, hard to see far in the gloom.

a few miles up, water’s eight feet deep on the banks. you see cabin tops holding against the current. some overturned, surrounded by driftwood, maybe the start of a new island someday.

Saving coal was a must since we couldn't get any during the trip. We kept an eye out for wood. As we rounded a point, a pirogue with a young guy paddling and a girl in the bow came up. She was about fifteen, with fair skin and striking black eyes. The boy asked for a paper, which we tossed over, and they paddled back into the boat's wake.

Then this little girl, probably not older than twelve, came out in the tiniest canoe. She handled it like she'd been doing it her whole life, laughing when asked if she was scared. She was off to pick willow leaves for the stock and pointed to her house with water three inches deep inside. There was a raft tied up at the back door, packed with cows and hogs. The family didn't seem to mind much, except about losing their stock, and they brought us wood on a flat.

From there to the Mississippi, fifteen miles with no land above water. Westward, thirty-five miles of river flood. Black River rose three-quarters of an inch on Thursday, the 23rd, and kept rising. As we moved upriver, houses popped up more often, though still miles apart. Most were empty, with outbuildings floated away. Everything felt deserted. No birds, no squirrels—just quiet, the kind that feels like things are ending. Down the river, bits of life floated by: a hen-house, some fence rails, maybe a bloated carcass with buzzards picking at it. A picture frame with a cheap soldier print floated by, a sign of a home the water had claimed.

At nightfall, we tied up to a tall gum-tree by the woods. The moon was just right, lighting up the forest and river. Engines stopped, steam gone, and silence took over. Not the usual forest sounds—just quiet, like nature was holding its breath.

friday morning, we got moving up the Black. sky was clear, river looked straight as an arrow, dressed in spring’s best. haw blossoms made the air sweet, and a few birds threw in some tunes. bigger trees now, older forest compared to downstream. more fields too, but familiar sights—smokey shacks in pastures, quarters huddled by oaks, modest houses just peeking above the water. sun came up like a red flare, trees popping with greens. no soil in sight, water’s rising, licking the branches of big trees. willows stripped bare—folks been pulling leaves for animal feed. asked an old man in a pirogue how the willow leaves were treating his cattle. he paused, shook his head: "keeps 'em warm, but rough on hogs, especially the little ones. they’re dropping fast. but what can you do? it’s all we got."

thirty miles up from Black River’s mouth, water stretched from Natchez on the Mississippi to Louisiana’s pine hills—73 miles, all under ten feet of water. current runs west up the Black, pushing Red River waters down from Calcasieu country. Black’s waters now hit Red 15 miles upstream—a first for even the oldest steamboat guys. all this water we see is Mississippi’s gift.

up near Trinity, or Troy, folks mostly moved out, those left just covering personal needs. cattle aren’t faring well, stuck on rafts, bad feed spreading disease.

after a pause, we moved on and hit areas with open fields and cabins everywhere. more distressing scenes—inside houses, people built scaffolds on boxes for their stuff. bed-posts cut short, ceiling just four feet above makeshift floors. buildings looked ready to float off anytime. cattle nearby, standing chest-deep in water, still as stones. they don’t budge, waiting for help. tough sight—unless rescued soon, they’ll drown. cattle won’t wander like horses; they’ll just stand till they drop.

half past twelve, got a hail from a flat-boat near the bank line. We pulled alongside, and General York hopped aboard. He was busy getting stock off, and he was real glad to see the 'Times-Democrat' boat. Said we were needed bad since the situation was as rough as it sounded. Folks were struggling, hard to even picture it. Water was way up, close to sweeping away houses. Already hitting the eaves, so any higher and it's real dangerous. If those houses go, lives get lost. General talked about the brave folks trying to save their stock, but said around 25% didn't make it. Already handed out rations to 2,500 people from Troy, Black River, and towed a bunch of cattle, but plenty still in trouble. Water's 18 inches higher than it was in '74, no land showing between Vidalia and Catahoula hills.

by two, 'Susie' reached Troy, 65 miles up from Black River's mouth. On the left's Little River, then the Ouachita, and right's Tensas. These come together to form Black River. Part of Troy sits on three big Indian mounds, round and above water by 12 feet. Each about 150 feet across, 200 yards apart. Houses between 'em are flooded, 18 inches deep on floors.

these mounds, built by old tribes centuries back, are the only dry spots for miles. Found 'em packed with stock, all looking rough. Sheep, hogs, horses, mules, cattle all mixed together. One mound's a graveyard, even saw skinny cows resting against tombstones, chewing corn General York gave 'em. Women and girls real skilled with small pirogues, kids paddling like pros.

General York's got a solid relief system running. He checks where help's needed, sees what's to be done, then sends out boats with flats to load cattle and get 'em to higher ground in Catahoula. Troy's his base, boats come here for cattle feed. Across Little River, left of Black, between it and Ouachita, lies Trinity, under threat. It's lower than Troy, water's 8-9 feet deep in houses. Strong current sweeping through, surprised any houses still standing. Folks in Troy and Trinity are cared for, but some stock still need feeding.

soon as 'Susie' hit Troy, she got handed over to General York. he had her ready to speed up relief work. most of her supplies got dumped on a mound to lighten her up, then she headed downstream to help folks below. near Tom Hooper's, a few miles out, we picked up a big flat with about fifty animals. fed 'em, got 'em back on their feet. today, we're hitting Little River where it's rough.

DOWN BLACK RIVER

Saturday Evening, March 25.

early run down Black River with General York, aiming to save whatever stock we could reach. dropped a flat in a central spot, then men poled it behind plantations, picking up animals wherever they found 'em. found seventeen head in a gin-house loft, built a gangway, led 'em down into the flat easy enough. took a skiff with the General, got to a little two-room house with two feet of water on the floors. horses and cows crammed in one big room, Widow Taylor and her son up on a scaffold in the other. a couple dug-outs floating around, ready to go. when we got the flat up, had to cut away part of the house to get the animals out. drove the cattle onto the boat. General York asked the family if they wanted out, said Major Burke from 'The Times-Democrat' sent 'Susie' for it. Mrs. Taylor thanked him but said she'd stick it out. these folks hold tight to their homes, can't figure it. just downriver, sixteen miles from Troy, heard Tom Ellis's place was in danger, family stuck inside. got there quick, saw the mess. Mrs. Ellis, in bad health, peeking from a half-window above water. seven kids at the door, oldest not even fourteen. animals on one side, family on the other, water two inches from the bed-rail. stove underwater, cooking on a fire on top of it. house looked ready to collapse, one end sinking. Mr. Ellis came out in a dug-out, General York offered to move the family to the hills, stock to go Monday. despite the mess, Mr. Ellis wanted to wait it out. kids looked fine with it, not bothered by danger. that's just a couple of stories. after weeks of rough times, folks still hang on till there's no room to stand. love for their place stronger than fear.

left the Ellis place, headed to the Oswald spot next. flat's pulled up next to the gin-house, got fifteen head just standin there in water. they're on scaffolds, heads above the entrance, but stuck. had to cut a gap in the front with axes to get em out. got em onto the flat after a lot of work.

every stop, more dug-outs show, bringing news of stock needing help. lotta folks moved some stock to the hills earlier, but plenty still out there. General York's workin hard, aiming to get em all to the pine hills by Tuesday.

Black River's full of planters tellin the same stories of loss. old-timer since 1844 says he's never seen a rise like this, thinks a quarter of the stock's gone. folks put work stock first, horses and mules safe when possible. rise keeps comin, two inches last night, pushin folks to move em to the hills. General York's all over, directing calmly, giving hope. bad story about a New Orleans merchant—planters had balances with him, asked for basic supplies, got nothing. no one's happy with him now.

hills are in Catahoula parish, 24 miles from Black River. filled the flat with cattle, picked up T.S. Hooper's family, seven of em, couldn't stay in their home. takin them up Little River to the hills now.

flood's still rising.

Troy: March 27, 1882, noon.

flood's up three and a half inches every day, rains makin it worse. General York's shiftin focus to saving lives, water's puttin houses in danger. headin up the Tensas soon, then back down Black River to get families out. steam transport's short, General's got three boats and flats, but demand's too high. 'Susie' barely stops. Trinity's in trouble, houses might float off any time. Troy's a bit higher but still wet. heard a woman and child got washed away, two cabins off too. same folks who wouldn't leave two days ago. can't believe how passive some folks are.

Haven't heard anything about the steamer 'Delia' yet. It's supposed to be the one that went down in yesterday's storm on Lake Catahoula. Should've been here by now, but no sign. Even the mail's unreliable, so I'm sending this by skiff to Natchez to get it to you. Hard to get solid info on past crops and such—folks who know are gone, and the rest don't know much about the local production.

General York wanted me to tell you that the rations we sent before need to be doubled and shipped out ASAP. Can't make a good estimate since everyone's fleeing to the hills with the water rising so fast. The locals are in a panic you wouldn't believe unless you saw it—everything's falling apart.

If we draw rations for a specific area, there's no guarantee they'll get distributed right. Best to send everything to Troy as a hub, and the General will sort it out. He's asked for a hundred tents, but with all the folks heading for the hills, we'll probably need two hundred.

---

So after the war, the Lower Mississippi valley's a mess, and that's one of the real downsides of the war. Slave-based property was rightly wiped out, but that also took down a lot of work done by slaves, especially the levee system.

You might think the States would've jumped on fixing the levees, but with interest rates at 18-30% and farmers pledging crops before planting just to buy supplies at double costs, the States can't handle it.

It's clear that if the Mississippi River's gonna be managed, the national government has to step in. The river's gotta be treated as a whole, not in bits by different states.

The States can't team up for this. The work's gotta start way up the river, at least to Cairo, maybe further, and needs a consistent plan all the way down.

Doesn't take a genius to see the basics if you look into it a bit. The Mississippi River Commission is made of sharp folks from different fields, so their judgment should stick, at least as far as any theory on river control goes.

Remember, the board's got General Gilmore, General Comstock, General Suter from the U.S. Engineers; Professor Henry Mitchell, the top guy on hydrography; B. B. Harrod, Louisiana’s State Engineer; Jas. B. Eads, who nailed it with the New Orleans jetties; and Judge Taylor from Indiana.

Anyone challenging this board's judgment would be overstepping, no matter how skilled they are.

Alright, here's how it boils down. The commission's plan ties into what we've learned from engineering and nature. Just like trees stabilize banks when they fall, we're using timber and brush to do that for riverbanks. Cut the width with brushwood dykes, start low, raise 'em as the mud settles, then angle 'em back for willow growth. No need for contraction work on most of the river, but the banks on the concave side? They need reinforcement to handle the stream's wear. We're talking brushwood revetments, woven or wire-netted. Missouri River's seen success with this, those mats lock in sediments and willows grow over them, making 'em solid. We'll use some rubble-stone for securing, maybe pave with stone between high and low water areas.

If you've seen the Rhine, you've seen something similar. Lots of European rivers have needed this kind of work for navigation and farming.

Levees cap it off, though they aren't directly attached. They might sit back a bit from the bank, but they're crucial. To get a single stable channel, we need control over all river stages. Even the big rises need managing, since they threaten the levee and can wreck the revetments.

The idea is that the river's slope comes from the bed's resistance. A deep, narrow stream should have less slope since there's less friction. Levees and revetments deepen the channel, reduce slope. First, levees lift the surface, but that boosts flow speed, enlarging the section. If banks hold, the bottom erodes, improving flow. Mississippi's seen levee success without bank holding, but if early levees had revetments, we'd have a low-water navigable river and safe nearby land.

ok, so here's the deal with the Mississippi River. thinking you could lower flood risks to make levees unnecessary? not happening. but by controlling the river's path, we can shape it so even big floods won't wreck regular levees. the channel's capacity depends on how it handles floods, but not those weird, rare floods.

talking about making new outlets for the river? just not practical. some folks might think it's a good idea, but engineers don't back it. the riverbed isn't solid; it's flexible. best to stick with one deep channel for efficiency, not spread out with multiple paths.

now, this whole thing is about improving the river, and it's a big deal. it matters to everyone in the U.S. it's like fixing something that got messed up in a war, and it's a shared responsibility.

switching gears, let's talk about Captain Basil Hall's book. when it hit the U.S., it was like a shockwave. Americans were super sensitive about how they're perceived, and this book really hit a nerve. i was in Cincinnati when it came out, and the reaction was intense. bookstores were hesitant to sell it, but somehow it spread everywhere. the uproar showed that craving approval too much can lead to weakness.

man, it's wild how folks who usually have some sense can just lose it over this kind of thing. never saw common sense in national criticism get so wrecked by emotion. justice and fair interpretation? yeah, not happening here. we talk about thin-skinned nations, but these guys, they're like no skin at all. even a breeze sends them into a tizzy unless it's full of praise. so when a traveler's sharp observations hit, they took it hard. the real shocker? how mad they got and the silly excuses they made to explain away what they thought was harsh treatment.

they didn't just say the books were lies from start to finish (heard that more times than i can count), the whole country went to work figuring out why Captain Hall came to the U.S. and wrote his book.

folks seriously claimed, with a straight face, like it was some official report, that Hall was sent by the British Government to dampen England's admiration for the U.S. they said he was commissioned by the treasury and only found faults because he was ordered to.

this isn't just gossip; a big chunk of the country seems to believe it. they can't fathom being seen without being admired and can't handle the idea someone might honestly find flaws.

the American Reviews are known in England, so no need to quote them here. but i did wonder why they never translated Obadiah's curse into American English; swapping (he, Obadiah) with (he, Basil Hall) would've saved a lot of fuss.

i finally sat down to read these so-called scandalous volumes, and man, was i surprised. not a single exaggeration in the whole work. anyone who knows the country can see Hall tried to find stuff to admire. when he praises, it's genuine; when he criticizes, it's reluctant unless he feels it's for his country's good.

Hall got to see the country at its best. he had letters to all the big names and his own reputation helped too, so he was welcomed in style across the Union. he saw the country all dressed up, unlike me and my family, who often saw it raw and unpolished.

Captain Hall had some top-notch chances to dive into how the government and laws work, and chat with the big names about it all. He didn't waste a minute. Every important detail got the deep dive only a savvy traveler could give. Makes his books a solid read. But if someone else, equally sharp, just lived the day-to-day life here, they'd likely see the moral vibe of the country a whole lot lower than Hall did. I bet if Hall hadn't held back, he'd have hit harder on some American traits he knew well. His game plan was clear: tell enough truth to give readers a solid picture, but skip the extra pain for the folks he's writing about. He shares his thoughts and lets folks guess he’s got solid reasons, but he cuts the Americans slack from the harsh details that’d sting.

If someone wants to say my view comes from some deep-seated dislike for a bunch of strangers, fine, I'll take it. But this isn't just idle chatter.

. . . . . . .

His honesty, which he truly means, gets mistaken for sarcasm or plain disbelief; his effort not to hurt those who’ve been kind to him gets brushed off as fake. Even though they surely know deep down how much more he could've said, they still act like he blew their flaws out of proportion. Truth is, he let them off easy, though they might not deserve it, while going out of his way to boost their good points when he found them.

up north, there was this guy Iamo and his sister, living way out where they'd never seen another person. dude never had to go far for food. he'd just stick his arrows in the ground and tell his sister where to find 'em. every morning, she'd head out and find a deer dead on the spot, drag it back, and cook it up. this went on until she grew up. one day, Iamo tells her, "sis, you're gonna get sick soon. listen up. take our fire-starting gear, set up a fire away from the lodge. when you need food, i'll guide you. cook for yourself, and i'll do the same. if you get sick, stay away from the lodge, and keep your tools on you. you never know when you'll need 'em. i'll manage somehow." she agrees.

not long after, Iamo had to leave. she's alone, doing her hair, and takes off the belt with her tools. bang, it happens just like Iamo said. she bolts out but forgets the belt. stuck outside, she thinks it over and decides to dash back and grab it. she figures Iamo's gone, it'd be quick. she rushes in, snatches it, and bam, Iamo spots her. he knows right away. "didn't i warn you? now you've done it. i'm doomed." she's heading out, but he stops her. "what's the point now? it's done. stay here like always. what about you? you've sealed my fate."

he takes off his hunting gear. his feet start going black, can't move. still, he guides her on where to place the arrows for food. the infection creeps up to his ribs. he tells her, "sis, i'm near the end. follow my instructions. see my medicine-sack and war-club? it's got all my stuff. when the infection hits my chest, use the club, cut off my head. put it in the sack, hang it like before. don't forget my bow and arrows. keep one for food, stash the rest in the sack so i can face the door. i'll talk now and then, but not much." she promises to do as he says.

his chest started acting up. "now," he said, "grab the club and take my head off." she hesitated, but he told her to be brave. "do it," he said, smiling. she found her courage, swung, and the head came off. "now," the head said, "put me where i told you." she followed his orders, even though it scared her. the head still alive, looked around the lodge like normal, telling his sister where to go for the animals she needed. one day, the head said, "soon i'll be free from this, but i'll face hard times. the great spirit's will, gotta handle it." we leave the head there.

somewhere in the country, a village full of fierce indians. a family of ten brothers lived there. come spring, the youngest painted his face black and fasted. his dreams looked good. after fasting, he snuck out to gather his brothers so no one would know their path. the drum was heard, but that's common. after the rituals, he shared his dreams and asked if they'd join him for war. they all agreed. the third brother, known for his quirks, grabbed his war-club. "yeah," he said, "here's how i'll treat our enemies," and struck the post with a yell. the others told him, "easy, Mudjikewis, we're in someone else's lodge." he listened and sat. they each took the drum, sang, and ended with a feast. the youngest warned them not to tell their wives, to get ready quietly. they promised, Mudjikewis first.

departure time came up. they were to meet on a certain night to leave. Mudjikewis was shouting for his moccasins. his wife kept asking why. "besides," she said, "your pair's still good." "hurry," he said, "since you gotta know, we're going to war; now hurry up." he let slip the secret. that night, they gathered and left. snow on the ground, they traveled all night to avoid being followed. at daylight, the leader made a snowball and tossed it up, saying, "i dreamt of snow falling like this, so we can't be tracked." told them to stay close or get lost; snow came down in big flakes. even close, they could barely see each other. snow fell all that day and night, making tracking impossible.

they'd been walking for days, mudjikewis always trailing. one day, he rushed up, let out a war-whoop, and smashed a tree with his club. 'like this,' he said, 'ill deal with the ones we're fighting.' the leader said, 'slow down, mudjikewis, we're up against more than you think.' mudjikewis fell back, wondering, 'who could he be leading us to?' silent, feeling a bit of fear. day after day, they marched till they hit a plain, scattered with bones sun-bleached. the leader pointed, 'those are the ones who went before. none ever made it back to tell their tale.' mudjikewis got restless again, ran forward, yelled, and shattered a big rock with his club. 'see, brothers, this is how ill handle them.' the leader again said, 'keep calm, mudjikewis. what we face isn't like a rock.'

mudjikewis fell back, thinking hard: 'who is this enemy?' fear crept in. still, they passed bones of past warriors, some retreating as far as the first pile. finally, they reached a rise and saw it: a massive bear sleeping on a distant mountain.

distance was big, but that creature was huge, easy to spot. leader goes, "there he is, the one i brought you for. now the real test starts. he's a mishemokwa and a manito. he's got what we want, the wampum. warriors died for this. don't be scared. stay strong. we'll find him asleep." leader steps up, touches the belt on the animal's neck. "this is it," he says, "the wampum's inside." they ask the eldest to slip it off the sleeping bear. no luck. almost there with the second youngest. then the youngest leader goes, gets it done, and they bolt. run like hell. there's a system, pass it around when tired. get past the bones, look back, bear's up. roars like thunder, "who stole my wampum?" he starts after them. whole earth shakes with his jumps. he's catching up. leader asks, "anyone dream of a guardian spirit?" dead silence. "i did," he says, "saw a lodge, old man helped me. let's hope it happens now." they run, leader yells, sees a lodge. hope kicks in, they rush inside. leader begs the old man for help. "sit, eat," old man says. "who's manito? only me." checks outside, bear's coming. "yep, big manito," he says. "but you asked, so i'll protect you." tells them to run out the back when bear shows. pulls out a bag, two small black dogs. "my fighters," he says. pats one, it swells up, fills the lodge, teeth out. it jumps out, meets the bear. battle's wild. skies full of howls. second dog joins. brothers sneak out the back. hear dogs' death cries. leader says, "old man's doomed, let's move." they got energy from food, but the bear's back in sight. leader asks again, "any ideas?" silence. "i dreamed," he says, "another old man helped me. let's find his lodge." run on, spot it, rush in. plead for safety. old man feeds them, says, "eat. who's manito? none but me." earth shakes, bear's near. old man checks outside, "trouble's here." grabs medicine-sack, pulls out black stone war-clubs, tells them to run out back. clubs grow huge. he steps out as bear reaches the door. strikes, club breaks, bear stumbles. hits again, second club breaks, bear falls. each hit thunders, bear's howls echo across the sky.

they'd run a good distance before checking back. bear was shaking off the hits, moving his paws, standing up. the old man didn't make it either. you could hear his screams. bear was on them again, gaining. the young guys just kept running, but the leader knew they were out of options. "my dreams are running out, one more after this," he said, calling on his spirit. "dreamed of a big lake, a canoe ready with ten paddles. don't worry, it's close." and there it was. they jumped in, paddled to the middle. bear hit the lake's edge, tried swimming, circled around, then started drinking up the lake. water rushed their way. leader pushed them to paddle hard, but they were getting pulled back.

he told them to face it head-on. "now's your time, Mudjikewis. get to the bow, swing that club when we're close." Mudjikewis got ready. they aimed for the bear's mouth. just about to go in, Mudjikewis smashed the bear's head. bear buckled, spat out the water, launching them to shore. they bailed, ran till they couldn't. earth shook, bear still on their tail. spirits low, leader rallied them. "think, anything to save us?" silence again. "last shot," he said. "if this doesn't work, we're done." sprinted forward, calling his spirit, yelling. "almost there," he told them. "trust this spirit, don't freeze up. his lodge is near. run."

iamo was stuck just like we left him. his head was telling his sister how to get food with those magic arrows. one day she noticed his eyes brighten up. finally, he spoke. "sister," he said, "look at the mess youve put me in. soon some young guys will show up needing my help, but how can i help now? still, take two arrows, place them where you usually do, and have meat ready before they arrive. when they call my name, tell them, 'an accident happened long ago. it was my fault.' if they come closer, invite them in and feed them.

now, listen closely. when the bear comes, go out to meet him. take my medicine-sack, bows and arrows, and my head. open the sack, lay out my colors, eagle feathers, dried hair, and everything else. as the bear approaches, show him each item one by one, say, 'this is my brother's paint,' and so on, throwing each far away. these will weaken him. finally, throw my head, calling out, 'see, this is my brother's head.' he'll fall. by then, the young men will have eaten, and you can call them for help. cut the bear into pieces, scatter them to the winds, or he'll come back."

she promised to do everything he said. she barely got the meat ready when they heard the leader calling for iamo. she went out and did as her brother told her. the war party, being chased, came to the lodge. she invited them in and served the meat. while they ate, they heard the bear coming. she untied the sack and got the head ready. when the bear arrived, she did what iamo said. before she finished with the paints and feathers, the bear started to totter. when it got close, she threw the head far, shouting as instructed. blood poured as the head rolled. the bear fell with a huge noise. she called for help, and the young men rushed out, feeling stronger.

mudjikewis came up, yelled, and hit the bear's head until it was a mess. the others quickly cut it into tiny pieces and scattered them. soon they saw small black bears, just like today's, popping up and running everywhere. and thats how bears came to be.

they beat the pursuer and headed back to the lodge. the woman gathered up her tools and the head, packed them in the sack. the head didn't talk again, probably worn out from tackling the monster.

after all that running and covering so much ground, the young guys figured they wouldn't make it back home. game was plenty, so they decided to stick around. one day, they wandered off from the lodge to hunt, leaving the wampum with the woman. they did well, joking around like young guys do. one said, "hey, let's ask our sister if we can bring the head here. might like the company. let's take her some food." they asked, and she agreed. they brought the head to their hunting spot, tried to entertain it, but it only lit up now and then.

while busy at camp, they got ambushed by some unknown indians. the fight was rough, bloody, outnumbered thirty to one. they fought hard till they all went down. the attackers regrouped to count losses. one guy lagging behind stumbled on the head, still lively. stared at it, curious, then grabbed a feather and stuck it on his head.

heading out, the feather waved over him till he reached his group. tossed the head and sack down, shared the find. sack had paints and feathers. they mocked the head, some painted their faces, one grabbed the head by the hair, mocked it.

"look, you ugly thing, see your paints on warriors."

but those feathers were too beautiful, more of them wore them. they mistreated the head, and those using the feathers paid with their lives. chief told them to ditch everything but the head. "we'll see what we can do with it at home. try to make it shut its eyes."

back home, they hung it in the council-lodge, raw hide soaked and strapped, fire tightening it. "we'll see if we can shut its eyes now."

the sister waited for days for the young guys to bring back the head. finally fed up, she went looking. found them dead, wounded, bodies everywhere. no head, no sack. she wailed, blackened her face, wandered till she reached the spot where the head was taken. found the magic bow and arrows they left behind, clueless. figured she'd find her brother's head. found his paints and feathers, hung them on a tree branch for later.

by dusk, she hit the first lodge of a big village. used a charm to get in good with the old folks there. told them her story. old man said he'd help. head was at the council-fire, chiefs and young men guarding it. those chiefs are like manitoes. she just wanted to see it, knew she couldn't take it by force. old man said, 'come with me.' they sat near the lodge door. warriors inside, playing games, smoking the head like dry meat. head moved, warriors joked it was the smoke. sister looked at her brother's eyes, saw tears rolling. chief laughed, noticed her, asked who she was. the man with her covered, saying she was family. a boastful young man claimed he knew her well, which helped her slip away unnoticed.

she went back to the man's lodge, then headed home. found her adopted brothers' bodies, laid them east. threw an ax up, warned them to get up or it'd hit them. did it three times, and third time, they stood up.

Mudjikewis wakes up, rubbing his eyes. "Why'd I oversleep?" he asks. "Oh, we all died," someone else says. "Our sister brought us back." They burn their enemies' bodies. Sister goes off to find them wives and comes back with ten women for the ten men, starting with the oldest. Mudjikewis paces, worried he won't get the one he wants. But he does—she's a magician. All move into a big lodge. Sister says the women need to take turns freeing their brother's head each night. They agree. The oldest tries first, flies through the air back by dawn, unties just one knot. Each woman does the same—one knot per night. The youngest goes, starts right away. Smoke fills the lodge, drives them out. She carries off the head.

They hear her coming, saying to prep their brother's body. They go to where Iamo's blackened body lies. Sister cuts the neck, it bleeds. They rub the body, apply medicine, get rid of the blackness. The one with the head cuts it too, makes it bleed. They place the head on the body, use medicine, bring Iamo back to life, looking good as ever. Everyone's happy. Iamo divides the wampum, starting with the eldest. Youngest gets the best part of the belt.

Told they're spirits now, not mortal, since they died and returned. Given stations in the invisible world. Only Mudjikewis's place named—he'll direct the west wind, Kebeyun. They're to do good on earth, give freely, hold the wampum sacred. Light grains mean peace; dark ones, war.

Off they go to their new homes, singing and shouting. Iamo and his sister Iamoqua head down below.
